Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Special Cases in Shuf'ah: Dhimmi Buyer with Wine/Pig and Buyer's Improvements
Rulings on dhimmi transactions involving wine/pig and buyer's improvements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings when a dhimmi buys property with wine or pig, and when the buyer has made improvements, including the option to force removal.

Core Idea
A dhimmi pre-emptor pays wine like for wine and pig's value; a Muslim pays cash value for both; improvements are valued as uprooted or removed at buyer's cost.

Pre-emption becomes complex when the original sale involves prohibited items like wine or pigs, or when the buyer invests in the property. The Hanafi school provides clear rules: if both parties are dhimmis, the pre-emptor pays in kind for permissible items and cash for prohibited ones; if Muslim, cash only. For improvements, the pre-emptor can pay their salvage value or force removal, with a later view from Abu Yusuf giving him an option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties' religion
Determine if the buyer and pre-emptor are dhimmis or Muslims. This changes how the price is paid.
2
Rule for dhimmi pre-emptor
If the pre-emptor is a dhimmi, he pays: for wine, the like (equivalent amount of wine); for pig, the market value (since pig is not mithli).
Wine is considered mithli among dhimmis because it is lawful for them to transact.
3
Rule for Muslim pre-emptor
If the pre-emptor is Muslim, he pays the cash value of both wine and pig, because Muslims cannot possess or transact in these items.
They are treated as non-mithli due to impossibility of delivery.
4
Handle buyer's improvements
If the buyer has built or planted on the property, the pre-emptor has two options: pay the value of improvements as if they were uprooted (salvage value) and take the property, or force the buyer to remove them at his own expense.
This mirrors the rule in usurpation (ghasb).
5
Note Abu Yusuf's later view
Abu Yusuf later retracted the option to force removal; instead, the pre-emptor chooses between taking with compensation or leaving the improvements (i.e., the buyer keeps them).
This provides more flexibility for the pre-emptor.

Source Text

(قوله: بطلت شفعته... الخ؛ وقد روى ابن أبي مالك عن أبي يوسف له إنه كان يقول أولاً كقولهما، وهو ما ذكر في الكتاب، ثم رجع وقال : له أن يأخذها عند حلول الأجل، وإن لم يطلب في الحال؛ لأن الطلب إنما هو بلا أخذ. وهو في الحال لا يتمكن منه على الوجه الذي يطلبه؛ لأنه إنما يريد الأخذ بعد حلول الأجل أو ثمن مؤجل في الحال لا يتمكن من ذلك، فلا فائدة في طلبه في الحال؛ لسكوته لعدم الفائدة في الطلب لا لإعراضه عن الأخذ. قاله جلبي. And with the buyer's receipt (of the price), and he takes in the reduction of the whole for the whole. (And if the buyer claims a price, and his seller less than it without receipt, then the statement is his): i.e., without receipt of the price, then the statement is the seller's. (And with the buyer's receipt): i.e., with receipt of the price, then the statement is the buyer's. (And he takes in the reduction of the whole for the whole): the issue of partial reduction has already passed in the chapter of Murabahah by his statement: the pre-emptor takes the lesser in the two cases. (And in the purchase with a similar price by its like, and in other than it by value, and in real estate for real estate each is taken by the value of the other): and regarding a deferred price, (he takes) immediately or he demands immediately and takes after the term. This is our (Hanafi) position. As for Zufar and al-Shafi'i, in his old opinion, he may take it immediately with the deferred price. (And if he is silent about it, it becomes void): i.e., if he is silent about the demand. And he waits until he demands at the term, his pre-emption becomes void. And in the purchase by a dhimmi with wine or pig, and the pre-emptor (is) a dhimmi: with the like of wine and value of the pig; and the Muslim pre-emptor: with the value of each. And regarding the buyer's building and planting: with the price and value of the building and planting as uprooted, as in usurpation; and if he wishes, the buyer is tasked with uprooting them: i.e., the pre-emptor takes in the case where the buyer built or planted with the price and their value as uprooted, or the buyer is tasked with removing the building or planting. And the meaning of their value as uprooted is their value as deserving of removal, as has passed in the chapter of usurpation. And from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him): that he is not tasked with removal, but rather he chooses between taking. (His statement: his pre-emption becomes void... etc.; and Ibn Abi Malik related from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) that he initially used to say like their (the Sahibayn) statement, which is what is mentioned in the book, then he retracted and said: he may take it at the time of the term, even if he did not demand immediately, because demand is only without taking. And he cannot achieve it immediately in the manner he desires, because he only intends to take after the term or a deferred price; immediately he cannot achieve that, so there is no benefit in demanding immediately; his silence is due to lack of benefit in demand, not due to abandoning the taking. Jabiri said it. (His statement: and the pre-emptor is a dhimmi; he said in al-'Inayah: this is a precaution against the case of an apostate, for he has no pre-emption, whether he is killed for apostasy, dies, or joins the war zone, and he has no heirs, because it (pre-emption) is not inherited. Jabiri said it. (His statement: with the value of each... etc.; as for the pig, it is clear, for it is from the category of valuables, and likewise wine, due to the impossibility of delivery and receipt for a Muslim, so it is annexed to non-mithli items.

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on how the pre-emptor (shafi') takes the property (mashfu') in various scenarios. The text begins by establishing a key principle: when there is a dispute between the buyer and seller regarding the actual price paid, and the pre-emptor wants to take the property, the statement of the one who has not yet received the price is trusted. Thus, if the seller has not yet taken the price, his word is taken over the buyer's; if the seller has already received the price, the buyer's word is taken. This is because the pre-emptor is essentially stepping into the buyer's shoes, and the actual price determines his liability. Next, the text addresses the case where the seller reduces (hatt) the entire price for the buyer. In such a case, the pre-emptor takes the property at the full original price, not the reduced amount, because the reduction is considered a separate transaction that does not affect the original sale. Then, the text discusses transactions where the price is a similar item (mithli), such as wheat for wheat, where the pre-emptor pays in kind; if the price is non-mithli (qimiyy), he pays its cash value. If two properties are exchanged, each pre-emptor pays the value of the other property. For a deferred price (thaman mu'ajjal), the pre-emptor has the option to either take the property immediately by paying the full deferred price in cash, or demand the pre-emption immediately but postpone payment until the term. If he remains silent without demanding, his right to pre-emption becomes void. This is the Hanafi position; Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i (in his old view) hold that the pre-emptor can take immediately without demanding, paying the deferred price at its present value. Then, the text turns to cases involving a dhimmi buyer. If a dhimmi buys property in exchange for wine or pigs, the pre-emptor, if also a dhimmi, takes the property by paying the like of wine (i.e., an equivalent amount of wine) and the value of the pig (since pigs are not quantified by like); if the pre-emptor is a Muslim, he must pay the cash value for both because Muslims cannot own or transact in wine or pigs. Finally, the text addresses improvements made by the buyer, such as buildings or plants. The pre-emptor may either pay for these improvements at their value as uprooted (i.e., after removal) and take the property, or force the buyer to remove them at his own expense. This is similar to the rules in usurpation (ghasb). A later narration from Abu Yusuf indicates that he retracted his earlier view and held that the buyer should not be forced to remove improvements; instead, the pre-emptor chooses between taking with compensation or leaving the improvements. Additionally, the commentary notes that a non-Muslim pre-emptor cannot be an apostate, as an apostate has no right of pre-emption, nor can he inherit it. The text also clarifies why wine is treated like pig: for a Muslim, delivery and receipt of wine are impossible, so it falls under the category of valuables (qimiyyat) rather than similar (mithliyat).

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with guaranteed rights and obligations.
مقلوعين (maqlu'ayn): Uprooted or removed; referring to the value of improvements after removal (salvage value).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a Muslim pre-emptor pay if the property was bought with wine?
A) An equivalent amount of wine
B) The cash value of the wine
C) Nothing, because the sale is invalid
D) A combination of wine and cash

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi pre-emptor pays in kind for wine and value for pig; a Muslim pays cash for both; improvements are valued as salvage, and the pre-emptor may force removal (or not, per Abu Yusuf).

Shuf'ah: Taking Property When Buildings or Trees Are Destroyed
Explains pre-emptor's rights after natural destruction or buyer's demolition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pre-emptor's rights and obligations when a building or trees are destroyed naturally or intentionally demolished by the buyer, and identify the Hanafi majority position and the disagreement from Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Natural destruction does not reduce the pre-emption price; buyer's demolition reduces it to land's share only.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses a common practical scenario in pre-emption: what happens when the property changes between the sale and the pre-emptor's claim? The ruling hinges on whether the change is due to a natural calamity or the buyer's deliberate act, and whether the pre-emptor can recover lost value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Separate natural destruction (heavenly calamity) from buyer's deliberate demolition.
The ruling differs fundamentally based on the cause.
2
Determine the pre-emptor's right for natural destruction
The pre-emptor takes the land for the full price; the building/trees are followers whose loss does not reduce the price.
Price is for the land, not the attribute.
3
Determine the pre-emptor's right for buyer's demolition
The pre-emptor takes only the vacant land for its share of the price, not the demolished materials.
The materials are no longer real estate and no longer followers.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that the pre-emptor can recover the building's value from the seller; the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) disagrees.
The majority view is mufta bihi.
5
Understand the reasoning for the distinction
The buyer recovers from the seller because he was authorized; the pre-emptor takes by force, so no recovery.
Key difference: authorization vs. compulsion.

Source Text

And with the full price if it (the property) is ruined, or the trees dry up, and he takes the vacant land (al-arsah) without the demolished materials for its share of the price if the buyer demolished the building. And he does not recover the value of the building or the planting from anyone, unlike the buyer, for he recovers the value of the building or the planting from the seller, because he is authorized by him, unlike the pre-emptor, for he takes by force. And with the full price if it is ruined, or the trees dry up: meaning he bought a house and it was ruined, or an orchard and its trees dried up, so the pre-emptor, if he wants to take by pre-emption, he takes for the full price. (And he takes the vacant land) not the demolished materials for its share if the buyer demolished the building: he only takes for the share because the buyer intended damage, whereas in the first case it was a heavenly calamity, and he does not take the demolished materials because it is not real estate and no longer remains as a follower. And from Abu Yusuf: he returns to his seller for the value of the building because he (the seller) is considered to have taken possession, so they are treated as seller and buyer. [1] His saying: unlike the buyer, is an indication of the answer to Abu Yusuf's fatwa of returning the value in both, based on the fact that the one who takes it from him becomes like a deceived buyer from the seller’s side. Al-Jalabi said. [2] His saying: and with the full price... etc.: means if the house collapsed or its building burned or the trees of the orchard dried up without the action of anyone, the pre-emptor has the choice: if he takes it, he takes it for the full price because the building and planting are followers, such that they entered the sale without mention, so nothing of the price is allocated to them as long as they are not intended; because the existence of the building on the land is like an attribute. And the loss of the attribute does not reduce anything of the price if it is a heavenly calamity, because the price is compensation for the principal not the attribute, and if he wishes he may abandon it because the pre-emptor may refrain from owning the house for his money. This is the summary of what is in al-Hidayah, so you must reflect. (3) His saying: and taking the vacant land... etc.: means if the buyer demolished the building, it is said to the pre-emptor: if you wish, take the vacant land for its share of the price, and if you wish, leave it, because it became intended by destruction. (1) From Dhakhirat al-Uqba. (2) Al-Hidayah (4:33). Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. And in buying land with fruit of palm trees therein, or no fruit on it and it fruits with him, he takes it with its fruit and its share of the price if the buyer harvested it in the first case, and with the full in the second. (And in buying land with fruit of palm trees therein, or no fruit on it and it fruits with him: he takes it with its fruit and its share of the price if the buyer harvested it in the first case, and with the full in the second). He bought land and mentioned palm fruit in the sale, since it does not enter without mention, or he bought and there was no fruit on the trees, then it fruited in the buyer’s hand, so the pre-emptor takes the land with the fruit in both cases, and if the buyer harvested it, the pre-emptor takes the land without the palm fruit, but in the first case something of the price corresponds to it, so the price is divided on the value of the land and the building on the day of purchase, and he takes the land for its share of the price. Unlike the first scenario because the ownership is by heavenly calamity, and the pre-emptor cannot take the demolished materials because it became separated and no longer a follower. Thus in al-Hidayah (1). [1] His saying: he takes it... etc.: this is istihsan, and by qiyas he does not take it because it is not a sale; do you not see that it does not enter the sale without mention, so it resembles goods in the house? The face of istihsan is that by virtue of connection it becomes a follower of the real estate like the building in the house, and whatever is fixed in it, the pre-emptor takes it. Thus in al-Hidayah. (2) His saying: harvested it... etc.: know that 'jadd' originally means cutting, from which 'jadd al-nakhl' means 'he cut its fruit as a harvest', so he is a 'hâd'. Thus in al-Mughrib, and from it the letter dâl is understood from the form of writing, and it is said with the letter dhâl (mu'jama) specifically for harvested dates and harvested crops. [3] His saying: in the two cases: i.e. the case where there was fruit on the palm trees when the sale occurred and then the buyer harvested it, and the case where there was no fruit on the palm trees when the sale of the land with palm trees occurred, then the palm trees fruited, then the buyer harvested it, then the pre-emptor came. Thus in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah (by a later commentator) deals with the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) when the property has undergone changes between the sale and the pre-emptor's claim. The passage is structured around several rulings, each explained with legal reasoning. 

First: If a house collapses or an orchard's trees dry up due to natural causes (a heavenly calamity), the pre-emptor may take the land for the full price he would have paid. The building and trees are considered 'followers' (tābi') attached to the land; they were included in the sale without a separate price. Their loss does not reduce the price because the price is for the land itself, not for the attributes that have vanished through no fault of the buyer. The pre-emptor also has the option to decline. 

Second: If the buyer himself demolishes the building, the pre-emptor may take only the vacant land (al-arsah) for its proportional share of the price. He cannot take the demolished materials (al-naqd) because they are no longer real estate and are not attached as followers. The buyer's intentional destruction renders the building an independent object, no longer a follower. The land's share of the price is determined by apportioning the total price according to the value of land and building at the time of purchase. The pre-emptor cannot recover the value of the building from anyone—neither from the seller nor from the buyer—because he takes by force (jabr), not by consent. 

Exception: If the pre-emptor takes from the buyer after the buyer has already taken possession, the buyer himself can recover the value of the building or planting from the seller, because the seller authorized the buyer (by selling) unlike the pre-emptor who takes by force. This distinction is made explicitly. 

Abu Yusuf has a differing view: he holds that the pre-emptor should recover the value of the building from the seller, treating the situation as if the seller had wrongfully taken possession and then sold to the buyer. The commentator then rebuts this by noting that the pre-emptor is not like a deceived buyer; he takes by force, not by consent. 

Third: When the property includes fruit-bearing palm trees, two sub-cases arise: (a) if fruit existed at the time of sale and was mentioned (since it does not enter the sale automatically), the pre-emptor takes the land along with the fruit if the buyer has not yet harvested it. If the buyer harvests it, the pre-emptor takes the land only, but deducts a share of the price corresponding to the fruit's value. (b) If no fruit existed at sale but later appears during the buyer's possession, the pre-emptor takes the land with the fruit regardless of harvest (because the fruit is linked to the land), unless the buyer harvested it, in which case the pre-emptor takes only the land, again with a price adjustment. The commentary notes that this ruling is by istihsan (juristic preference); by strict analogy (qiyas), the fruit would not be taken because it is not part of the sale and resembles movable goods. But istihsan gives more weight to the connection between fruit and land. 

Throughout, the author relies on al-Hidayah as the primary source, and the commentary serves to clarify and expand upon the rulings.

Key Terms
العرصة (al-‘arṣah): The vacant land after removal of the building; the land without the structure.
النقض (al-naqḍ): The demolished materials (rubble, bricks, etc.) from the building that the buyer tore down.
تبع (tābi‘): That which is attached and considered part of the principal; here, the building and trees are followers of the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a buyer demolishes the building after purchase, what does the pre-emptor take?
A) The land with the demolished materials for the full price
B) The land only, for its share of the price, without the demolished materials
C) The land only for the full price
D) The demolished materials for their value

Key Takeaway
Natural destruction does not reduce the pre-emption price; buyer's intentional demolition reduces it to the land's share only.

Shuf'ah: Taking Land with Fruit-Bearing Trees
Extends shuf'ah rulings to fruit-bearing trees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pre-emptor's entitlement to fruit on palm trees when the land is sold, including the distinction between fruit that existed at sale and fruit that appears later, and explain the role of istihsan in this ruling.

Core Idea
Fruit attached to land is a follower; the pre-emptor takes it unless harvested.

This part of the passage extends the follower principle to fruit on trees. The ruling applies istihsan over strict qiyas, treating the fruit as attached to the land. Two subcases are distinguished based on whether fruit existed at sale and whether it was harvested. Understanding this helps the pre-emptor know what they are entitled to and how to adjust the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios for fruit
First: fruit existed at the time of sale and was mentioned. Second: no fruit at sale, but fruit appeared later during the buyer's possession.
If fruit existed but not mentioned, it does not enter the sale, so it is not considered.
2
Determine entitlement when fruit is not harvested
If the buyer has not harvested, the pre-emptor takes the land and the fruit together, paying the full price.
The fruit is treated as a follower like a building.
3
Determine entitlement when fruit is harvested
If the buyer harvested the fruit, the pre-emptor takes only the land, and the price is adjusted proportionally based on the fruit's value.
The fruit is no longer attached, so it is no longer a follower.
4
Understand the price adjustment
The total purchase price is divided between the land and the fruit according to their values at the time of purchase.
The pre-emptor pays only the land's share.
5
Recognize the use of istihsan
Strict qiyas would not include fruit in pre-emption because it is not part of the sale. Istihsan gives weight to the physical attachment.
This reflects Hanafi preference for practical connection over theoretical separation.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah (by a later commentator) deals with the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) when the property has undergone changes between the sale and the pre-emptor's claim. The passage is structured around several rulings, each explained with legal reasoning. 

First: If a house collapses or an orchard's trees dry up due to natural causes (a heavenly calamity), the pre-emptor may take the land for the full price he would have paid. The building and trees are considered 'followers' (tābi') attached to the land; they were included in the sale without a separate price. Their loss does not reduce the price because the price is for the land itself, not for the attributes that have vanished through no fault of the buyer. The pre-emptor also has the option to decline. 

Second: If the buyer himself demolishes the building, the pre-emptor may take only the vacant land (al-arsah) for its proportional share of the price. He cannot take the demolished materials (al-naqd) because they are no longer real estate and are not attached as followers. The buyer's intentional destruction renders the building an independent object, no longer a follower. The land's share of the price is determined by apportioning the total price according to the value of land and building at the time of purchase. The pre-emptor cannot recover the value of the building from anyone—neither from the seller nor from the buyer—because he takes by force (jabr), not by consent. 

Exception: If the pre-emptor takes from the buyer after the buyer has already taken possession, the buyer himself can recover the value of the building or planting from the seller, because the seller authorized the buyer (by selling) unlike the pre-emptor who takes by force. This distinction is made explicitly. 

Abu Yusuf has a differing view: he holds that the pre-emptor should recover the value of the building from the seller, treating the situation as if the seller had wrongfully taken possession and then sold to the buyer. The commentator then rebuts this by noting that the pre-emptor is not like a deceived buyer; he takes by force, not by consent. 

Third: When the property includes fruit-bearing palm trees, two sub-cases arise: (a) if fruit existed at the time of sale and was mentioned (since it does not enter the sale automatically), the pre-emptor takes the land along with the fruit if the buyer has not yet harvested it. If the buyer harvests it, the pre-emptor takes the land only, but deducts a share of the price corresponding to the fruit's value. (b) If no fruit existed at sale but later appears during the buyer's possession, the pre-emptor takes the land with the fruit regardless of harvest (because the fruit is linked to the land), unless the buyer harvested it, in which case the pre-emptor takes only the land, again with a price adjustment. The commentary notes that this ruling is by istihsan (juristic preference); by strict analogy (qiyas), the fruit would not be taken because it is not part of the sale and resembles movable goods. But istihsan gives more weight to the connection between fruit and land. 

Throughout, the author relies on al-Hidayah as the primary source, and the commentary serves to clarify and expand upon the rulings.

Key Terms
ثمر (thamar): Fruit, specifically dates on palm trees in this context.
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference that overrides strict analogy due to practical consideration.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical deduction; here, strict qiyas would exclude fruit from pre-emption.

Key Takeaway
Fruit attached to land is treated as a follower by istihsan; if harvested, it is no longer attached and the price is adjusted.

Shuf'a on Land with Date Palm Fruit: Ruling and Istihsan Basis
Ruling on shuf'a with date palm fruit and istihsan justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shuf'a when land with date palm fruit is sold, and explain the istihsan basis that overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
Fruit attached to land follows it in shuf'a; if cut, it becomes separate and the pre-emptor takes land alone with a proportionate price.

This lesson examines a specific ruling on the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) in the Hanafi school, focusing on land containing date palm fruit. The passage distinguishes two scenarios—fruit present at sale versus fruit appearing later—and explains how the buyer's cutting (jadd) affects the pre-emptor's entitlement. More importantly, it reveals the school's use of istihsan (juristic preference) to override strict analogy (qiyas), demonstrating a key feature of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the two cases: (1) land bought with fruit already on the date palms, and (2) land bought without fruit, which then appears and is cut by the buyer.
In both, the fruit is mentioned explicitly in the sale.
2
Understand the ruling when fruit is uncut vs cut
If the buyer has not cut the fruit, the pre-emptor takes the land together with the fruit. If the buyer cuts it, the pre-emptor takes only the land, paying a proportionate share of the price in the first scenario and the full price in the second.
The key difference is that cut fruit becomes separate property (muufaq) and no longer follows the land.
3
Recognize the qiyas (analogy) that would deny the fruit
By strict analogy, the fruit would not be included in shuf'a because it is not part of the sale unless explicitly mentioned—like goods in a house. This would mean the pre-emptor never takes the fruit.
This analogy is overridden by istihsan.
4
Grasp the istihsan reasoning
The commentary explains that because the fruit is physically attached to the land, it becomes a follow-up (taba') to the real estate, similar to a building. Therefore, even if not mentioned, it is included in shuf'a by virtue of its connection.
This istihsan is based on the idea that what is attached is treated as part of the principal for pre-emption.

يأخذ بحصة الأرض من الثَّمن ، وفي الفصل الثاني يأخذُ بكلِّ الثّمن ؛ لأنَّ الثَّمَرَ لم يكن موجوداً وقت العقدِ فلا يقابله شيءٌ منَ الثّمن.
[١] قوله : يأخذ بحصة الأرض ... الخ ؛ قيل : طريق معرفة الحصة أن تقوم الأرض والنخل وحدهما، ويقوم الثمرُ ،وحده، ثمّ يضم ويقسم الثمنُ عليهما، فما أصاب الثمار سقط من الشفيع، وما أصاب الأرض والنخل أخذ به الشفيع. قاله

(۱) في ذخيرة العقبى» (ص ٥٦٢).

And regarding buying land with fruit of date palms in it, or without fruit upon it then it fruits with it: he takes it with its fruit and with its share of the price if the buyer cuts it in the first case, and with the whole in the second. (And regarding buying land with fruit of date palms in it, or without fruit upon it then it fruits with it: he takes it with its fruit and with its share of the price if the buyer cuts it (3) in the first, and with the whole in the second). He bought land and mentioned the fruit of date palms in the sale, for it does not enter without mention, or he bought and there was no fruit on the trees, then it fruited in the hand of the buyer, so the pre-emptor takes the land with the fruit in both cases. And if the buyer cuts it, then the pre-emptor takes the land without the fruit of date palms, but in the first case something of the price corresponds to it, so the price is divided over the value of the land and the building on the day the purchase occurred, so he takes the land with its share of the price. Contrary to the first case, because the ownership is by a heavenly affliction, and the pre-emptor cannot take the detached fruit because it has become separate, so it no longer remains a follow-up. Thus in al-Hidayah (1). [1] His saying: He takes it etc.: This that he mentioned is istihsan, and by analogy he does not take it, because it is not a sale — do you not see that it does not enter the sale without mention, so it resembles goods in a house. The basis of istihsan: Because by virtue of connection it became a follow-up to the real estate, like the building in the house, and whatever is attached in it, the pre-emptor takes it. Thus in al-Hidayah. (2) His saying: He cuts it etc.: Know that 'jadd' originally means cutting, and from it: jadd an-nakhl: he cut it, i.e., he cut its fruit as a harvest, so he is jadd. Thus in al-Mughrib. It is understood from it the unpointed dal according to writing, and it is said with the pointed dhal is specific to harvested dates and reaped crops. [3] His saying: In both cases: i.e., in the case when there was fruit in the date palms at the time of purchase and then the buyer cut it, and in the case when there was no fruit on the date palms at the time of purchase on the land with date palms, then the date palms fruited, then the buyer cut it, then the pre-emptor came. Thus in an-Nihayah. (1) Al-Hidayah Sharh Bidayat al-Mubtadi (4:34). (2) Al-Hidayah (4:34). (3) Al-Mughrib (p. 77). Book of Pre-emption: Its definition, types and rulings. He takes with the share of the land from the price, and in the second case he takes with the entire price, because the fruit did not exist at the time of the contract, so nothing of the price corresponds to it. [1] His saying: He takes with the share of the land etc.: It is said: The method of knowing the share is that the land and the date palms alone are valued, and the fruit alone is valued, then they are combined and the price is divided over them. What falls to the fruit is dropped from the pre-emptor, and what falls to the land and date palms, the pre-emptor takes it with it. Al-Jalabi said it. (1) In Dhakirat al-'Uqba (p. 562). Wa ji [?].
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn from the Hanafi school regarding the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) when land containing date palms is purchased. The ruling is given in two scenarios: (1) when fruit exists on the trees at the time of sale and the buyer later cuts it (jadd), and (2) when there is no fruit at the time of sale but fruit appears afterward and the buyer cuts it. In both cases, if the buyer has not cut the fruit, the pre-emptor (shafi') takes the land together with the fruit. If the buyer has cut the fruit, then in the first scenario the pre-emptor takes only the land, paying its proportionate share of the price, because the fruit had a portion of the price allocated to it. In the second scenario, since the fruit did not exist at the time of contract and no price was paid for it, the pre-emptor takes the land for the entire price. The commentary explains that this ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference), not qiyas (analogy). By qiyas, the fruit would not be taken because it is not part of the sale unless mentioned; it would be like separate goods in a house. But istihsan considers that the fruit, by virtue of its physical connection to the land, becomes a follow-up (taba') to the real estate, similar to a building. The term 'jadd' is explained lexically as cutting, specifically of dates. The method for calculating the proportionate share is also given: value the land and trees separately from the fruit, then divide the price proportionally. This ruling is confirmed from al-Hidayah and other authoritative Hanafi texts.

Key Terms
الشفيع (ash-shafi'): The pre-emptor who has the right to acquire the property sold to a third party by virtue of proximity or partnership.
جد (jadd): To cut or harvest, specifically the fruit of date palms.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: setting aside strict analogy for a more equitable ruling based on need or custom.
تبع (taba'): A follow-up; something that is attached to and follows the legal status of the principal item (e.g., fruit attached to land).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario (fruit existed at time of sale and buyer cut it), what does the pre-emptor take and what does he pay?
A) Takes land with fruit, pays full price
B) Takes land only, pays proportionate share of price
C) Takes land with fruit, pays proportionate share
D) Takes nothing if fruit is cut

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi shuf'a, attached fruit follows the land by istihsan; buyer's cutting separates it, reducing the pre-emptor's entitlement to land only with a proportionate price reduction.

Shuf'ah: Excluding Fruits from Pre-emption Price
Ruling with reasoning and calculation method for shuf'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the pre-emptor does not pay for fruits and apply the proportional calculation method for land and trees.

Core Idea
Fruits not existing at contract are not compensated by the pre-emptor.

When a property with fruit-bearing trees is sold, the pre-emptor (shafi') has the right to take the sold share. But does he also pay for the fruits? The Hanafi school rules that he only pays for the land and trees, not the fruits. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the precise arithmetic to calculate the pre-emptor's payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its rationale
The passage states: the pre-emptor takes the share of the land from the price. The reason given is that fruits did not exist at the time of the contract, so no part of the price corresponds to them. Grasp that the ruling is based on the non-existence of fruits as part of the sale.
The 'existence' refers to the fruit being present as an object of sale; if fruits were already harvested or sold separately, the ruling may differ.
2
Understand the calculation method
Al-Jalabi explains: value the land and palm trees separately, value the fruits separately, then combine both values and divide the total sale price proportionally. The portion allocated to fruits is subtracted from what the pre-emptor pays. This ensures the pre-emptor only buys the land and trees.
The method uses current market valuation, not the contract price allocation, to determine the correct share.
3
Apply the calculation with a hypothetical example
Suppose a property (land + palm trees) plus fruits is sold for $1000. Value of land+trees = $800, fruits = $200. Total value = $1000. The pre-emptor pays only for land+trees: ($800/$1000)*$1000 = $800. The fruits' share ($200) is not paid by the pre-emptor.
If the sale price is not equal to total current value, adjust proportionally using the same ratio.

This passage presents a ruling on shuf'ah concerning property with land and fruit-bearing trees. The reason: the fruits were not present at the contract, so no portion of the price is allocated to them. The commentary then explains the calculation: value land/palm trees and fruit separately, add both, divide total price proportionally. The amount for fruit is not paid by the pre-emptor. This ensures the pre-emptor does not pay for something not part of the original transaction. The ruling is attributed to al-Jalabi.

Definition and Types of Shuf'ah and Its Conditions
Defines shuf'ah, its types, and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of shuf'ah, distinguish its two types, and identify the conditions for its application.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is the right to take over a sold share of real estate; appurtenances are included unintentionally.

This passage introduces the chapter on shuf'ah (pre-emption), explaining its scope and conditions. It clarifies that shuf'ah applies intentionally only to real estate owned for a price that is wealth, and unintentionally to things attached to that real estate. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the right of pre-emption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The passage states that shuf'ah is established intentionally in real estate owned for a price that is wealth. This is the basic ruling.
2
Distinguish the two types of shuf'ah
Shuf'ah is divided into qasdiyyah (intentional) and ghayr qasdiyyah (unintentional). Intentional applies directly to real estate; unintentional applies to appurtenances like trees or mills attached to the real estate.
The shuf'ah in the appurtenance follows the main property.
3
List the conditions for shuf'ah
Three conditions: (1) property must be real estate (ʿaqār), (2) ownership must be acquired in exchange for a price that is māl (wealth), (3) the shuf'ah must be exercised intentionally for the real estate.
If the property is obtained through khulʿ (which is not māl), shuf'ah does not apply.
4
Understand the rationale
Shuf'ah is to prevent harm that would befall the co-owner from the expense of division (e.g., surveyor fees).
This rationale helps explain why shuf'ah is limited to real estate where division is costly.

And unintentional shuf'ah, which occurs through real estate, such as in trees, and trees are subsidiary to real estate: meaning the mill house with the mill, so shuf'ah is intentional in the house and unintentional in the mill. Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on shuf'ah, clarifying when it applies and what invalidates it. The core matn states that shuf'ah is established intentionally (qasdan) only in real estate (ʿaqār) owned in exchange for wealth (māl). The commentary first refines the chapter heading, explaining the correct wording is 'what it is in and what it is not in' to avoid confusion. Then it divides shuf'ah into two types: 1) Intentional shuf'ah (shuf‘ah qasdiyyah) which applies directly to real estate, and 2) Unintentional shuf'ah (shuf‘ah ghayr qasdiyyah) which applies to items subsidiary to real estate, like trees attached to land or a mill attached to a mill house. In such cases, the pre-emption is intentional for the main property but unintentional for the appurtenance. The text also defines al-ʿaqār as any property with a foundation—houses, estates, or portions thereof in cities or villages. Finally, it notes that the price must be wealth (māl); for instance, if property is obtained through khulʿ (which is not māl), shuf'ah does not apply. The rationale is that shuf'ah prevents harm from division expenses. This establishes the foundational limits of the right of pre-emption.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption for a co-owner to purchase a share sold to an outsider
الشفعة القصدية (al-shuf'ah al-qasdiyyah): Intentional pre-emption that directly applies to real estate without intermediary
الشفعة غير القصدية (al-shuf'ah ghayr al-qasdiyyah): Unintentional pre-emption that applies to items subsidiary to real estate (e.g., trees, mill)
العقار (al-ʿaqār): Real estate; everything with a foundation such as a house, landed estate, or quarter

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah applies intentionally to real estate owned for a price that is wealth, and unintentionally to its appurtenances.

The Scope of Shuf'ah: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on shuf'ah scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on shuf'ah in indivisible property and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the underlying wisdom.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah prevents neighbor harm, not division costs — so it applies to indivisible property.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) protects co-owners and neighbors from unwanted partners. But does it apply when the property cannot be divided, like a shared mill or bathhouse? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools disagree. This lesson walks through the dispute, the proofs, and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school hold regarding shuf'ah in indivisible real estate? Identify the core ruling.
Focus on the phrase 'إنما تجب قصداً في عقار' and the expansion to indivisible properties like حمام and رحى.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school disagrees and on what grounds? What is their exclusive cause for shuf'ah?
The opposing school is Imam al-Shafi'i and his followers. Their illa is mu'nat al-qismah.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is the Shafi'i dalil? How do they justify restricting shuf'ah to divisible property?
They argue that shuf'ah was legislated to avoid division costs, so it cannot apply where division is impossible.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How do the Hanafis rebut the Shafi'i argument? What is their evidence (naqli and aqli)?
They cite the general hadith 'الشفعة في كل شيء' and the broader hikmah of daf' darr al-jiwar.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings? In which real-life scenarios do the schools diverge?
Example: a shared bathhouse – Hanafis grant shuf'ah, Shafi'is deny it.

وتوضيح
المسألة الشفعة واجبة ؛ أي ثابتة في العقار وإن كان مما لا يقسم كالحمام
سنك آسيا والبئر والطريق، وقال الشافعي : لا شفعة فيما لا يحتمل والرحى : يعني القسمة ؛ لأنّ الشفعة إنّما وجبت دفعاً لمؤنة القسمة، وهذا لا يتحقق فيما لا يقسم ، فإنّه لو قُسِمَ قـ م قسمة لا ينتفع به كالحمام والرحي. ولنا : قوله : «الشفعة في كلّ شيء»(۳) ؛ عقاراً أو ريعاً أو غير ذلك من العمومات ؛ ولأنّ الشفعة سبب الاتصال في الملك، والحكمة دفع ضرر الجوار، وهذا ينتظم ما يقسم وما لا يقسم. [1] قوله : كرحى وحمام.... الخ ؛ ذكر في «البحر » : ويدخل في الحمام ما كان مركبا في بنيانه دون المنفصل ؛ كالقصعة، ويدخل في الرحى الحجر الأسفل دون الأعلى؛ لأنه مبني في الأرض. [٢] قوله : لا في عرض ؛ وهو على وزن الفلس: المتاع، وكل شيء فهوا عرض
Chapter on what shuf'ah applies to or not, and what nullifies it. [Section on distinguishing where shuf'ah is obligatory and where it is not.] It is obligatory primarily in real estate owned by a consideration that is wealth. Explanation of the chapter title. The author says: The meaning of the chapter is what shuf'ah applies to or not. The detailed exposition of where shuf'ah is obligatory and where it is not after mentioning the obligation in summary. Know that shuf'ah is of two types: primary shuf'ah specific to real estate without mediation, and secondary shuf'ah that is through real estate, as in trees and fruits following real estate. Real estate is everything that has a foundation like a house or estate. Then the commentator said: The consideration for the building must be wealth. Shuf'ah is to repel the harm of the cost of division according to al-Shafi'i, while according to us it is to repel the harm of adjacency. Even if it is not divisible, like a mill, bathhouse, well, not in movable property. The primary shuf'ah is specific to real estate, unlike secondary which is established in non-real estate. The real estate must be owned by consideration, so if owned by gift, shuf'ah is not established. The consideration must be wealth, so if a wife gives her house in khul', shuf'ah is not established. Shafi'i holds that shuf'ah is not established in what cannot be divided because shuf'ah is to avoid the cost of division, while we hold it is to repel harm of adjacency. Not in movable property. Clarification: Shuf'ah is established in real estate even if it cannot be divided like a bathhouse, mill, well, and road. Shafi'i says: No shuf'ah in what cannot be divided because shuf'ah was legislated to avoid the cost of division, which is not realized in indivisible property. Our proof: The hadith 'Shuf'ah applies to everything' - real estate or produce or other general texts; and because shuf'ah is a means of connecting ownership and the wisdom is to repel harm of adjacency, which applies to both divisible and indivisible. The commentary on 'like a mill and bathhouse' includes what is fixed in the building, not movable like a bowl. The mill includes the lower stone because it is built into the ground. Commentary on 'not in movable property': 'Ard is like 'fals' meaning goods, and every thing is 'ard.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is on the scope of shuf'ah (pre-emption). The author begins by stating that shuf'ah is primarily obligatory in real estate that is owned by a consideration (not gift) and the consideration must be wealth (mal). He then explains two types of shuf'ah: primary (qasdiyyah) which is specific to real estate, and secondary (ghayr qasdiyyah) which applies to trees and fruits that follow real estate. Real estate is defined as anything with a foundation like a house or land. The author emphasizes that the real estate must be owned by a consideration (e.g., purchased, not gifted), and the consideration must be mal (wealth), so if a wife gives her house in khul' (divorce with payment) which is not mal, shuf'ah does not apply. The core dispute: Does shuf'ah apply to indivisible property like a shared mill or bathhouse? Shafi'i says no, because the wisdom behind shuf'ah is to avoid the cost and trouble of division (mu'nat al-qismah), and indivisible property cannot be divided without loss. The Hanafis say yes, because the wisdom is to prevent harm from neighbors (daf' darr al-jiwar), which applies to both divisible and indivisible property. They support their position with the general hadith "al-shuf'ah fi kulli shay'" (shuf'ah applies to everything) and the rational argument that shuf'ah connects ownership and prevents neighbor harm. The passage also clarifies that 'ard (movable property) is not subject to shuf'ah except as accessory. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the Hanafi view.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption in real estate, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the share of another who sells to a third party, primarily to prevent harm from adjacency.
عقار ('iqar): Immovable property with a foundation, such as a house, land, or estate.
عرض ('ard): Movable property other than dirhams and dinars; all goods (متاع).
ضرر الجوار (darr al-jiwar): Harm caused by introducing a new neighbor or co-owner whose presence is undesirable; the underlying wisdom for shuf'ah according to Hanafis.
مؤنة القسمة (mu'nat al-qismah): The cost, effort, and trouble involved in dividing jointly owned property; the Shafi'i cause for shuf'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is shuf'ah established in indivisible property like a shared mill?
A) To avoid the cost and trouble of dividing the property (mu'nat al-qismah).
B) To prevent harm caused by neighbors (daf' darr al-jiwar).
C) Because the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) specifically mentioned mills.
D) Because of analogy with movable property.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah applies to all real estate, divisible or not, because its purpose is to protect against neighbor harm, not to facilitate division.

Shuf'a in Undivided Real Estate (Hanafi vs Shafi'i)
Commentary on shuf'a applying to indivisible real estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school applies shuf'a to indivisible real estate and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith 'Pre-emption is in everything'.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is for removing neighborly harm, not just avoiding division costs.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'a) allows a co-owner or neighbor to step into a sale and buy the property. But does this right apply to shared property that cannot be divided, like a small bathhouse or mill? The Hanafi school says yes, while the Shafi'i school says no. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the decisive Hanafi proof.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school holds shuf'a wajib for all real estate, including indivisible property like a mill or bathhouse.
The key is that shuf'a is not limited by the possibility of physical division.
2
Identify the opposing school's view
Imam al-Shafi'i restricts shuf'a to divisible property only, reasoning that shuf'a's purpose is to avoid the costs of division.
For Shafi'is, indivisible property cannot be divided beneficially, so no shuf'a.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument: shuf'a exists to prevent the hardship of forced division; this hardship does not occur when division is impossible.
They see the illah (effective cause) as the burden of division.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis counter with the general hadith 'Pre-emption is in everything' and argue the real purpose is removing harm from a new neighbor, which exists regardless of divisibility.
The Hanafi illah is 'removing harm of adjacency', which is broader.
5
Determine the practical difference
In Hanafi practice, a co-owner can claim shuf'a for an undivided share; in Shafi'i practice, no such claim exists.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf — the concrete legal consequence.

The Book of Pre-emption: its definition, types, and rulings. 'And if it is not divided, like a mill, bathhouse, and well.' [1] 189. 'And if it is not divided, like a mill, bathhouse, and well': meaning that pre-emption by intention is specific to real estate, unlike non-intentional pre-emption which is established in non-real estate, for trees and fruits are taken by pre-emption as a follow-up to real estate. Then the real estate must be owned with consideration, so if one owns by gift, pre-emption is not established. Then the consideration must be property, so if one divorces in exchange for a house, pre-emption is not established for the neighbor. He only said 'and if it is not divided' because pre-emption is not established according to al-Shafi'i (1) for him in what is not divisible, because pre-emption for him is to remove the burden of division. According to us: to remove harm of adjacency. 'Not in 'ard' [2] water for example, and shared land. According to us: indeed the obligation of pre-emption is to remove harm of adjacency as well, for pre-emption is for the neighbor as well. Clarification of the issue: Pre-emption is obligatory, i.e., established in real estate even if it is not divisible like a bathhouse, millstone [?], well, and road. Al-Shafi'i said: No pre-emption in what does not admit division, because pre-emption was only obligated to remove the burden of division, and this is not realized in what is not divisible, for if it is divided—a division from which one cannot benefit—like the bathhouse and mill. Our proof: His statement: 'Pre-emption is in everything' (3); real estate, produce, or other generalities; and because pre-emption is a cause of connection in ownership, and the wisdom is to remove harm of adjacency, and this encompasses what is divisible and what is not. [1] His statement: 'like a mill, bathhouse... etc.'; mentioned in al-Bahr: includes in the bathhouse what is built into its structure, not separate items like a bowl; and includes in the mill the lower stone, not the upper, because it is built into the ground. Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the application of shuf'a (pre-emption) to real estate that is not divisible, such as a mill, bathhouse, or well. The Hanafi school holds that shuf'a is established in all real estate, whether divisible or not, because the wisdom behind shuf'a is to remove harm from a neighbor by a new co-owner. Al-Shafi'i limits shuf'a to divisible property only, as he sees shuf'a's purpose as avoiding the costs of division. The Hanafis support their position with the general hadith 'Pre-emption is in everything' and the principle that shuf'a connects ownership. The commentator also clarifies that shuf'a applies to trees and fruits only when sold with the land (follow-up), and that shuf'a requires ownership with consideration (not gift, inheritance, etc.). The marginal note explains that for a built-in bath fixture or lower millstone, they are part of the real estate and thus subject to shuf'a.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of pre-emption, i.e., the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the property sold to a third party by compulsory sale.
العقار (al-'iqar): Real estate, including land, buildings, and fixed structures.
العرض (al-'ard): Movable goods other than dirhams, dinars, animals, and real estate.
ضرر الجوار (darar al-jiwar): Harm of adjacency; the potential negative impact of a new neighbor or co-owner.
مؤنة القسمة (mu'nat al-qisma): The burden or expense of dividing property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the bathhouse is considered movable property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school extends shuf'a to all real estate, including indivisible property, based on the general hadith and the principle of preventing neighborly harm.

No Shuf'a in Movables or Ships: The Hanafi-Maliki Dispute
Hanafi refutation of shuf'a in movable goods and ships, with Maliki disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school denies shuf'a in movables and ships, and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument using the hadith and the principle that shuf'a is khilaf al-qiyas.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is exclusive to real estate; movables and ships are excluded because the exception cannot be extended by analogy.

Shuf'a (pre-emption) is a right to replace a buyer in a sale of property, but its scope is limited. In this passage, the Hanafi school firmly denies shuf'a in movable goods and ships, even though Imam Malik considered a ship analogous to real estate. We will examine the Hanafi proof from a hadith and the methodological principle that shuf'a, being a departure from general legal analogy, must be strictly confined to its textual grounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
State clearly: There is no shuf'a in movable goods ('ard) or ships (fulk). This is the mufta bihi position.
Movables include everything except dirhams, dinars, animals, and real estate; ships are specifically mentioned.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The hadith: 'La shuf'ata illa fi rab'in aw ha'it.' (No shuf'a except in a house or garden.) Also the principle that shuf'a is khilaf al-qiyas (contrary to analogy) and thus cannot be extended.
The hadith is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) and is considered a restriction.
3
Analyze the Maliki position and its proof
Imam Malik holds that shuf'a applies to a ship because it is a dwelling (sakan), like real estate. His proof is qiyas: ships are used for dwelling, so they are in the meaning of real estate.
The Maliki analogy is based on the function of the ship as a dwelling.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reject this analogy because (a) the hadith explicitly limits shuf'a to real estate (house and garden), and (b) shuf'a is an exception to the general rule that one cannot force a sale; exceptions are not analogically extended.
Furthermore, a ship is movable, not immovable like real estate, so it is not in the same category.
5
Resolve the apparent objection from the hadith
The hadith seems to restrict shuf'a to only two types of real estate (rab' and ha'it), but shuf'a is agreed upon for all real estate. The commentator explains that the restriction is relative (qasr idaafi), meaning emphasis on these types, not an exhaustive list.
Thus, real estate other than house and garden is included because the cause ('illah) is shared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفُلْكِ) وبناء وتحل بيعاً قصداً [1] حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية (٢) وفُلْكِ) وبناء وتحل بيعاً قصداً حتى إن بيع البناء والنخيل بتبعية الأرض تجب فيهما الشفعة (۲) إلا الدارهم والدنانير، فإنّهما عينان ، قال أبو عبيد : العروض الأمتعة التي لا يدخلها كيل ولا وزن ولا يكون حيواناً ولا عقاراً. كذا في «الصحاح (۱). قال الجلبي) [1]قوله : لا في عرض وفلك...الخ ؛ قال في «البحر»(۳) : يعني لا تجب الشفعة في (٤) عرض وفلك، وقال مالك الله : يجب في السفينة ؛ لأنها تسكن كالعقار. ولنا : ما روي عنه الله إنه قال : لا شفعة إلا في ربع أو حائط ؛ ولأنّ الأخذ بالشفعة ثبت على خلافِ القياس، فلا يجوز إلحاق المنقول به ؛ لأنه ليس في معنى العقار، وهذا الاستدلال فيه شيء، فإنّ ظاهره حصرُ ثبوتِ الشفعة في الربع والحائط ، فدل ذلك على انتفاء حق الشفعة في غيرهما من العروض والسفن. فيرد عليه أن مقتضى الحضر أن لا تثبت الشفعة في عقار غير ربع وحائط ، كضيعة خالية مثلاً، وليس كذلك قطعاً، فكيف يَتَمَسَّك به. قلت : يمكن حمل القصر على القصر الإضافي دون الحقيقي، فالقصر بالنسبة إليهما لا بالنسبة إلى جميع ما عداهما، فتأمل، قال في «العناية») : الربع : الدار، والحائط : البستان، وأصله ما أحاط به [٢]قوله : البناء...الخ ؛ المراد بالبناء والنخيل ذاتهما وأنفسهما بلا واسطة الأرض، وأما بواسطة الأرض فالشفعة فيهما ثابتة كما مرّ غير مرّة، وكذا لا شفعة في الإرث والصدقة لعدم العوض، فالإرث والصدقة والهبة قد خرجت بقوله : ملك بعوض، فإنّ العقار بهذه الأسباب وإن كانت تملك لكن لا بعوض، فلا شفعة بها. أما لو كانت الهبة مشروطة بشرط العوض في صلب العقد يكون بيعاً انتهاءً
'And in a ship, and a building, and it becomes permissible for sale intentionally.' [1] 190. 'Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah' (2) 'And in a ship, and a building, and it becomes permissible for sale intentionally' such that if the building and palm trees are sold as a follow-up to the land, shuf'a is obligatory in them. (2) Except dirhams and dinars, for they are 'ayn. Abu Ubaid said: Al-'urud are goods that are not measured by capacity or weight, and are not animals or real estate. Thus in al-Sihah (1). al-Jalabi said: [1] His statement: 'Not in 'ard or ship... etc.'; he said in al-Bahr (3): meaning that shuf'a is not obligatory in (4) 'ard and ship. Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) said: it is obligatory in the ship because it is a dwelling like real estate. Our proof: what is narrated from him (may Allah have mercy on him) that he said: 'There is no pre-emption except in a house (rab') or a garden (ha'it).' And because taking by pre-emption is established contrary to analogy, so it is not permissible to attach movables to it, because they are not in the meaning of real estate. There is something in this argument, for its apparent meaning restricts the establishment of shuf'a to a house and garden, indicating the negation of the right of pre-emption in other than them from goods and ships. It can be objected that the apparent restriction would entail that shuf'a is not established in real estate other than a house and garden, like a vacant estate for example, and that is not the case absolutely, so how can one hold onto it? I say: it is possible to interpret the restriction as relative restriction, not absolute; so the restriction is with respect to them, not with respect to all others. Consider. He said in al-'Inayah: 'Rab' means a house, and ha'it means a garden; originally it is what is enclosed.' [2] His statement: 'building... etc.'; the intended meaning is the building and palm trees themselves, by themselves without the intermediary of the land. As for by the intermediary of the land, shuf'a is established in them as has been mentioned many times. Similarly, there is no shuf'a in inheritance and charity due to absence of consideration. Thus inheritance, charity, and gift are excluded by his statement: 'ownership with consideration', because real estate by these means, although it is owned, is not with consideration, so there is no shuf'a in it. But if the gift is conditional upon consideration in the contract itself, it becomes a sale, end.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the scope of shuf'a, now addressing movables ('ard) and ships (fulk). The Hanafi position is that shuf'a does not apply to movables or ships, only to real estate. Imam Malik disagreed, holding that a ship, being a dwelling, is like real estate. The Hanafis cite the hadith 'There is no pre-emption except in a house (rab') or a garden (ha'it)' to limit shuf'a to real estate, and also argue that shuf'a is a deviation from analogy (khilaf al-qiyas) and thus should not be extended to movables. The commentator addresses a potential objection: the hadith seems to restrict shuf'a to only a house and garden, but real estate like a vacant estate would be excluded, which is not the case. He resolves this by interpreting the restriction as relative ('idaafi), meaning the focus is on these two types, not an exhaustive list. The passage also clarifies that buildings and palm trees are subject to shuf'a only when sold with the land as a follow-up; if sold separately without land, shuf'a does not apply. Additionally, shuf'a requires ownership with consideration, so it does not apply to inheritance, gift, or charity.

Key Terms
الفلك (al-fulk): A ship; movable vessel.
العرض (al-'ard): Movable goods other than dirhams and dinars.
الربع (al-rab'): A house; a dwelling place.
الحائط (al-ha'it): A garden or an enclosed piece of land.
خلاف القياس (khilaf al-qiyas): Contrary to analogy; a ruling that departs from regular legal reasoning.
قصر إضافي (qasr idaafi): Relative restriction; restricting a ruling to specific examples for emphasis, not exclusion of all others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school deny shuf'a in a ship according to this passage?
A) Because a ship is not considered real estate and shuf'a is only for real estate.
B) Because the ship is too large to be divided.
C) Because ships are not valuable enough.
D) Because the seller must be a neighbor.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is limited to real estate with consideration; movables and ships are excluded because the textual proof restricts it and the exception cannot be extended by analogy.

Shuf'ah Not for Movables and Ships: The Hanafi Ruling and Its Proof
Establishes shuf'ah is not for movables and ships with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that shuf'ah does not apply to movables or ships, and explain the proof from hadith and the principle that shuf'ah is contrary to analogy.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is exceptional (khilaf al-qiyas) and thus restricted to immovable property; it cannot be extended to movables or ships.

In this lesson, we examine a key dispute regarding the scope of shuf'ah. The Hanafi school holds that the right of pre-emption exists only for immovable property, not for movable goods or ships. This position is based on a specific prophetic hadith and a principle that shuf'ah, being a departure from analogy, must be confined to its original domain. We will work through the proof and address the Maliki counterargument.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate that shuf'ah does not apply to 'urud (movable goods) or ships. The right is limited to immovable property.
The ruling is about the scope of the right, not a taklifi hukm.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof: the hadith
Cite the hadith: 'La shuf'ata illa fi rub'in aw ha'it'. Note its apparent restriction to a quarter (house) and garden wall.
This hadith is the primary dalil.
3
Analyze the Maliki objection
Imam Malik argues that ships are inhabited like real estate, so shuf'ah should apply by analogy.
This is a qiyas argument based on habitability.
4
Examine the Hanafi response: hadith interpretation and qiyas principle
The Hanafi response has two parts: (1) The hadith's restriction is interpreted as qasr idafi (additional) not absolute, so it includes all immovable property. (2) Shuf'ah is khilaf al-qiyas (established contrary to analogy), so qiyas cannot extend it to movables.
This reconciles the hadith's wording and maintains the rule.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference is clear: Hanafi denies shuf'ah in ships; Maliki grants it. This affects property transactions and the rights of co-owners.
In fatwa, the Hanafi position is mu'tamad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفُلْكِ وبناء وتحل بيعاً قصداً حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية (٢) وفُلْكِ) وبناء وتحل بيعاً قصداً حتى إن بيع البناء والنخيل بتبعية الأرض تجب فيهما الشفعة (۲) إلا الدارهم والدنانير، فإنّهما عينان ، قال أبو عبيد : العروض الأمتعة التي لا يدخلها كيل ولا وزن ولا يكون حيواناً ولا عقاراً. كذا في «الصحاح (۱). قال الجلبي) [1]قوله : لا في عرض وفلك...الخ ؛ قال في «البحر»(۳) : يعني لا تجب الشفعة في (٤) عرض وفلك، وقال مالك : يجب في السفينة ؛ لأنها تسكن كالعقار. ولنا : ما روي عنه ﷺ إنه قال : لا شفعة إلا في ربع أو حائط ؛ ولأنّ الأخذ بالشفعة ثبت على خلافِ القياس، فلا يجوز إلحاق المنقول به ؛ لأنه ليس في معنى العقار، وهذا الاستدلال فيه شيء، فإنّ ظاهره حصرُ ثبوتِ الشفعة في الربع والحائط ، فدل ذلك على انتفاء حق الشفعة في غيرهما من العروض والسفن. فيرد عليه أن مقتضى الحضر أن لا تثبت الشفعة في عقار غير ربع وحائط ، كضيعة خالية مثلاً، وليس كذلك قطعاً، فكيف يَتَمَسَّك به. قلت : يمكن حمل القصر على القصر الإضافي دون الحقيقي، فالقصر بالنسبة إليهما لا بالنسبة إلى جميع ما عداهما، فتأمل
And ship and building, and it becomes permissible as a sale intentionally... except for dirhams and dinars, for they are commodified goods. Abu Ubaid said: 'Urud are goods that are not measured by weight or capacity, nor are they animals or real estate.' Al-Jalabi said: The statement: not in 'urud and ship... Al-Bahr says: It means shuf'ah is not obligatory in 'urud and ship. Malik said: It is obligatory in the ship because it is inhabited like real estate. Our proof: What is narrated from him (ﷺ) that he said: No shuf'ah except in a quarter or a garden wall. And because taking by shuf'ah is established contrary to analogy, so it is not permissible to attach movable property to it because it is not in the meaning of real estate. There is something in this reasoning, for its apparent meaning is the restriction of shuf'ah to a quarter and a garden wall, which indicates the negation of the right of shuf'ah in other than these two, such as goods and ships. It is objected that the apparent meaning would require that shuf'ah not be established in real estate other than a quarter and a garden wall, such as a vacant estate, and that is certainly not the case, so how can one cling to it? I say: It is possible to interpret the restriction as additional (idafi) rather than absolute, so the restriction is with respect to these two, not with respect to all others. Ponder.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage deals with the scope of shuf'ah (pre-emption) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating that shuf'ah is not established in 'urud (movable goods) and ships, and also addresses buildings and palm trees sold separately from the land. The commentator first clarifies the definition of 'urud: it refers to commodities not measured by weight/capacity and not animals or real estate. He then presents the Hanafi view that shuf'ah does not apply to ships or movable goods, contrasting it with Imam Malik's opinion that shuf'ah does apply to ships because it is inhabited like real estate. The Hanafi proof is twofold: a hadith (Narrated from the Prophet ﷺ: 'La shuf'ata illa fi rub'in aw ha'it'), and a rational argument that shuf'ah is established contrary to qiyas (analogy), so it cannot be extended to movables which are not similar to real estate. The commentator then addresses an objection: the hadith seems to limit shuf'ah to rub' (house) and ha'it (garden), which would exclude other real estate like a vacant plot, yet shuf'ah is agreed to apply to all real estate. He resolves this by interpreting the hadith's restriction as 'additional' (qasr idafi) rather than absolute, meaning the Prophet mentioned these two as examples, not as an exhaustive list. Thus, the ruling remains that shuf'ah applies to all immovable property, but not to movables or ships.

Key Terms
عروض ('urud): Movable goods other than dirhams, dinars, animals, and real estate.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner to purchase the share of a new owner in the same property upon sale.
ربع (rub'): A house or dwelling.
حائط (ha'it): A garden or enclosed land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is shuf'ah established in a ship that is sold?
A) Yes, because it is inhabited like real estate.
B) No, because ships are movable goods and shuf'ah is only for immovable property.
C) Yes, by analogy with houses.
D) No, only if the ship is attached to land.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is limited to immovable property; it does not apply to 'urud or ships, establishing that this right is an exception confined to real estate.

Shuf'ah Not in Non-Compensatory Transfers
Negative ruling on shuf'ah in non-sale transfers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which property transfers do not establish the right of shuf'ah and state the reason related to absence of compensation.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah only arises in sales with compensation; inheritance, charity, and pure gifts are excluded.

Shuf'ah (pre-emption) is a right tied to the sale of immovable property. This passage clarifies that shuf'ah does not apply to transfers that lack a compensatory element, such as inheritance, charity, and unconditional gifts. Understanding this limitation prevents misapplication of the right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that shuf'ah is not established in inheritance, charity, and gift (without compensation). Recognise that these are non-compensatory transfers.
2
Recognize the underlying principle
The reason given is 'lack of compensation' ('iwad). In Hanafi fiqh, shuf'ah is a right that arises only in sales, where property is exchanged for a consideration.
The term 'iwad appears in the text as the key condition.
3
Apply the principle to the categories
For each category (inheritance, charity, gift), confirm that no compensation is given. Therefore, the condition for shuf'ah is absent.
Inheritance is a succession right; charity and gifts are voluntary transfers.
4
Note the exception
The passage adds an exception: if a gift is conditioned on compensation in the contract itself, it is considered a sale from the outset, and shuf'ah applies.
This is the case of hibah bi shart al-'iwad, treated as a sale.
5
Connect to broader shuf'ah rules
Recall that shuf'ah is generally established in sales of undivided shares (musha') of immovable property. This lesson reinforces that the presence of 'iwad is a prerequisite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لا شفعة في الإرث والصدقة لعدم العوض، فالإرث والصدقة والهبة قد خرجت بقوله : ملك بعوض، فإنّ العقار بهذه الأسباب وإن كانت تملك لكن لا بعوض، فلا شفعة بها. أما لو كانت الهبة مشروطة بشرط العوض في صلب العقد يكون بيعاً انتهاءً وارث وصدقةٍ وهبة إلا بعوض [1]قوله : إلا بعوض أي بشرط عوض مقبوض بلا شيوع في الموهوب، وعوضه لأنها هبة ابتداء، وإن لم يكن العوض مشروطاً فلا شفعةً فيها. قاله الجلبي
Similarly, no shuf'ah in inheritance and charity because of the absence of compensation. So inheritance, charity, and gift have been excluded by his statement: ownership by compensation. For real estate acquired through these means is owned but without compensation, so there is no shuf'ah in them. If the gift is conditional on compensation in the contract itself, it becomes a sale. End. Inheritance, charity, and gift except with compensation. [1] His statement: except with compensation, meaning with a condition of compensation that is received without being common in the gifted item, and its compensation because it is a gift initially, and if the compensation is not conditioned, then there is no shuf'ah in it. Al-Jalabi said it.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage explains that shuf'ah is not established in transfers that are not sales, such as inheritance, charity (sadaqah), and gifts (hibah) that are given without compensation. The reasoning is that shuf'ah is only established when ownership is transferred in exchange for a consideration ('iwad). In these cases, there is no consideration, so the right of shuf'ah does not arise. The commentary also clarifies an exception: if a gift is made with a stipulation of compensation in the contract itself, it is effectively a sale and shuf'ah would apply. The phrase 'except with compensation' (illa bi 'iwad) in the matn means that the only gifts that trigger shuf'ah are those where compensation is a condition, and even then, it is considered a sale. This ruling is consistent with the general principle that shuf'ah is tied to sale.

Key Terms
عوض ('iwad): Compensation or consideration given in exchange for property.
هبة بشرط العوض (hibah bi shart al-'iwad): A gift conditioned on the recipient giving something in return; treated as a sale in Hanafi fiqh.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption right allowing a co-owner to purchase a sold share to avoid a new partner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is shuf'ah established in a property received as a gift without any condition of compensation?
A) Yes, because property has been transferred.
B) No, because there is no compensation.
C) Yes, if the gift is for a relative.
D) No, only if the gift is from a stranger.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is restricted to sales with compensation; non-compensatory transfers like inheritance, charity, and unconditional gifts do not trigger pre-emption.
