Shuf'ah Applies to Exchanges Beyond Cash Sales
Lists exceptions where shuf'ah applies despite non-sale countervalues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which non-sale transactions trigger shuf'ah in Hanafi fiqh and explain the Hanafi rationale.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah applies to any exchange where property is given as compensation.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) is typically associated with sales, but the Hanafi school extends it to other transactions where property is exchanged for compensation. This passage from the sharh clarifies that shuf'ah applies when a property is given as dower (mahr), compensation for khul' (badal al-khul'), rent (ujrah), settlement for intentional killing (sulh 'an dam 'amd), and manumission payment ('itq). The key is that these are mutual exchanges ('iwad), even if the countervalue is not cash. Let's examine the Hanafi reasoning and the Shafi'i objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed cases
List the five types of countervalues mentioned: dower, khul' compensation, rent, blood money settlement, and manumission payment. Recognize that in each case property is given as a countervalue in an exchange.
2
Understand the Hanafi principle
The common factor is that these are mutual exchanges ('iwad). The Hanafi school holds that shuf'ah applies because the cause of ownership (sabab) is the exchange, not the specific form of consideration. Benefits (manafi') are appraisable, so the pre-emptor can pay the value.
3
Contrast the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that these countervalues are not like property in kind, so shuf'ah should only apply by taking the value, but taking without compensation is not legislated. Thus, he denies shuf'ah in these cases.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: 'The valuation of benefits is necessary' (taqwim al-manafi' daruri), meaning benefits can be appraised. Also, 'the pre-emptor takes by the same cause as the buyer' – the cause is the exchange, which is legislated. Therefore, shuf'ah applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودار قسمت أو جعلت أجرة أو بدل خلع أو عتق أو صلح عن دم عمد، أو مهر وإن قوبل ببعضها مال [2]قوله : خلاف الشافعي؛ قال في «البحر» : وقال الإمام الشافعي : يجب فيها الشفعة، فيأخذ هذا بقيمتها عند تعذر الأخذ بمثلها بخلاف الهبة بلا عوض؛ لتعذر الأخذ بلا عوض، إذ هو غير مشروع. انتهى. ولنا على ما قال الشارح : إن تقوم المنافع ضروري، فلا تظهر في حق الشفعة، وأيضاً : إنّ الشفيع يتملك بما يملك به المشتري من السبب، لا بسبب آخر ، وهاهنا لو أخذه كان يأخذ بسبب آخر وهو غير مشروع
And a house that is divided, or made as rent, or as compensation for khul', or manumission, or settlement for intentional killing, or dower, even if it is compensated with some wealth. [2] His statement: contrary to al-Shafi'i; al-Bahr said: Al-Shafi'i said: Shuf'ah is obligatory in it, so the pre-emptor takes it by its value when taking the like is difficult, unlike a gift without compensation because taking without compensation is difficult and it is not legislated. End. Our proof according to what the commentator said: That the valuation of benefits is necessary, so it does not appear in the right of shuf'ah, and also: The pre-emptor owns by the same cause as the buyer, not by another cause, and here if he takes it, he would be taking by another cause which is not legislated.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage lists specific cases where a property is acquired through a means that is not a straightforward sale (such as when a house is given as rent payment, as dower, as compensation for khul', as payment for manumission, or as blood money for intentional killing) but shuf'ah still applies according to the Hanafi school. The issue is that these countervalues are not fungible (like cash) but are evaluated based on use or specific rights. The Hanafi position is that shuf'ah does apply in these cases because the underlying transaction is a mutual exchange ('iwad), even if the consideration is not cash. The commentator then presents the Shafi'i view that shuf'ah does not apply because these countervalues are not like the property in kind, and the Shafi'i would require taking by value. The Hanafi response is that benefits (manafi') are necessarily appraised and thus the right of shuf'ah is still valid; moreover, the pre-emptor must take by the same cause as the buyer, and here the cause is the exchange, which is legislated. So shuf'ah applies.

Key Terms
بدل الخلع (badal al-khul'): Compensation paid by the wife to the husband to obtain divorce through khul'.
صلح عن دم عمد (sulh 'an dam 'amd): A settlement agreement in case of intentional killing, where the victim's family accepts compensation instead of qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does shuf'ah apply when a man gives a house as dower (mahr) in marriage?
A) Yes, because the house is given as compensation, like a sale.
B) No, because marriage is not a sale.
C) Yes, but only if the dower is cash.
D) No, because there is no compensation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, shuf'ah attaches to any transaction where property is given as compensation in an exchange, not just to formal sales.

Cases Where Pre-emption Does Not Apply
Lists exceptions and provides proofs with disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the types of property dispositions that do not give rise to pre-emption and explain the Hanafi reasoning for each exception.

Core Idea
Pre-emption applies only to absolute property exchanges; not to gifts, inheritances, or compensations for benefits or blood.

Pre-emption (shuf'ah) is a right of first refusal for co-owners in jointly owned property, but it does not attach to every transfer. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya details the specific dispositions where no pre-emption arises, including non-commercial transfers and transactions where ownership is not fully transferred. The text also presents the Hanafi proofs and addresses disagreements with Imam al-Shafi'i and within the school itself. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying the law of pre-emption in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify non-exchange transactions
Read the list: inheritance, charity, gift without compensation, divided property. These are not sales or property exchanges, so pre-emption cannot arise.
Division (qismah) is separation of ownership, not transfer.
2
Examine compensations for benefits and blood
Rent, khul', emancipation, blood-money, and dower are compensations for benefits or blood, not for property itself. The Hanafi proof: benefits are estimated necessarily, not as independent property. Similarly, blood is not property.
Shafi'i disagrees, considering these compensations as property (muttaqawwimat).
3
Analyze mixed transactions
When a marriage includes a house plus a cash payment, the sale element is subordinate (tabi'). Abu Hanifa denies pre-emption entirely, while Sahibayn apply it to the cash portion. Understand the reasoning: the transaction's primary nature is marriage, not sale.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute with practical consequences.
4
Understand sales with options and invalid sales
Pre-emption is suspended while a seller's option exists or during an invalid sale (fasad), because ownership has not fully transferred. Once the option lapses or the invalid sale is upheld, pre-emption becomes established.
The mudarib example illustrates that an agent's sale also does not trigger pre-emption.

أو بيعت بخيار البائع وما سقط خياره أو بيعاً فاسداً وما سقط حق فسخه مبادلة مالية، وهو يقول : معنى البيع تابع فيه ؛ ولهذا ينعقد بلفظ النكاح، ولا يفسد بشرط النكاح ، ولا شفعةً في الأصل، فكذا في البيع.
[1].
(أو بيعت بخيار البائع وما (۱) سقط خياره ) حتى إذا سقط الخيار تثبتُ الشفعة ) ، (أو بيعاً فاسدا وما سقط حق فسخه) ، فإنَّهُ إذا بيع بيعاً فاسداً وسقط فكذا في البيع، ألا ترى أنّ المضارب إذا كان رأس ماله ألفاً فأحـجـر وربح ألفاً، ثمّ اشترى بألفين داراً في جوار ربِّ المال، ثم باعها بألفين، فإنّ رب المال لا يستحق الشفعة في حصة المضارب، تبعاً لرأس المال ؛ لأنّ المضارب وكيل في حقه، وليس في بيع الوكيل شفعة، وكذا في حق المضارب. كذا في «العناية»(۲).

(3) His statement: 'an invalid sale' etc. That is, the house was sold with an invalid sale, so there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor, neither before nor after delivery. As for before delivery, because the seller's ownership has not been transferred; as for after delivery, because of the possibility of rescission, and the right of rescission is established by the law to remove corruption. And in establishing the right of pre-emption, there is confirmation of corruption, so it is not permissible, unlike when the option is for the buyer in a valid sale, because he became more specific to it in terms of disposal, but in an invalid sale, he is prevented from it. Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from the work 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqayah, discussing the cases where the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) does not apply. The author lists several types of property transfers that are not considered sales or exchanges of property, hence pre-emption does not arise. The list includes inheritance, charity, and a gift without compensation; a divided house; a house given as rent, khul' compensation, emancipation, blood-money settlement, or dower — even if these are accompanied by some monetary consideration. The commentary explains that in a division (qismah), the property is merely separated, not transferred. For rent and similar compensations, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that pre-emption applies because these are property compensations, but the Hanafi position relies on the principle that benefits (manafi') are estimated as necessary, not as independent property, and blood (dam) is not property. Furthermore, if compensation is partially monetary (e.g., marriage with a house plus payment), Abu Hanifa denies pre-emption in the entire house, while the two Sahibayn limit pre-emption to the portion corresponding to the payment. The passage then addresses sales with a seller's option and invalid sales. In a sale with a seller's option, pre-emption is not established while the option remains, but once the option lapses, ownership transfers and pre-emption becomes established. Similarly, an invalid sale initially does not transfer ownership, so no pre-emption; but if the right of rescission lapses (meaning the buyer takes possession and the transaction is upheld), then pre-emption arises. An example from mudaraba illustrates that pre-emption does not apply when an agent sells, as the sale is not by the owner. The commentary clarifies the conditions for demand in such cases. Overall, the lesson clarifies that pre-emption is a right tied exclusively to valid, unconditional sales (or exchanges with full property consideration), and does not extend to gratuitous transfers or those where the transfer of ownership is incomplete or temporary.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the share of a sold property at the price paid by the buyer, prior to the buyer's full ownership.
مبادلة مالية (mubadalah maliyyah): A financial exchange where property is transferred in exchange for other property.
منافع (manafi'): Benefits or usufruct, such as the use of a property.
صلح عن دم عمد (sulh 'an dam amd): Settlement for intentional bloodshed (homicide), often involving financial compensation (diyah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does pre-emption apply to a house given as a compensation for khul' (divorce at wife's request)?
A) Yes, because it is a property transfer.
B) No, because it is a compensation for benefits, not a sale.
C) Yes, if the house is divisible.
D) No, because khul' is a gift.

Key Takeaway
The right of pre-emption is strictly limited to valid, unconditional property sales; it does not extend to gratuitous transfers or compensations for benefits, blood, or marriages.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Shuf'ah When Ownership Obstacles Are Removed: Seller's Option and Fasid Sale
Commentary explaining when shuf'ah becomes established after obstacles are removed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which shuf'ah is established in sales involving a seller's option or a fasid sale, and explain the ruling on the timing of the demand (talab).

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is not established while an obstacle prevents full ownership transfer; only when the obstacle lapses does shuf'ah become wajib.

In Hanafi fiqh, the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) attaches when ownership of a property transfers from seller to buyer. However, certain obstacles can delay that transfer, such as a seller's option (khiyar al-ba'i') or the right to rescind a fasid (voidable) sale. Understanding precisely when these obstacles are removed is critical for determining when a neighbour or co-owner can claim shuf'ah. This passage from Husn al-Dirayah clarifies the rule, including an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether the demand (talab) should be made at the time of sale or at the lapse of the obstacle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is the establishment of shuf'ah (right of pre-emption). The ruling is that it is wajib (obligatory) for the shafi' (pre-emptor) to claim it when the conditions are met. Determine the hukm level and the scenarios where shuf'ah is initially absent but later becomes obligatory.
Shuf'ah is wajib based on necessity, not fard.
2
Locate the dalil and the underlying rationale
The dalil is not a specific text from Quran or Sunnah but a qiyas based on the transfer of ownership. The rationale ('illa) is that shuf'ah follows ownership transfer; any obstacle to complete transfer suspends shuf'ah until the obstacle is removed.
This is a jaliy (clear) qiyas: asl = valid sale without obstacles, far' = sale with obstacles, illa = obstacle preventing ownership transfer.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah for the two scenarios
For seller's option: the option prevents ownership from leaving the seller. When it lapses, ownership transfers and shuf'ah becomes wajib. For fasid sale: the right of rescission (haqq al-faskh) means ownership is provisional; shuf'ah is not established even after possession until that right lapses, because establishing shuf'ah would confirm the fasid sale which is impermissible.
Distinguish from buyer's option (khiyar al-shart) where shuf'ah is immediate because ownership transfers upon sale.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the demand (talab)
Some early mashayikh held that the demand should be made at the time of the sale because the sale is the cause of shuf'ah. The correct (sahih) view, preferred by Al-Hidayah and Al-Inayah, is that demand must be made when the option lapses, because that is when ownership actually transfers and thus shuf'ah becomes due.
This ikhtilaf affects the validity of a prior demand if made too early.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi is the view that shuf'ah is established only upon lapse of the obstacle, and demand is required at that time. This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah and the later Hanafi authorities.
Authority_weight: zahir_riwayah. Cross-madhhab: mukhtalaf fih (other schools may differ on conditions of shuf'ah).

Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the text of al-Wiqayah concerning the rules of shuf'ah (pre-emption). It addresses two scenarios where shuf'ah is initially absent but later becomes established due to the removal of an obstacle. First, when a sale is made with a seller's option (khiyar al-ba'i'), shuf'ah is not established while the option is active because ownership does not transfer completely. However, once the option lapses (i.e., the seller confirms the sale), ownership transfers and shuf'ah becomes obligatory. Second, in a fasid (voidable) sale, shuf'ah is not established even after possession because the right to rescind the sale remains, which prevents the complete transfer of ownership. Only when that right of rescission lapses (e.g., by the buyer's consent or by court ruling) does shuf'ah become applicable. The author provides an example involving a mudarib (silent partnership) to illustrate that no shuf'ah arises in the sale by an agent, as the agent's sale does not transfer ownership from the agent but from the principal. The footnotes further refine: if the buyer has an option (khiyar al-shart), shuf'ah is established immediately because the option does not prevent ownership transfer; the seller's ownership is gone. The demand (talab) for shuf'ah should be made at the time the option lapses, not at the time of sale, according to the correct view. For the fasid sale, shuf'ah is denied even after possession to avoid confirming the invalid transaction, unlike a valid sale with buyer's option where the buyer has full control.

Key Terms
خيار البائع (khiyar al-ba'i'): An option (right to cancel the sale) given to the seller, usually for a specified period.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is invalid due to a defect in the subject matter or consideration, but can be rectified.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbour to pre-empt (buy) a property sold to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately at the time of the contract.
B) When the option lapses.
C) Only after possession of the property.
D) Never, because khiyar prevents any right of pre-emption.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is established only after the removal of any obstacle (seller's option or right to rescind a fasid sale) that prevents full ownership transfer; the demand must be made at that moment.

Shuf'ah upon Return and Iqalah: When a Return is a New Sale
Clarifying when shuf'ah is established or not upon return or rescission of a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between return by court ruling and return by mutual agreement, and state whether shuf'ah is established, citing the Hanafi reasoning and the dispute with Zufar.

Core Idea
A return without court order or an iqalah is a new sale for the shafi, reviving his right of shuf'ah.

In the previous lesson, we saw that shuf'ah attaches to valid sales. But what happens when a sale is undone? This lesson examines two specific scenarios: the buyer returns the property due to a defect or option, or the parties mutually rescind (iqalah). The Hanafi school draws a crucial distinction between a court-ordered return (which is a pure rescission) and a mutual agreement return (which is treated as a new sale in the right of the shafi). This distinction determines whether the neighbour can pre-empt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two return scenarios
First scenario: return by qada' (court ruling) due to option of sight, stipulation, or defect. Second scenario: return by mutual consent without court order (radd bi-la qada').
The text says: 'or returned due to option of sight or stipulation or defect by court ruling' and 'it is obligatory upon return without court ruling'.
2
Classify each return as faskh or new sale
Court-ordered return is a rescission (faskh) because it undoes the original contract. Mutual agreement return is treated as a new purchase because the seller was not obligated to accept it, so the parties' consent creates a new transaction.
The reasoning: 'since the return was not obligatory, taking it by mutual consent is as if he bought it again'.
3
Apply the shuf'ah rule
Shuf'ah attaches only to new sales, not to rescissions. Therefore, no shuf'ah on court-ordered return; shuf'ah is established on mutual agreement return. Similarly, iqalah is considered a sale in the right of third parties (the shafi').
The text: 'iqalah is a sale in the right of a third party, and the shafi' is the third of them'.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Zufar held that iqalah and mutual return do not revive shuf'ah because the shafi' right was extinguished upon delivery, and iqalah is a rescission from the parties' perspective. The majority Hanafi response: the parties have authority over themselves but not over third parties, so iqalah is a new sale for the shafi'.
The dispute is captured in Takmilat al-Bahr. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
5
Apply to a fasid sale scenario
If a property was sold in a fasid (defective) sale and then the seller delivers it to the buyer before the shafi claims, the seller loses his right to pre-empt because the property is no longer in his ownership.
This is an extra point from the text about removal of the obstacle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة تعريفها وأنواعها وأحكامها
أو رد بخيار رؤيةٍ أو شرط أو عيب بقضاء بعدما سلمت وتجب برد بلا قضاء، وبإقالة وتجب برد بلا قضاء، وبإقالة حق الفسخ بأن بنى المشتري فيها يثبت الشفعة)، (أو رد بخيار رؤية أو شرط أو عيب بقضاء بعدما سُلّمت) : أي بيعت وسلّمت الشفعة ، ثم ردَّ البيع بخيار الرؤيةِ وبقضاء القاضي فلا شفعة ؛ لأنه فسخ " لا بيع.
وتجب برد بلا قضاء، وبإقالة ) : أي يثبتُ الشُّفعةُ في الرَّد بالعيب بلا قضاءِ القاضي ؛ لأنه لما لم يجب الرَّدُّ فأخذه بالرّضاء صار كأنه اشتراه ، وكذا تجب الشفعة بالإقالة ؛ لأن الإقالة بيع في حق الثالث، والشفيع ثالثهما .
[1]قوله : يثبت الشفعة ؛ لزوال المانع ، وإن بيعت دار بجنبها وهي في يد البائع بعد فله الشفعة ؛ لبقاء ملكه، وإن سلّمها إلى المشتري فهو شفيعها؛ لأنّ الملك له، ولا يؤدي إلى تقرير الفساد ؛ لأنّ الفسخ ممكن بعد الأخذ، ولا فساد في المأخوذ.
ثم إن سَلّم البائع الدار المبيعة بالبيع الفاسد إلى المشتري قبل الحكم بالشفعة للبائع بطلت الشفعة ؛ لزوال ما كان يستحقها به ، كما إذا باع رجلٌ داره قبل الحكم بالشفعة له بطلت شفعته.
[٢] قوله : لأنه فسخ ، فعاد إلى قديم ملكه، والشفعة في إنشاء العقد لا في الاستمرار والبقاء على ما كان. (۳) قوله : وتجب برد بلا قضاء وبإقالة...الخ ؛ قال في تكملة البحر» : يعني تجب الشفعة إن ردّها المشتري بعيب بغير قضاء، أو تقايلا البيع، وقال زفر ه : لا تجب ؛ لأنّ شفعته بطلت بالتسليم، والردّ بالعيب بغير قضاء إقالة، والإقالة فسخ لقصدهما ذلك، والعبرة لقصد العاقدين.
قلنا : الإقالة سبب للملك بتراضيهما كالبيع غير أنهما قصدا الفسخ، فيصح فيما لا يتضمن إبطال حق الغير؛ لأنّ لهما ولاية على أنفسهما، فيكون فسخاً في حقهما، ولا ولاية لهما على غيرهما، فيكون بيعاً جديداً في حق الشفيع، فيتجدد له به حق الشفعة.
Book of Shuf'ah: its definition, types and rulings. Or: returned due to option of sight or stipulation or defect by court ruling after it has been delivered, and it is obligatory (shuf'ah) upon return without court ruling and upon iqalah. And it is obligatory upon return without court ruling, and upon iqalah, the right of rescission like if the buyer builds on it, shuf'ah is established. (Or returned due to option of sight or stipulation or defect by court ruling after it has been delivered): that is, the property was sold and delivered, then the sale was rescinded due to option of sight and by the ruling of the qadi: there is no shuf'ah because it is a rescission (faskh), not a sale. And it is obligatory upon return without court ruling, and upon iqalah: that is, shuf'ah is established in the return due to defect without the qadi's ruling, because since the return was not obligatory, taking it by mutual consent is as if he (the buyer) bought it (again). Similarly, shuf'ah is obligatory upon iqalah, because iqalah is a sale in the right of a third party, and the shafi' is the third of them. [1] He said: Shuf'ah is established due to the removal of the obstacle. If a house is sold adjacent to it and it is in the seller's hand afterwards, he has shuf'ah due to the continuation of his ownership. If he delivered it to the buyer, then he (the seller) is its shafi' because ownership belongs to him, and it does not lead to confirmation of fasād because rescission is possible after taking, and there is no fasād in what is taken. Then if the seller delivers the house sold by a fasid sale to the buyer before the ruling of shuf'ah for the seller, the shuf'ah becomes void due to the removal of what was the basis for it, just as if a man sells his house before the ruling of shuf'ah for him, his shuf'ah becomes void. [2] He said: Because it is faskh, it returns to his previous ownership, and shuf'ah is in the initiation of the contract, not in continuation and remaining as before. (3) He said: And it is obligatory upon return without court ruling and upon iqalah... etc. He said in Takmilat al-Bahr: meaning shuf'ah is obligatory if the buyer returns it due to a defect without a court ruling, or they mutually rescind the sale. And Zufar said: it is not obligatory because his shuf'ah was invalidated upon delivery, and return due to defect without court ruling is an iqalah, and iqalah is a rescission due to their intention, and consideration is given to the intention of the contracting parties. We say: iqalah is a cause for ownership by mutual consent like a sale except that they intend rescission, so it is valid as long as it does not involve annulment of a third party's right, because they have authority over themselves. So it is a rescission in their own right, but they have no authority over others, so it is a new sale in the right of the shafi', and thereby his right of shuf'ah is renewed.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on shuf'ah, focusing on cases where the property has been returned to the seller after the sale. The text distinguishes between different modes of return. First, if the buyer returns the property due to an option (like khiyar al-ru'yah or khiyar al-shart) or due to a defect, and this return is effected by a court ruling (qada'), then no shuf'ah arises because the return is a rescission (faskh) of the original contract, not a new sale. Shuf'ah attaches only to new sales, not to mere cancellations. However, if the buyer returns the property due to a defect without resorting to the qadi (i.e., by mutual agreement between buyer and seller), then shuf'ah is established. The reasoning is that since the seller is not obliged to accept the return, the mutual agreement constitutes a new transaction akin to a sale reviving the right of shuf'ah. Similarly, iqalah (mutual rescission) is treated as a sale in the eyes of third parties, so the shafi' gets a renewed right. The text then delves into a complication: if the seller, after selling in a fasid sale, delivers the property to the buyer before the court rules on the shafi' claim, the shafi' right is lost. The footnote by al-'Inayah and the rebuttal of Zufar's view are included. Zufar argued that iqalah is a rescission and the shafi' right terminated upon delivery, but the Hanafi response is that iqalah is a new sale vis-à-vis the shafi' because the parties have no authority over him.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a sale by the consent of both buyer and seller.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or cancellation of a contract, returning ownership to its previous state.
رد (radd): Return of a sold property by the buyer to the seller due to a defect or option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer returns a house to the seller due to a hidden defect, but without going to court. Does the neighbour (shafi') have shuf'ah?
A) Yes, because the return is considered a new sale by mutual consent.
B) No, because the return is a rescission, and shuf'ah only applies to new sales.
C) Yes, only if the buyer had an option of sight.
D) No, because the neighbour's right was lost when the house was delivered to the buyer.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a return of property without court order or an iqalah is considered a new sale for the pre-emptor, reviving shuf'ah, while a court-ordered return is a rescission and does not give shuf'ah.

Shuf'a in Return Without Qada and in Iqala
Ruling on shuf'a in return without qada and iqala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a return by judicial decree (with qada) and a return without judicial decree or by iqala, state the Hanafi ruling for each, and explain the reasoning based on the nature of the transaction (rescission vs. new sale in respect of third parties).

Core Idea
A return without judicial decree or an iqala is treated as a new sale for the shafi', renewing the right of shuf'a.

The right of shuf'a (preemption) allows a co-owner or neighbour to buy a property when it is sold to an outsider. But what happens if the buyer later returns the property to the seller? Does the shafi' get another chance? The Hanafi school distinguishes between returns by court order and returns by mutual consent. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the reasoning behind it, including a key internal disagreement with Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage discusses three situations after delivery: (1) return due to option of sight/condition/defect by a judge's ruling, (2) return due to defect without a judge's ruling, and (3) mutual cancellation (iqala). Determine the ruling for each.
The ruling for (1) is no shuf'a; for (2) and (3) shuf'a is established.
2
Understand the legal characterization
A return by judicial decree is a rescission (faskh) — the property reverts to the seller, and shuf'a only applies to new sales. A return without decree is treated as a new sale because it is by mutual consent. Similarly, iqala is a rescission between the parties but a new sale for third parties.
The key concept: 'al-iqalah bay' fi haqq al-ghayr' (iqala is a sale in the right of others).
3
Analyze Zufar's objection
Imam Zufar argues that the shuf'a was invalidated by delivery, and the return without qada is an iqala, which is a rescission based on the parties' intent. Thus no new shuf'a arises.
Zufar gives weight to the contracting parties' intention to rescind.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The majority (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) respond that the parties have authority over themselves (so it is rescission for them) but not over the shafi' (so it is a new sale for him). Hence the shafi' gains a renewed right.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of 'al-wilayah 'ala al-nafs wa 'adam al-wilayah 'ala al-ghayr'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو رد بخيار رؤيةٍ أو شرط أو عيب بقضاء بعدما سلمت وتجب برد بلا قضاء، وبإقالة. (أو رد بخيار رؤية أو شرط أو عيب بقضاء بعدما سُلّمت) : أي بيعت وسلّمت الشفعة، ثم ردَّ البيع بخيار الرؤيةِ وبقضاء القاضي فلا شفعة؛ لأنه فسخ لا بيع. وتجب برد بلا قضاء، وبإقالة: أي يثبتُ الشُّفعةُ في الرَّد بالعيب بلا قضاءِ القاضي؛ لأنه لما لم يجب الرَّدُّ فأخذه بالرّضاء صار كأنه اشتراه، وكذا تجب الشفعة بالإقالة؛ لأن الإقالة بيع في حق الثالث، والشفيع ثالثهما. [2] قوله: لأنه فسخ، فعاد إلى قديم ملكه، والشفعة في إنشاء العقد لا في الاستمرار والبقاء على ما كان. [3] قوله: وتجب برد بلا قضاء وبإقالة...الخ; قال في تكملة البحر: يعني تجب الشفعة إن ردَّها المشتري بعيب بغير قضاء، أو تقايلا البيع، وقال زفر: لا تجب; لأنّ شفعته بطلت بالتسليم، والردّ بالعيب بغير قضاء إقالة، والإقالة فسخ لقصدهما ذلك، والعبرة لقصد العاقدين. قلنا: الإقالة سبب للملك بتراضيهما كالبيع غير أنهما قصدا الفسخ، فيصح فيما لا يتضمن إبطال حق الغير; لأنّ لهما ولاية على أنفسهما، فيكون فسخاً في حقهما، ولا ولاية لهما على غيرهما، فيكون بيعاً جديداً في حق الشفيع، فيتجدد له به حق الشفعة.
Or return due to option of sight, condition, or defect by a judicial ruling after delivery: no shuf'a. And it is obligatory (shuf'a is established) in a return without a judicial ruling, and in an iqala. (Meaning:) The property was sold and the shuf'a was delivered (to the buyer), then the sale was returned due to the option of sight by the judge's ruling, so no shuf'a because it is a rescission, not a sale. And shuf'a is established in a return due to defect without a judge's ruling because since the return was not obligatory, taking it by mutual consent becomes as if he purchased it. Likewise shuf'a is established in iqala because iqala is a sale in the right of a third party, and the shafi' is the third party. [2] His statement: 'because it is a rescission' – so it returns to his previous ownership, and shuf'a is in the initiation of the contract, not in continuation and remaining as it was. [3] His statement: 'And it is obligatory in a return without a judicial ruling and in iqala, etc.' said in Takmilat al-Bahr: It means shuf'a is established if the buyer returns it due to a defect without a judicial ruling, or they cancel the sale (taqayala). And Zufar said: It is not established because his shuf'a was invalidated by delivery, and the return due to defect without a judicial ruling is an iqala, and iqala is a rescission due to their mutual intent, and consideration is given to the intent of the contracting parties. We say: Iqala is a cause for ownership by mutual consent like a sale, except that they intended rescission. So it is valid in matters that do not entail nullifying the right of a third party, because they have authority over themselves, so it is a rescission with respect to them, and they have no authority over others, so it is a new sale with respect to the shafi', and thus the right of shuf'a is renewed for him.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage addresses a key issue in shuf'a: whether the right persists after the buyer returns the property. The matn distinguishes between a return by judicial decree (qada) and a return by mutual consent (without qada) or by iqala (cancellation). When the buyer returns the property due to a defect or option with a judge's ruling after delivery, no shuf'a arises because the return constitutes a rescission (faskh), not a new sale. The property reverts to the seller's ownership, and shuf'a only applies to new sales, not to rescissions. However, if the buyer returns the property without a judge's ruling (by mutual satisfaction), or if they mutually cancel the sale (iqala), shuf'a is established. The reasoning is that in these cases, the transaction is effectively a new sale from the buyer back to the seller. Since the return was not obligatory, it is like a new purchase. Similarly, iqala is considered a sale in the right of a third party, and the shafi' is that third party. This is supported by the famous Hanafi position as stated in Takmilat al-Bahr, which also includes the dissenting view of Imam Zufar, who argues that the shuf'a was already invalidated by delivery and that iqala is a rescission. The Hanafi response is that iqala, while a rescission between the original parties, is a new sale regarding third parties because the parties have authority over themselves but not over others. Therefore, the shafi' acquires a renewed right of shuf'a.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Mutual cancellation of a sale contract by the parties, equivalent to a new sale in the right of third parties.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; annulment of a contract, returning the property to the seller.
شفعة (shufʿah): Preemption right; the right of a co-owner or neighbour to replace a buyer in a sale of property.
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): Judicial ruling; a decree by a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is shuf'a established in the case of iqala (mutual cancellation of sale)?
A) Because iqala is a rescission
B) Because iqala is considered a new sale in the right of third parties
C) Because the buyer intended to sell
D) Because the judge ordered it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a return by mutual consent or an iqala after delivery renews the shafi's right of preemption because it is treated as a new sale for the third party, ensuring the shafi' is not deprived by parties acting to their own advantage.

Shuf'a in a Fasad (Invalid) Sale
Ruling on shuf'a in a fasad (invalid) sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of an invalid (fasad) sale on shuf'a rights and identify when the right is established or lost.

Core Idea
An invalid sale does not transfer ownership, so shuf'a can still be claimed — but the right is lost if the seller delivers the property before the shuf'a ruling.

Shuf'a (pre-emption) gives a neighbor the right to purchase an adjacent property when it is sold. But what happens when the sale itself is invalid (fasad)? Does the neighbor still have a right? This passage from the Hanafi tradition clarifies that the shuf'a right may survive an invalid sale, provided the shafi' acts promptly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A house adjacent to the shafi's property is sold in a fasad (invalid) sale. The passage examines the shafi's right.
The fasad sale does not transfer ownership.
2
Determine possession status
If the seller still holds the property, ownership remains with him, so the shafi' can claim shuf'a against the seller.
If the seller delivers to the buyer, the buyer gains possession, but ownership is still not fully transferred.
3
Apply the rule for delivery to buyer
Even if the seller delivers to the buyer, the shafi' still has shuf'a against the buyer, because the shafi' can later rescind the invalid sale.
The right is not blocked by the buyer's possession.
4
Note the exception: early delivery voids shuf'a
If the seller delivers the property to the buyer before the shafi' obtains a judicial ruling (or before claiming), the shuf'a right is lost.
The shafi' must act before possession changes hands.
5
Understand the analogy
Just as a man who sells his own house before claiming shuf'a loses his right, the shafi' loses the right if he delays until the buyer takes possession.
Prompt action is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: يثبت الشفعة; لزوال المانع، وإن بيعت دار بجنبها وهي في يد البائع بعد فله الشفعة; لبقاء ملكه، وإن سلّمها إلى المشتري فهو شفيعها; لأنّ الملك له، ولا يؤدي إلى تقرير الفساد; لأنّ الفسخ ممكن بعد الأخذ، ولا فساد في المأخوذ. ثم إن سَلّم البائع الدار المبيعة بالبيع الفاسد إلى المشتري قبل الحكم بالشفعة للبائع بطلت الشفعة; لزوال ما كان يستحقها به، كما إذا باع رجلٌ داره قبل الحكم بالشفعة له بطلت شفعته.
His statement: 'Shuf'a is established due to the removal of the preventer. If a house adjacent to it is sold while it is still in the hand of the seller, he (the neighbor) has shuf'a due to the continuation of his ownership (of the adjacent property). And if (the seller) delivers it to the buyer, then he (the neighbor) is its shafi' (has shuf'a) because the ownership (of the right) is for him. This does not lead to affirmation of the invalid contract, because rescission is possible after taking, and there is no invalidity in the taken property. Then if the seller delivers the house sold in the invalid sale to the buyer before the judicial ruling of shuf'a for the seller, the shuf'a becomes void due to the removal of that on which he deserved it, just as if a man sold his house before the judicial ruling of shuf'a for him, his shuf'a becomes void.'
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of a fasad (invalid) sale on shuf'a. The scenario involves a neighbor's shuf'a right when an adjacent house is sold in an invalid sale. If the seller still has possession, the neighbor can claim shuf'a because the seller's ownership persists (the invalid sale did not transfer ownership). If the seller delivers possession to the buyer, the neighbor still has shuf'a against the buyer because the buyer has a possessory interest. The passage notes that this does not affirm the invalid sale because the shafi' can rescind after taking. However, if the seller delivers the property to the buyer before the shuf'a claim is adjudicated (i.e., before the shafi' obtains a ruling), the shuf'a becomes void. This is analogous to a person selling his own house before claiming shuf'a on an adjacent property; his right is lost. The ruling emphasizes that the shafi' must act promptly; once the seller completes the transaction by delivery, the opportunity for shuf'a is lost.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bayʿ fāsid): An invalid sale that lacks a condition for validity but can be corrected by consent or rescission.
شفعة (shufʿa): Pre-emption: the right of a neighbor or partner to purchase a property that has been sold to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the sale contract is signed.
B) When the seller delivers the property to the buyer before the shuf'a claim.
C) When the buyer pays the price.
D) When the shafi' learns of the sale.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a can arise even from an invalid sale, but the shafi' must assert his right before the seller transfers possession to the buyer.

Shuf'a for the Authorized Slave and His Master
Ruling on shuf'a for slave and master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of shuf'a for an authorized slave in debt and explain the underlying rationale of mutual rights.

Core Idea
An authorized slave in debt and his master each have shuf'a in each other's sales.

We now examine a unique extension of shuf'a rights in Hanafi law: the interplay between a master and his authorized slave who is deeply in debt. This passage clarifies that both parties possess the right of pre-emption in sales involving each other, reflecting the Hanafi view of the slave's qualified legal capacity and the master's ownership of the slave's acquisitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
The rule concerns two parties: the authorized slave (ʿabd maʾdhūn) who is in debt, and his master.
2
Understand the slave's condition
The slave must be in debt that encompasses his entire person and earnings, giving him a real financial interest.
Debt 'encompassing his person and earnings' means the debt equals or exceeds his value and income.
3
Recognise the dual shuf'a rights
The slave has shuf'a in his master's sale of property; the master has shuf'a in the slave's sale of trade property.
It is a symmetrical right: each can pre-empt the other's sale.
4
Grasp the rationale
The slave's debt creates an independent financial interest; the master owns the property the slave holds. Both are legitimate sharers.
The principle: 'what is in the slave's hand is owned by the master' gives the master an interest.
5
Apply the rule
In practice, if the master sells adjacent land, the slave may claim shuf'a; if the slave sells from his trading stock, the master may claim.
Both must be neighbours/participants in the sale property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللعبد المأذون مديوناً في بيع سيّدِهِ، ولسّيّده في مبيعه: أي تجب الشفعة للعبد المأذون حال كونه مديونا دينا محيطا برقبته وكسبه، فله الشفعة فيما باغ سيده، وكذا للسيدِ حق الشفعة فيما باع العبد المأذون المذكور بناءً على أنَّ ما في يده ملك له.
And for the authorized slave in debt regarding the sale of his master, and for his master in his sale: meaning shuf'a is obligatory (established) for the authorized slave while he is in debt with a debt encompassing his person and earnings, so he has shuf'a in what his master sold. Likewise, the master has the right of shuf'a in what the aforementioned authorized slave sold, based on the fact that what is in his hand is his ownership.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage extends the right of shuf'a to a special category: the authorized slave (al-ʿabd al-maʾdhūn) who is heavily in debt. Such a slave is permitted to trade. The rule states that this slave has the right of shuf'a in property sold by his master, even though the slave himself is owned by the master. This is because the slave's debt encumbers his entire value, so he has an independent financial interest. Conversely, the master also has shuf'a in property sold by his authorized slave. The underlying principle is that the property in the slave's hand is considered owned by the master (since the slave is property), but the slave's trading capacity gives him a stake. Both are considered potential shafīʿs because each has an interest in the property adjacent to theirs.

Key Terms
عبد مأذون (ʿabd maʾdhūn): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade, typically to pay off debts.
شفعة (shufʿa): Pre-emption: the right to acquire property that is sold to a third party, usually on account of adjacency or partnership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the master sells any property, regardless of the slave's debt
B) When the slave is in debt encompassing his person and earnings
C) Only when the slave is not in debt
D) Only when the master grants permission

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an authorized slave in debt and his master each have a right of shuf'a in the other's property sales, reflecting the slave's qualified legal capacity and the master's ultimate ownership.

Who Has Shuf'a: The Buyer, Not the Seller
General principle establishing shuf'a for the buyer and denying it for the seller and guarantor of darak

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which parties have the right of shuf'a according to the Hanafi school and explain the rationale for excluding the seller and the guarantor of darak.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is a right for the buyer only—the seller and any guarantor of the sale's validity forfeit it.

This passage from al-Hidayah lays down a fundamental rule of shuf'a (pre-emption): it is established for the buyer, whether he buys personally or through an agent, but not for the seller, nor for one on whose behalf a sale is made, nor for a guarantor of the price (ḍāmin al-darak). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying shuf'a in property transactions and for recognizing that the right does not transfer to the seller or to those who facilitate the sale from the seller's side.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties mentioned in the passage
List each party: the buyer, the one for whom it was bought (principal), the seller, the one for whom it was sold, and the guarantor of darak. Note the Arabic terms used for each.
The term 'sharā' (bought) and 'ish tarā' (bought for) are key.
2
State the ruling for each party
Determine for which party shuf'a is established (wājib) and for which it is not. The passage explicitly says shuf'a is 'for the one who bought or for whom it was bought, not for the one who sold or for whom it was sold, or who guaranteed the darak.'
The ruling is a clear affirmative (thubūt) for buyer/principal and negation (nafy) for seller/guarantor.
3
Understand the rationale behind the exclusions
Explain why the seller and guarantor are excluded: the seller has already divested his interest in the property, so he has no standing to claim pre-emption. The guarantor of darak, by guaranteeing the price or warranty, has effectively become a party in perfecting the sale, so he cannot later challenge it. 'Because the perfection of the sale was from him, so he cannot nullify what was perfected by him.'
These rationales are based on uṣul principles of consistency (adam al-tanāquḍ).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf on the condition of return after possession
The passage includes a discussion from al-Hidayah about whether the condition that a return due to defect occurs after possession is necessary. Imām Muḥammad holds that it is necessary because sale of immovable before possession is invalid (like movables), so the return must be after possession to trigger shuf'a. The Ṣāḥibayn (Abū Yūsuf and Abū Ḥanīfa) hold that sale before possession is valid for immovables, so the condition is irrelevant. Recognize that this difference affects only a specific sub-case and does not alter the general rule.
This is an intra-madhhab dispute; the majority view in later texts follows Imām Muḥammad for this ruling.
5
Apply the rule to a practical scenario
Consider a scenario: A (principal) appoints B as an agent to buy a house adjacent to A’s property. B buys the house. Later, A exercises shuf'a to buy it from himself? No, the benefit of the rule becomes clear when A is a partner in the purchased property. The agent's purchase is attributed to A, so A has shuf'a as if he bought directly. But if A were the seller, he would have no such right.
The passage also notes that if the buyer or principal is a partner, the shuf'a allows him to protect his share from an outsider.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمن شرى أو اشترى له، لا لمن باع أو بيع له، أو ضمن الدرك: أي تجب الشفعة للمشتري سواء اشترى أصالة أو وكالة، وكذا تجب الشفعة من اشترى له: أي من وكل آخرَ بالشّراءِ فاشترى لأجل الموكل، والموكَّلُ شفيع كان له الشفعة، وفائدتهُ: إِنَّهُ لو كان المشتري أو الموكَّلُ بالشَّراءِ شريكاً، وللدار شريك قال الشارح: قال صاحب «الهداية»: ومراده بالرد بالعيب: الردّ بعد القبض، قال: وهذا إنما يستقيم على قول محمد له; لأنّ بيع العقار عنده قبل القبض لا يجوز كما في المنقول، وأما على قولهما يجوز بيعه قبل القبض، فلا يفيد القيد المذكور، والله أعلم. [2] قوله: ضمن الدرك ...الخ; يعني إذا ضمن الشفيع الدَّرْكَ عن البائع فلا شفعة له; لأنّ تمام المبيع إنما كان من جهته فليس له أن ينقض ما تم من جهته. كذا في «تكملة البحر». [3] قوله: أي تجب الشفعة للمشتري...الخ، أي يثبت للمشتري مطلقاً، وللموكل بالشراء الشفعة في مشتراه، ولما أوردَ عليه أنّه ما فائدة أخذ الشفعة في ملك نفسه، وأجاب عنه بقوله: وفائدته ... الخ، صورة المسألة التي تظهر فيها الفائدة دار بين ثلاثة، ولها جار ملاصق.
And for the one who bought or for whom it was bought, not for the one who sold or for whom it was sold, or who guaranteed the darak: i.e. shuf'a is obligatory (established) for the buyer whether he bought as a principal or as an agent, and likewise shuf'a is established for the one for whom it was bought: i.e. the one who appointed another to buy, so he bought for the principal, and the principal is a shafi' and has shuf'a. Its benefit is that if the buyer or the one who appointed for purchase was a partner, and the house has a partner... the commentator said: the author of al-Hidayah said: and his intent by 'return due to defect' is the return after possession. He said: This only stands on the view of Muhammad because according to him, sale of immovable property before possession is not valid, as in movables. As for their view, it is valid to sell it before possession, so the mentioned restriction is not beneficial. And Allah knows best. [2] His statement: 'guaranteed the darak' etc. means if the shafi' guarantees the darak (price guarantee) from the seller, then he has no shuf'a because the perfection of the sale was from him, so he cannot nullify what was perfected by him. This is in Takmilat al-Bahr. [3] His statement: 'i.e. shuf'a is established for the buyer, etc.' means it is established absolutely for the buyer, and for the principal in his purchase. And when it was objected: what is the benefit of taking shuf'a in one's own property? He answered: its benefit... the scenario where the benefit appears is a house between three partners, and it has an adjacent neighbor.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage lays down a general rule: shuf'a is only for the buyer (whether he buys directly or through an agent), and not for the seller (the one who sold), nor for the one on whose behalf a sale was made (if someone else sold for him), nor for the one who guarantees the price (darak). The rationale is that the seller has already divested his interest, and the guarantor's involvement makes him a party that would be contradicting his own guarantee. The passage also includes a relevant discussion from al-Hidayah about the timing of return due to defect. According to Imam Muhammad, the sale of immovable property before possession is invalid, so the condition of return after possession is meaningful. On the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the sale is valid before possession, so the condition is moot. Another note clarifies that if the shafi' himself guarantees the seller against any claim on the property (daman al-darak), he forfeits his shuf'a because he has become a party to the sale's completion. Finally, the benefit of the rule that the principal has shuf'a is illustrated by a scenario where the buyer or the principal is a partner; then the shuf'a allows him to protect his share from an external neighbor acquiring adjacent property.

Key Terms
ضمن الدرك (ḍamina al-darak): To guarantee the price or indemnify the seller against any claim that might invalidate the sale.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent to buy; he has shuf'a on the purchase made by his agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer who buys for himself
B) The agent who buys for someone else
C) The seller
D) The principal who appoints an agent to buy

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is a right exclusively for the buyer (including the principal) and not for the seller or any guarantor of the sale.

The Legal Device (Hila) to Drop the Right of Shuf'a for a Neighbor
Explaining a legal device to nullify neighbor's pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi legal device (hila) for dropping the neighbor's right of pre-emption and apply its conditions to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Selling all but a tiny strip of the property drops the neighbor's shuf'a because the sale is no longer complete.

You are selling your house but want to avoid the neighbor's right to pre-empt. The Hanafi school provides a lawful device: exclude a small physical portion from the sale. In this lesson, we examine the conditions and reasoning behind this hila, and why other schools object.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
The neighbor's shuf'a right (pre-emption) attaches to a complete sale of the entire adjacent property. If the sale is incomplete, the right does not arise.
2
Explain the hila
The seller sells the house except for a small, measurable portion (e.g., a cubit, handspan, or finger) along the neighbor's boundary. This renders the sale partial, and the neighbor's shuf'a is dropped.
The exception must be a physical part of the property, not a separate right.
3
List conditions for validity
The excepted portion must be measurable (e.g., a cubit in width and the full length of the boundary) and must be of the property itself. It cannot be a purely nominal or non-separable portion.
If the exception is nominal, the hila may be invalid.
4
Consider the subsequent procedure
After the sale, the seller may gift or sell the excepted strip to the buyer, making the buyer a co-owner. This reinforces that no shuf'a exists for the neighbor.
This step is not required for the hila to be effective, but it is often done.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i and Maliki schools reject such hiya (legal devices) as evasions of the law. They hold that the neighbor retains the right of pre-emption regardless of the exception.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (difference of opinion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة تعريفها وأنواعها وأحكامها ولا فيما بيع إلا ذراعاً من طول حد الشفيع آخر، فلهما الشفعة ولو كان هو شريكاً، وللدَّارِ جار فلا شفعة للجار مع وجوده، ولا يكون للبائع شفعة سواء كان أصيلاً أو وكيلاً، وكذا لا شفعة لمن بيع له : أي إن وكل بالبيع، والموكَّلُ شفيع فلا شفعة له، وكذا إذا ضمن الدرك فبيع، وهو شفيع له، لا شفعة له؛ لأن الاستخلاص عليه). (ولا فيما بيع إلا ذراعاً من طول حد الشفيع)، هذا حيلة لإسقاط شفعة الجوار، وهي أن تباعَ الدَّارُ إلا مقدار عرضه ذراع، أو شبر، أو أصبع، وطوله تمام... أو شرى سهماً منهما بثمن ثم باقيها إلا في السهم الأول لا تثبت الشفعة... لا تثبت الشفعة؛ لو وهب هذا القدر بعد بيع ما عدا هذا للمشتري، ومثله ما لو باعه له؛ لأنه صار شريكاً في الحقوق، فلا شفعة للجار. [٢] قوله : في السهم الأوّل؛ أي في السهم الأوّل فقط، والباقي للمشتري؛ لأنه شريك: أي نظراً إلى ما قبل الأخذ منه.
The book of pre-emption: its definition, types, and rulings. And there is no pre-emption in what is sold except a cubit from the length of the neighbor's boundary. Then both have pre-emption even if he is a partner. And for the house there is a neighbor, so no pre-emption for the neighbor in his presence. And the seller does not have pre-emption whether he is the principal or an agent. Similarly, no pre-emption for the one on whose behalf it is sold: i.e., if he appointed an agent to sell, and the principal is a pre-emptor, he has no pre-emption. Similarly, if he guarantees the liability and then it is sold, he being a pre-emptor, he has no pre-emption because the completion came through him. (And there is no pre-emption in what is sold except a cubit from the length of the neighbor's boundary). This is a legal device to drop the neighbor's pre-emption, which is that the house is sold except for the amount of a cubit in width, or a handspan, or a finger, and its full length... Or he buys a share from them for a price then the rest of it, except for the first share, pre-emption is not established... Pre-emption is not established; if he gifts this amount after selling the rest to the buyer, and similarly if he sells it to him, because he became a partner in the rights, so the neighbor has no pre-emption. His statement: 'in the first share' means only in the first share, and the rest is for the buyer because he is a partner, i.e., considering before taking from him.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation from the Hanafi school regarding the right of pre-emption (shuf'a), specifically the legal device (hila) to eliminate the neighbor's right of pre-emption (shuf'at al-jiwar). The text begins by stating that if a property is sold except for a small strip (a cubit or less) along the neighbor's boundary, then the neighbor's right of pre-emption is dropped. This is because the property is not sold in its entirety, and the neighbor's right only applies to a complete sale of the entire property. The passage then addresses the scenario where someone buys two shares of a property: if he buys one share first, then buys the remaining share except for the first share, the pre-emption right does not apply to the first share. The commentary explains that if the owner later gifts or sells that small strip to the buyer, the neighbor still has no pre-emption because the buyer has become a partner in the property. The ruling is based on the principle that pre-emption is established only for a complete sale, not for partial sales or devices that break the unity of the sale. This is a well-known Hanafi hila to avoid the obligation of pre-emption for a neighbor.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption whereby a co-owner or neighbor can purchase the share sold to a third party by paying its price.
حيلة (hila): A legal device used to achieve a permissible result without violating the apparent ruling, here dropping the neighbor's shuf'a by selling all but a tiny portion.
شفعة الجوار (shuf'at al-jiwar): The right of pre-emption for an adjacent neighbor (who is not a co-owner) in a sale of immovable property.

In Hanafi fiqh, selling a property except for a measurable physical portion is a valid legal device to drop the neighbor's right of pre-emption.

Exception of the First Share from Pre-emption
Expansion of grammatical exception for first share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pre-emption when a buyer purchases a share adjoining the pre-emptor's house first and then the rest, and explain the grammatical exception that exempts the first share from pre-emption.

Core Idea
The first share adjoining the pre-emptor's property is excepted from pre-emption because it was not sold as a separate adjoining plot.

Pre-emption (shuf'a) allows a neighbor or co-owner to buy a sold property. However, when a buyer acquires a share that adjoins the pre-emptor's house first and then buys the rest, the first share is excepted from pre-emption. This lesson unpacks the grammatical analysis (istithna') that determines this exception, showing how a seemingly technical point yields a clear legal outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon (buying one share then the rest) and the ruling: pre-emption does not apply to the first share.
The first share is the 'sahm al-awwal' that adjoins the pre-emptor's house.
2
Locate the exception in the text
Find the phrase 'إلا في السهم الأول' (except in the first share). This is the grammatical exception that triggers the analysis.
The exception is from the pronoun of sale, not from the relative pronoun.
3
Analyze the grammar of the exception
Understand that the exception is from a complete affirmative statement (the act of sale), making the accusative case obligatory. This is contrasted with a negative statement where both cases might be allowed.
The commentator uses the analogy of 'I will not honor those who insulted me except Zaid' to illustrate.
4
Apply the grammatical analysis to determine scope
Conclude that the first share is excepted from the scope of pre-emption, meaning the buyer's first purchase does not trigger shuf'a, and the buyer becomes a partner in the rest.
If the exception were from the relative pronoun, pre-emption would apply, which is false.
5
Note the practical consequence
The neighbor cannot claim pre-emption on the first share because it is excepted; the buyer has priority as a partner in the remaining property.
This avoids unfairness: the buyer already owned a share before purchasing the rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو شرى سهماً منهما بثمن ثم باقيها إلا في السهم الأول ما يلاصق من الدار المبيعةِ دار الشفيع ؛ فإنَّهُ إذا لم يبع مالاً يلاصق دار الشفيع ، لا تثبت الشفعة ) (أو شرى سهماً منهما بثمن ثم باقيها إلا في السهم الأول) [Y] وأجاب عنه في «العَزْميَّة»: بأنه مستثنى من مالاً من ضمير بيع، فالنصب على التبعية باعتبار محل المجرور، والتبعية بضمير بيع ، تقتضي الرفع ؛ لأنه كلام تام من كلام تام غير موجب... اهـ ملخصاً. أقول : أمّا النَّصبُ في عبارة المصنّف - أي مصنف «تنوير الأبصار» – فواجب بلا شبهة ؛ لأنه استثناء من كلام تام ،موجب، وأما في عبارة «الوقاية» و«الدرر» فكذلك، والاستثناء من ضمير بيع لا من الموصول، وهو من كلام تام موجب أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ النفي غيرُ متوجه إليه. توضيحه : لو أهانك جماعة إلا زيداً منهم ، فقلت : لا أكرم من ما هانوني إلا زيداً، على أن زيداً مستثنى من الواو ، لا من الموصول، ووجب فيه النصب ؛ لأنه مستثنى من الواو قبل دخول النفي ؛ لأن معنى : من أهانوني إلا زيداً لا أكرمهم، وصار زيد كالمسكوت عنه في حصول الإكرام له وعدمه. ولو جعلته مستثني من الموصول إن كان من المهينين أيضاً جـاز فـيـه النـصـبُ والرفع ؛ لأنه من كلام تام غير موجب ، وصار محكوماً عليه بالإكرام قطعاً. وعبارة «الدرر» من قبيل الأول ؛ لأنَّ المعنى ما بيع إلا ذراعاً لا شفعة فيه، ولو كان الذراع من الموصول لكان المعنى أنّ الشفعة تثبت فيه، ولا يخفى فساده ، فاغتنم هذا التحرير في هذا المقام، فقد زل فيه كثير من الأفهام. [١] قوله : لا تثبت الشفعة ؛ لو وهب هذا القدر بعد بيع ما عدا هذا للمشتري ، ومثله ما لو باعه له ؛ لأنه صار شريكاً في الحقوق، فلا شفعة للجار. [۲] قوله : في السهم الأوّل ؛ أي في السهم الأوّل فقط، والباقي للمشتري ؛ لأنه شريك : أي نظراً إلى ما قبل الأخذ منه. قال في «العناية»(۱) : لأنه حين اشترى الباقي كان شريكاً لشراء الجزء الأول، (۱) «العناية» (٩ : ٤٢١).
Or he bought one share from them with a price then the remainder, except in the first share what adjoins from the sold house the pre-emptor's house; for if he did not sell property adjoining the pre-emptor's house, pre-emption is not established. (Or he bought one share from them with a price then the remainder, except in the first share) [Y] And he answered in al-Azmiyya: that it is excepted from the pronoun of sale (mālā), so the accusative case is due to dependency considering the place of the genitive, and the dependency on the pronoun of sale requires the nominative; because it is a complete speech from a complete speech that is not affirmative... summarized. I say: As for the accusative in the wording of the author - that is the author of Tanwir al-Absar - it is obligatory without doubt because it is an exception from a complete affirmative speech. And as for in the wording of al-Wiqaya and al-Durar, it is likewise, and the exception is from the pronoun of sale, not from the relative pronoun, and it is also from a complete affirmative speech because negation is not directed to it. Explanation: If a group insulted you except Zaid from them, and you said: 'I will not honor those who insulted me except Zaid,' with Zaid excepted from the plural pronoun, not from the relative, and the accusative is required because it is excepted from the plural pronoun before the entrance of negation; because the meaning is: those who insulted me except Zaid I will not honor them, and Zaid becomes as if unspoken regarding the occurrence of honor for him and its absence. And if you made it excepted from the relative pronoun, if he was also from the insulters, then both accusative and nominative are allowed because it is from a complete non-affirmative speech, and he becomes definitely judged to be honored. And the wording of al-Durar is of the first type because the meaning is 'nothing was sold except a cubit in which there is no pre-emption', and if the cubit was from the relative, the meaning would be that pre-emption is established in it, and its falsity is not hidden. So seize this analysis in this context, for many understandings have slipped in it. [1] His saying: Pre-emption is not established; if he donated this amount after selling what excepted this to the buyer, and likewise if he sold it to him because he became a partner in rights, so there is no pre-emption for the neighbor. [2] His saying: in the first share; meaning only in the first share, and the rest is for the buyer because he is a partner, meaning considering before taking from him. He said in al-Inaya: because when he bought the rest he was a partner in buying the first part.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage deals with the exception in the pre-emption ruling when someone buys a share and then the remainder. The text states that the first share that adjoins the pre-emptor's house is excepted; if the seller did not sell a property adjoining the pre-emptor's house, pre-emption does not apply. The commentator then discusses the grammatical analysis of the exception, whether it is from the pronoun of the verb 'sale' or from the relative pronoun. He explains that the correct interpretation is that the exception is from the pronoun of sale, making it an exception from an affirmative complete statement, which requires the accusative case. He provides an analogy about honoring people who insulted you, showing the difference. He then applies it to the text: the exception of the first share means that pre-emption does not operate on that share; the buyer becomes a partner in the rest and thus has priority over the neighbor.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by paying its price.
استثناء (istithna'): Grammatical exception, where something is excluded from the general ruling of the sentence.
السهم الأول (al-sahm al-awwal): The first share of the property that is sold, which adjoins the pre-emptor's property in this context.

Key Takeaway
The first share adjoining the pre-emptor's house is excepted from pre-emption due to a grammatical exception that changes the legal outcome, emphasizing the importance of precise linguistic analysis in Hanafi fiqh.

Legal Tricks to Drop Pre-emption: A Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on legal stratagems with two concrete tricks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain two Hanafi legal tricks for dropping pre-emption, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding their dislike, and state the mufta bihi positions for pre-emption and zakat.

Core Idea
Legal tricks to drop pre-emption are mubah per Abu Yusuf; fatwa follows him in pre-emption but not in zakat.

The Hanafi school recognises the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) for neighbours. To avoid this right, buyers sometimes employ legal stratagems (hiyal). This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya describes two such tricks and then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Is it disliked to use tricks to drop pre-emption or zakat? Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ, and the fatwa is nuanced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tricks
The passage gives two tricks: (a) buying with a price then paying with a garment; (b) buying a tiny share first to become a partner. Identify how each works and what they aim to achieve.
The first trick generalizes to all pre-emption; the second is specific to neighbor's pre-emption.
2
Understand the mechanism
Explain how each trick discourages the pre-emptor from exercising his right. The first makes the price too high; the second makes the pre-emptor only eligible for a small share at a high price.
3
State the dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that tricks to drop pre-emption and zakat are not disliked (mubah). Muhammad holds they are disliked (makruh). The passage also notes that this dispute applies only when the right is not yet established.
4
Apply the tarjih
The fatwa in pre-emption follows Abu Yusuf (not disliked). In zakat, the fatwa follows Muhammad (disliked). This shows case-by-case tarjih.
Explicitly stated: 'وبِهِ يُفْتَى في الشفعة وبضده في الزكاة'
5
Note the qualification
The dispute is only when the pre-emption or zakat right has not yet become established. If already established, using a trick to drop it is disliked unanimously.
Source: 'اعلم أن الخلاف فيما إذا لم يكن ثابتةً بعد'

(أو شرى بثمن ثم دفع عنه ثوباً لا يأخذ إلا بالثمن)، هذه حيلة أخرى تعم الجوار وغيره، وهي ما إذا أريد بيعُ الدَّارِ بمئة فيشتري الدار بمئة فيشتري الدار بألف ثم يدفع ثوباً يساوي مئة في مقابلة الألف، فالشفيع لا يأخذه إلا بألف". Or he bought with a price then paid for it with a garment — he does not take except with the price; and the trick to drop pre-emption and zakat is not disliked according to Abu Yusuf, and by this the fatwa is given in pre-emption, and the opposite in zakat. This is another trick to drop neighbor's pre-emption: that if he wants to buy the house for a thousand, he buys a small portion of it, like one share out of a thousand shares, for a thousand minus a dirham, then buys the remainder for a dirham. So the pre-emptor does not take pre-emption except in the first share at its price, not in the remainder, because the buyer has become a partner and he is more entitled than the neighbor. (Or he bought with a price then paid for it with a garment — he does not take except with the price), this is another trick that generalizes to neighbor and other, and it is that if it is intended to sell the house for a hundred, and he buys the house for a thousand then pays a garment worth a hundred in return for the thousand, so the pre-emptor does not take it except for a thousand. [3] And the trick to drop pre-emption and zakat is not disliked according to Abu Yusuf, and by this the fatwa is given in pre-emption and the opposite in zakat. Know that the trick to drop them is not disliked according to Abu Yusuf, and it is disliked according to Muhammad. And the pre-emptor's entitlement to the first part does not invalidate the buyer's pre-emption in the second part before litigation, because it is in his ownership, so he is given precedence over the neighbor. This is in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His saying: this is another trick that generalizes to neighbor and other; meaning contrary to what came before it, for those two are not utilized for the right of a partner, because they are for dropping neighbor's pre-emption. [2] His saying: he does not take it except for a thousand; in Radd al-Muhtar: and the pre-emptor does not desire that sale because of the high price, and he indicated that this trick does not invalidate his pre-emption, for if he agrees to pay that price he may take, unlike the first trick as we have mentioned. [3] His saying: and the trick to drop is not disliked; know that the dispute is when it is not yet established. As for dropping an established right, it is disliked unanimously. It is that the buyer says to the pre-emptor after taking pre-emption: 'I will sell it to you for what you took,' so there is no benefit in taking pre-emption. The pre-emptor is deceived by his sweet words and surrenders the pre-emption, so it drops, and the house remains in the buyer's hand safely. This is in al-Bazzaziyya and al-Kafi.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents two legal tricks to avoid pre-emption. The first trick: the buyer purchases the property for a high price but pays with a garment worth less, so the pre-emptor can only take at the stated price (the high price), discouraging him. The second trick is specific to neighbor's pre-emption: the buyer first buys a tiny share (e.g., one part of a thousand) for a high price minus a dirham, then buys the remainder for a dirham. The pre-emptor can only take the first share at its high price, not the remainder, because the buyer has become a partner and is thus prioritized over the neighbor. Then the ruling on whether such tricks are disliked (makruh) is discussed: Abu Yusuf holds that tricks to drop pre-emption and zakat are not disliked, and the fatwa is given accordingly in pre-emption, but in zakat the opposite is followed (i.e., they are disliked). Muhammad, however, considers such tricks disliked. The commentator further clarifies that this dispute applies only when the pre-emption right has not yet become established; if it is already established, then tricking to drop it is unanimously disliked.

Key Terms
حيلة (hila): A legal trick (stratagem) used to achieve a permissible result while avoiding a legal ruling.
شفعة الجوار (shuf'at al-jiwar): The right of pre-emption due to being a neighbor, applicable in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on using a legal trick to drop pre-emption if the right is not yet established?
A) It is haram
B) It is disliked according to Abu Yusuf
C) It is not disliked according to Abu Yusuf
D) It is invalid

Key Takeaway
Legal tricks to drop pre-emption are not disliked according to the mufta bihi, but this ruling is restricted to unestablished rights; for zakat, the opposite view is followed.

Legal Stratagems to Cancel Shuf'a and Zakat
Intra-madhhab dispute on hiyal for shuf‘a and zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on stratagems to avoid pre-emption and zakat, identify the mufta bihi position for each, and explain the distinction between preventing an obligation and nullifying an established one.

Core Idea
Stratagems that prevent a right from becoming obligatory are not disliked for shuf‘a, but are disliked for zakat — the fatwa differs by context.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah delves into the Hanafi school’s internal debate on using legal stratagems (hiyal) to circumvent the right of pre-emption (shuf‘a) and the obligation of zakat before they become binding. It presents the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with the relied-upon fatwa that distinguishes between the two obligations. Understanding this dispute requires appreciating the nuance between preventing a right from arising and cancelling an existing one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two stratagems
Read the description of the first stratagem (buying with a cloth worth less) and the second (buying a tiny share at high price then the rest cheaply). Understand how each makes pre-emption unattractive for the neighbour.
The first stratagem is general; the second targets neighbour pre-emption.
2
Locate the core ruling and fatwa
Find the sentence: 'Wa la yukrah hilat isqat al-shuf‘ah wa al-zakah ‘ind Abi Yusuf, wa bihi yufta fi al-shuf‘ah wa bi-diddihi fi al-zakah'. This is the key: the stratagem is not disliked according to Abu Yusuf, and fatwa follows him for shuf‘a but the opposite for zakat.
'Bihi yufta' indicates the operative position.
3
Trace the intra-madhhab disagreement
Note that Muhammad ibn al-Hasan considers such stratagems disliked (makruh). The disagreement is only when the right is not yet established; after establishment, both agree it is disliked.
The text explicitly mentions 'wa yukrah ‘ind Muhammad'.
4
Determine the scope before vs. after establishment
The commentary clarifies that the dispute applies before the right of pre-emption or zakat obligation becomes binding. Once it is established, any stratagem to nullify it is unanimously disliked.
See the quote from al-Bazzaziyya and al-Kafi in the margins.
5
Apply the distinction to other obligations
The passage extends the discussion to zakat, hajj, and the prostration verse. For example, selling livestock before the year passes or reading the prostration verse silently to avoid prostration are analogous. The same principle: prevention before obligation vs. cancellation after.
The ruling differs because zakat and other obligations are purely for Allah (haqq Allah), whereas shuf‘a is a right of a human (haqq al-‘abd).
