Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses legal stratagems (hiyal) to avoid the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) and the obligation of zakat. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani. The passage first describes two stratagems. The first: to buy a property at a high price (e.g., one thousand) but pay with a cloth worth much less (e.g., one hundred), so the pre-emptor, if he wants to pre-empt, must pay the full high price, which discourages him. The second stratagem targets pre-emption of a neighbour: the buyer purchases a tiny share (e.g., one share out of a thousand) for almost the full price (e.g., 999 dirhams) and then buys the rest for a trivial amount (e.g., 1 dirham). The pre-emptor can only pre-empt the first share at its high price; the buyer is now a partner with ownership of the rest and takes precedence over the neighbour. Neither stratagem nullifies pre-emption entirely but makes it practically unattractive. The central ruling is then stated: according to Abu Yusuf, such stratagems are not disliked (makruh), and fatwa is given accordingly in cases of pre-emption. However, in zakat, the opposite is followed, meaning the stratagem is disliked. According to Muhammad, these stratagems are disliked. The commentator clarifies that this disagreement applies only before the right of pre-emption becomes established (i.e., before the buyer takes possession or the pre-emptor asserts his right). Once the right is established, eliminating it via stratagems is disliked by unanimous agreement. The text also notes the extension to other obligations: zakat, hajj, and the prostration verse; for example, selling livestock before the zakat year elapses or reciting the prostration verse silently to avoid the obligation of prostration. The stratagem is permissible only to prevent a right from becoming obligatory, not to cancel an existing obligation. Additionally, al-Mabsut reports that even after obligation, if no harm is intended, some stratagems may be allowed according to Abu Yusuf, while Muhammad still dislikes them. The overall principle is that stratagems that do not cause harm are permissible in fatwa but not necessarily in religious piety (diraya).

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf‘a): The right of pre-emption; a legal right of a co-owner or neighbour to purchase a property that has been sold to a third party, by paying the price to the buyer.
حيلة (hila (pl. hiyal)): A legal stratagem or device; a way to achieve a legitimate result indirectly through a permissible means, while avoiding an obligation or prohibition.
كراهة (karahah): Dislike; a legal category between permissible and prohibited. In this passage, it refers to tanzihi dislike (makruh tanzihi) rather than tahrimi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on using a stratagem to avoid the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) before it becomes established?
A) It is disliked (makruh) according to all Hanafi imams.
B) It is not disliked, following the view of Abu Yusuf.
C) It is forbidden (haram) because it impedes a lawful right.
D) It is permissible only if the buyer does not intend harm, but disliked if he does.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits stratagems to prevent the obligation of shuf'a, but not zakat; this distinction reflects the different nature of rights (haqq al-'abd vs haqq Allah).

Death of the Preemptor and the Inheritance of Shuf'ah
Ruling on death of shafi' vs death of purchaser

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that shuf'ah is not inherited upon the death of the preemptor and explain the legal reasoning based on the nature of the right as a mere option.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is a personal option, not an established right; it ends with the preemptor's death.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) allows a neighbor or co-owner to step in after a sale. But what happens if the preemptor dies before exercising this right? The Hanafi school draws a sharp distinction between the death of the preemptor and the death of the purchaser. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its rational proof, and the opposing Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two scenarios
The passage compares two deaths: the death of the shafi' (preemptor) and the death of the purchaser. For each, determine whether the right of shuf'ah survives.
The ruling is clear: death of shafi' voids shuf'ah; death of purchaser does not.
2
State the Hanafi position on inheritance
The Hanafi position is that shuf'ah is not inherited. It becomes null upon the shafi''s death. This is based on the classification of shuf'ah as a non-established right (ghayr mutaqarrir).
Contrast with established rights like qisas or ownership, which are inherited.
3
Trace the proof: why shuf'ah is a mere option
The passage argues that shuf'ah is akin to khiyar (option) – it is the preemptor's choice to take or leave. Options are extinguished by death. The cause (sabab) of shuf'ah is the preemptor's ownership, which ends at death.
The analogy with khiyar al-shart is explicitly drawn: it too is not inherited in the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-argument
Al-Shafi'i holds that shuf'ah is inheritable because rights are inherited like property, and the heir has the same need. The Hanafi response distinguishes: shuf'ah is not property but option, and options do not survive death.
The dispute is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different views of the nature of rights.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi law, if the preemptor dies before court judgment, the property remains with the purchaser. Under Shafi'i, the heirs may claim it. This affects inheritance planning and litigation.
The ruling applies only if death occurs before final judgment; after judgment, the right is established and passes to heirs.

[٢] (1) خلافا للشافعي ؛ لأنها ليست بمال، وهذا إذا مات بعد البيع قبل القضاء ، أما إذا مات بعد قضاء القاضي قبل نقدِ الثَّمن أو بعده تصير للورثة. [1]قوله : خلافاً للشافعي ... الخ ؛ قال في «الهداية»(۳) : وقال الشافعي : يورث عنه ، قال في «الكفاية) في توجيهه : فإنّ عنده كمـا تورث الأملاك تورث الحقوق اللازمة ما يعتاض عنها بالمال، وما لا يعتاضُ في ذلك سواء بطريق أنّ الوارث يقوم مقام المورث، فإنّ حاجة الوارث كحاجة المورّث. ونحن نقول : مجرد الرأي والمشيئة لا يجري فيه الإرث ؛ لأنه لا يبقى بعد موته ليخلف الوارث فيه، والثابتُ له بالشفعة مجرّد المشيئة بين أن يأخذ أو يترك. [٢] قوله : خلافاً للشافعي ... الخ ؛ قال صاحب «الهدايـة : وهـذا نظير الاختلاف في خيار الشرط، وأوضحه صاحب «الكفاية» (٦) : بأنه أي لا يورث خيار الشرط عندنا. وعند الشافعي له يورث، فكذلك في الشفعة، ووجه الإلحاق به ما ذكره في «الإيضاح»: إن الثابت للشفيع حق أن يتملك، فظهر أثر هذا الحق في أن يتخير بين أن يأخذ وبين أن لا يأخذ والإرث لا يجري في الخيار.
And the death of the preemptor (shafi'), not the death of the purchaser. For when the shafi' dies, the pre-emption is nullified and is not inherited from him. Explanation: If the shafi' said: 'I have dropped my pre-emption right in what you bought on condition that you drop your pre-emption right in what I bought,' then the purchaser drops his pre-emption right in what the shafi' bought. Thus it is learned that dropping pre-emption is not dependent on an allowed condition. [1] His statement: 'Nullifies the pre-emption...' because the right of pre-emption is not an established right. It may be said: The right of pre-emption is not an established right in the property such that it is valid to receive compensation for it—like the right of qiṣāṣ, ṭalāq, and ʿitāq in that they are not property, yet receiving compensation for them is valid. We reply, as in al-Hidāyah: unlike qiṣāṣ because it is an established right; and unlike ṭalāq and ʿitāq because there is compensation for ownership in the property. Its analogue is when a free woman is told: 'Choose me for a thousand,' or an impotent man tells his wife: 'Choose to waive the option (khiyār) for a thousand,' and she chooses; the option is nullified and no compensation is established. And guaranty of the person (kafālah bi al-nafs) in this matter is like pre-emption in one narration; in another, the guaranty is not nullified and no wealth is due. It is said: this narration is in pre-emption; and it is said: it is in guaranty specifically, meaning that the guaranty is not nullified by settlement for wealth, but pre-emption is nullified by settlement for wealth. This has been detailed in its place, so ponder. (2) His statement: 'not the death of the purchaser' that is, if the purchaser dies, pre-emption is not nullified because the entitled one remains and the cause of his right has not changed, unlike the death of the shafi', for the cause by which he takes pre-emption is eliminated by his death—and that cause is his ownership. The existence of the cause until the time of taking is a condition; for this reason, if he sold his property before taking the pre-empted property, he would not have the right to take by pre-emption; similarly, if it is eliminated by his death. And what is established for the heir in terms of propinquity (jiwār) and partnership (shirkah) that occurs after the sale does not entitle him to pre-emption. So the house is not sold for the debt of the purchaser nor for his will. If the judge or executor sells it, or the purchaser makes a will concerning it, the shafi' has the right to nullify that and take the house because his right precedes; for this reason he can break his disposal during his lifetime. This is a summary of some glosses of al-Hidāyah (3). [2] (1) Unlike al-Shafiʿi, because it is not wealth; this applies when he dies after the sale before judgment, but if he dies after the judge's judgment before or after payment of the price, it becomes for the heirs. [1] His statement: 'Contrary to al-Shafiʿi...' He said in al-Hidāyah (3): 'Al-Shafiʿi said: It is inherited from him.' In al-Kifāyah in its explanation: For in his view, just as properties are inherited, so are necessary rights for which compensation is given in wealth, and those for which it is not given are equal in that respect, by way that the heir stands in place of the predecessor, for the need of the heir is like the need of the predecessor. And we say: mere opinion and choice do not run in inheritance because it does not remain after his death for the heir to succeed him in it; what is established for him by pre-emption is mere choice between taking or leaving. [2] His statement: 'Contrary to al-Shafiʿi...' The author of al-Hidāyah said: 'This is analogous to the difference of opinion in khiyār al-sharṭ (option of condition).' And the author of al-Kifāyah clarified it: that this, i.e., the khiyār al-sharṭ, is not inherited in our school, while according to al-Shafiʿi it is inherited; similarly in pre-emption. The reason for linking it to that is what was mentioned in al-Īḍāḥ: that the right established for the shafi' is a right to own, and the effect of this right appears in his being able to choose between taking and not taking, and inheritance does not apply to an option (khiyār).
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah deals with the effect of death on the right of pre-emption (shufʿah). The author first states the general rule: if the preemptor (shafiʿ) dies, his right of pre-emption is void and is not inherited; but if the purchaser dies, the right remains. The reason is that shufʿah is a personal right based on the shafiʿ's ownership of an adjacent or shared property. Once the shafiʿ dies, that ownership is lost, and the cause (sabab) of the right disappears. The right does not pass to heirs because it is not an established right (ḥaqq mutaqarrir) like ownership; rather it is a mere option (mashiʾah) to take or leave the property. The commentary then addresses an objection: why can one receive compensation for rights like qiṣāṣ, ṭalāq, and ʿitāq even though they are not property? The answer is that qiṣāṣ is an established right, while ṭalāq and ʿitāq involve compensation for ownership in the property itself—unlike shufʿah. The author provides an analogy with an option given to a free woman or an impotent man, where the option is voided without compensation. Another narration compares shufʿah to guaranty (kafālah bi al-nafs), but the analysis shows that shufʿah is voided by a settlement for wealth while guaranty is not. The passage then explains the difference between death of the shafiʿ and death of the purchaser: in the latter, the cause (the shafiʿ's ownership) remains, so the right continues. If the shafiʿ dies after judgment but before payment, the right becomes effective for his heirs because the judgment has transformed the right into ownership. The text then presents the Shafiʿi view that shufʿah is inherited, and the Hanafi rebuttal: shufʿah is not like property but like an option (khiyār), which is not inheritable. The analogy used is the difference over khiyār al-sharṭ: in the Hanafi school, this option is not inherited; the Shafiʿi school holds it is inherited. The Hanafi reasoning is that inheritance applies to rights that remain after death, but an option is extinguished by death. The passage thus clarifies a key Hanafi principle: rights based purely on choice or will do not transfer to heirs.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shufʿah): The right of pre-emption; a right granted to a co-owner or neighbor to take over a sold property from the purchaser by paying the price.
حق متقرر (ḥaqq mutaqarrir): An established right, i.e., one that is fixed and stable in the property, like ownership or the right to qiṣāṣ.
المشيئة (al-mashi'ah): The option or will to choose; the right of the preemptor to either take or leave the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the right of shuf'ah when the preemptor (shafi') dies before judgment?
A) The right passes to his heirs.
B) The right becomes void and is not inherited.
C) The right transfers to the purchaser.
D) The right is transformed into a debt on the purchaser.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is not inherited because it is a mere option, not an established right; only after court judgment does it become inheritable property.

Selling the Entitled Property Before Judgment Voids Pre-emption
Ruling on pre-emption being voided by prior sale of the shafi's own property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the entitled property before judgment and identify the exception when a conditional sale preserves the right.

Core Idea
Selling the property that entitles you to pre-empt before judgment voids the right — unless the sale includes an option to cancel.

This passage addresses a critical timing issue in shuf'ah: the shafi's own property must remain in his ownership until the judge grants the pre-empted property. If he sells it earlier, the very cause of his right disappears. However, there is a nuance: if the sale includes a khiyār (option) that allows him to rescind, the right persists because ownership is not definitively transferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The shafi' sells his own property that gives him the right to pre-empt. The ruling: his shuf'ah right becomes void.
This is the basic rule.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The text gives a rational proof: 'because the cause of entitlement ceases before ownership' (lizawāl sabab al-istiḥqāq qabl al-tamalluk). The cause is ownership of the adjacent property.
No direct primary text is cited; it is a rational deduction.
3
Trace the legal reasoning
Ownership of the entitled property is the 'illa (effective cause) for shuf'ah. It must continue until the moment of acquiring the pre-empted property. Selling severs that link.
This is a classic example of cause-and-effect in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Note the exception
If the sale includes a condition of khiyār (option), the shafi's ownership is not definitively lost, so the right continues.
The key is whether ownership is permanently transferred.
5
Apply the principle
Any act that permanently transfers ownership of the entitled property before judgment voids shuf'ah. Temporary transfers (e.g., with option, lease with right to reclaim?) do not.
This principle governs other similar scenarios in shuf'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع ما يشفع به قَبْلَ القضاء بها) ؛ لزوال سبب الاستحقاق قبل التملك بخلاف ما إذا كان البيع بشرط الخيار. [٣] قوله : ويبيع ما يشفع به...الخ ؛ يعني وإذا باع الشفيع بالشفع به قبل أن يقضي له بالشفعة بطلت شفعته ؛ لأنّ بقاء ما يشفع به شرط إلى وقت القضاء بالشفعة، وانتفاء
And selling what gives the right to pre-emption before judgment—because the cause of entitlement ceases before ownership, unlike the case when the sale is with an option (khiyār). [3] His statement: 'And he sells what gives the right to pre-emption...' meaning, if the shafi' sells the property that gives him the right to pre-emption before the judge has adjudicated the pre-emption for him, his pre-emption is void because the continued existence of the property that gives the right to pre-emption is a condition until the time of adjudication, and it is non-existent.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage discusses a second scenario affecting the right to shuf'ah: the shafi' sells his own property that originally gave him the right to pre-empt. The ruling is that if he sells that property before the judge grants him the pre-empted property, his right to shuf'ah is void. The reason is that the cause (sabab) of shuf'ah—ownership of an adjacent or shared property—must continue until the moment of taking possession. If that ownership is transferred by sale, the cause no longer exists, so the right falls. An exception is made if the sale includes a condition (khiyār) that allows the shafi' to regain ownership; in that case, his right remains because his ownership is not definitively lost. The commentary from the text explains that the existence of the property giving the right to pre-emption is a condition until the time of judgment; if it is sold, the condition is broken and the right is nullified.

Key Terms
ما يشفع به (mā yushfaʿu bihi): The property that gives the right to pre-emption (the shafi's own adjacent or shared property).
القضاء (al-qaḍāʾ): The judgment of a judge granting the right of pre-emption or the final decree.
شرط الخيار (sharṭ al-khiyār): A condition in a sale that allows one party to cancel the sale within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, if the preemptor (shafi') sells his own property that entitles him to shuf'ah before the judge rules on his claim, what happens to his right of pre-emption?
A) It remains intact because he already had the right.
B) It is voided because the cause of the right has ceased.
C) It transfers to the buyer of that property.
D) It becomes a debt against the purchaser of the pre-empted property.

Key Takeaway
The shafi's right to pre-emption is voided if he sells the property that entitles him to it before the judge's decision, unless the sale includes an option that preserves provisional ownership.

Effect of Price Misinformation on Shuf'a: Mithli vs 'Arad
Explaining when shafi' retains right despite price/type discrepancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a shafi' who consents based on inaccurate price information retains his right of pre-emption when the actual price is a mithli (fungible) of equal or lesser value, but loses it when it is 'arad (non-fungible goods) of equal or greater value.

Core Idea
Consent to an assumed price revokes shuf'a only if the reality matches; when the price type or amount eases the burden, the right revives.

In the law of pre-emption (shuf'a), a co-owner or neighbour who waives his right after being told the sale price may later discover the reality was different. The Hanafi school, as detailed in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, draws a clear distinction between cases where the actual price is a fungible good (mithli) and where it is a unique commodity ('arad). This lesson unpacks the rule and its rationale (istihsan), enabling you to predict when the right of shuf'a survives despite initial consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling scenarios
The passage presents two main cases: (1) the shafi' is told the price is a thousand dirhams but it is less, or it is wheat/barley worth a thousand or more – he retains shuf'a. (2) The price is 'arad (e.g., clothes, slaves) worth a thousand or more – he loses shuf'a. List the conditions that trigger each outcome.
Focus on the type of price (mithli vs. qimi) and its value relative to the reported amount.
2
Extract the 'illa (legal cause)
The author states: 'because his consent was based on considering the price high or the commodity type inaccessible'. When the reality is easier (lower price, or mithli he can pay in kind), his consent is presumed conditional. This is the foundational reasoning (ta'lil).
The 'illa is the shafi's supposed motivation; when it vanishes, so does the binding effect of his consent.
3
Apply the distinction between mithli and 'arad
For mithli (weighed, measured, or counted fungibles), the shafi' can take the property by paying the equivalent in kind, which may be easier for him. For 'arad, the obligation is to pay the value (qimah) in cash, so no ease is apparent. Hence, if the actual price is 'arad of equal or greater value, the right is lost.
This distinction is central to the Hanafi position; other schools may not recognise it.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
The passage mentions al-Karkhi's view on the case where the reported price is 1000 dirhams but it is actually 100 dinars. If the dinars are worth 1000 dirhams or more, consent is valid (no shuf'a). Shaykh al-Islam holds this view, but other early authorities differ. Note the practical consequence.
This sub-rule shows that the value equivalence matters when the currency type changes.
5
Evaluate the practical outcome
In all cases, the ruling aims to prevent the shafi' from being forced into an unfavourable exchange. If the actual price is easier (less cash, or mithli he can easily provide), he may retract; if it is harder or equivalent in value, he cannot. Confirm the mufta bihi position.
The tarjih leans toward the general principle from al-Mabsut and al-Idah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أو بيعه بألف فسلم.... الخ ؛ توضيحه على ما في «تكملة البحر») : فإن قيل للشفيع : إنّها بيعت بألف فسلّم، ثمّ عَلِمَ أنها بيعت بأقل أو ببر أو شعير قيمته ألف ، أو أكثر فله الشفعة ؛ لأنّ تسليمه كان لاستكثار الثمن أو لتعذر الجنس ظاهراً. فإذا تبين خلافه كان له الأخذ للتيسير وعدم الرضا، على تقدير أن الثمن غيره ؛ لأن الرغبة في الأخذ تختلف باختلاف الثمن قدراً وجنساً، فإذا سلم على بعض الوجوه لا يلزم منه التسليم في الوجوه كلها. وكذا كل موزون أو مكيل أو عددي متفاوت، وكذا لو أخبر أن الثمن عروض كالثياب والعبيد فظهر أنه مكيل أو موزون ، أو أخبر أن الثمن مكيل أو موزون فظهر من خلاف جنسه من المكيل والموزون ، فهو على شفعته لما ذكرنا. بخلاف ما إذا علم أنها بيعت بعروض قيمتها ألف أو أكثر، كما أشار إليه بقوله : وبعرض كذلك لا ؛ لأنَّ الواجب فيه القيمة، وهي دراهم أو دنانير، فلا يظهر فيه التيسير ، فلا يكون له الأخذ وإن ظهر أنه جنس آخر من العروض قيمته مثل قيمة الذي بلغه ، أو ظهر أنه ذهب أو فضة قدره مثلُ قيمة ذلك، فلا شفعة له بعدم الفائدة ؛ لأن في غير المكيل والموزون الواجب القيمة فلا يظهر التفاوت. His statement: "Or if it was sold for a thousand and he consented..." etc.; its clarification as in 'Takmilat al-Bahr': If the shafi' is told: It was sold for a thousand, and he consented, then he learned that it was sold for less, or for wheat or barley worth a thousand or more, then he has the right of pre-emption (shuf'a); because his consent was based on considering the price high or the commodity type inaccessible in appearance. When the contrary becomes clear, he may take (the property) for ease and due to lack of consent, assuming the price is different; because desire to take differs by difference in price in amount and type. So if he consented in some respects, that does not necessitate consent in all respects. Likewise every weighed, measured, or counted (item) that varies, and likewise if he was told the price was goods like clothes or slaves, and it turned out to be measured or weighed; or he was told the price was measured or weighed, and it turned out to be of a different type of measured or weighed items, he retains his shuf'a for what we mentioned. Contrary to if he knew it was sold for goods worth a thousand or more, as he indicated by his statement: "And with goods similarly no." Because the obligation (in such a case) is the value, which is dirhams or dinars, so ease is not apparent. Therefore he may not take even if it turns out to be another type of goods whose value equals the value of what was reported to him, or it turns out to be gold or silver of equivalent value; so no shuf'a due to lack of benefit. Contrary to if it appears that the sale was for goods worth a thousand or more, then he retains no shuf'a; because the shafi' here takes by value. If its value is a thousand, he has consented to the sale with it; and if its value is more, then consenting to the sale for a thousand is consenting to selling the property for more by primary inference. And he may take the share of one of the buyers, not one of the sellers: meaning if a group bought from one person, the shafi' may take the share of one of them, but if a group sold to one person, he may not take the share of one seller and leave the rest. Rather if he wishes he takes all; because here (case of multiple buyers) the transaction is divided among the buyers, while there it is not divided. Also in the first case the harm to the neighbor is repelled, not in the second. And the half is partitioned if an undivided half of a house is sold and they partition: meaning he bought an undivided half of a house, then the seller and buyer partitioned, so the shafi' takes the half as a separate portion; because partition completes possession. This is as if he was told the price is a thousand and it turned out less. And if he was told the price was a thousand dirhams and he consented, and it turned out to be one hundred dinars. This is not mentioned in al-Asl, and al-Karkhi mentioned it: Consider if the value of the dinars is a thousand dirhams or more, the consent is valid, and it is the saying of Shaykh al-Islam. Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary (sharh) on the text of al-Wiqaya, dealing with the law of pre-emption (shuf'a). The author explains a nuanced rule: if a person entitled to pre-emption (shafi') is informed that a property was sold for a certain price (e.g., one thousand) and he consents (i.e., waives his right or accepts the sale), but later discovers that the actual price was lower, or that the price was in kind (like wheat or barley) worth a thousand or more, the shafi' retains his right to claim the property. The reason ('illa) is that his consent was based on an assumption that the price was high or that the type of price (cash vs. commodity) was difficult for him. When reality differs, he may take the property because his initial willingness is presumed to be conditional. This applies to all fungible goods (mithliyyat) that are weighed, measured, or counted with variations. Similarly, if he was told the price was goods (e.g., clothes or slaves) and it turns out to be measured or weighed (a more difficult commodity for him to pay), he keeps the right. Conversely, if he learns that the price was actually goods worth a thousand or more, he loses the right because the obligation in such cases is the value (qimah) in dirhams or dinars, and no ease is apparent. Even if the goods are of a different type but equal or greater value, there is no shuf'a because the shafi' is only entitled to the value, and the property's value remains the same. The commentator also resolves an objection: the restriction 'its value a thousand or more' is beneficial because it clarifies that the ruling applies even if the value is more, but by even stronger reason if it is less. The passage then covers two additional rules: first, if multiple buyers purchased from one seller, the shafi' may take the share of just one buyer; but if multiple sellers sold to one buyer, the shafi' cannot take the share of one seller only—he must take all or none. Second, if an undivided half of a house is sold and then the seller and buyer partition, the shafi' takes the half as a separate portion because partition is considered completion of possession. Finally, an example from al-Muhit: if the reported price was a male slave but it turns out to be a female slave, the shafi's right is void if her value is equal or more, but remains if it is less (like the case of monetary price). And if the price reported was 1000 dirhams but it is actually 100 dinars, al-Karkhi says: if the value of the dinars is 1000 dirhams or more, the consent is valid (i.e., no shuf'a).

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption whereby a co-owner or neighbour can purchase a sold share from the buyer at the sale price.
مثلي (mithlī): A fungible good that can be replaced by an equivalent in terms of quantity and quality, such as weighed/measured items.
عرض ('arad): Commodity goods (non-money) like clothes, slaves, animals, which have subjective value (qimi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A shafi' is told the property was sold for 1000 dirhams in cash. He waives. Later he finds the actual price was a plot of land worth 1000 dirhams (an 'arad). According to the passage, what is the ruling?
A) He may claim the property because the price is different.
B) He loses shuf'a because the obligation is the value, not the specific land.
C) He may claim the property only if the land is worth less than 1000 dirhams.
D) He loses shuf'a because he already waived.

Key Takeaway
The shafi''s right of pre-emption revives when the actual price is a mithli of lesser or equal value, but is lost when it is 'arad of equal or greater value, because the former allows payment in kind (ease) while the latter forces valuation.

Regulation of Qassam Fees and Validity of Consensual Partition
Explaining the ruling that qassam should not form a partnership and partition is valid with consent except when a minor is involved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits qassam from sharing fees and state the conditions under which a consensual partition is valid and binding.

Core Idea
A consensual partition is valid but not binding until ratified by the judge, especially when a minor or absentee is involved.

This lesson examines two interconnected rules from the Hanafi fiqh of partition: the prohibition on qassam (official dividers) colluding on fees, and the validity but non-binding nature of consensual partition when a minor or absentee is involved. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying the school’s position in property division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings stated in the passage.
The passage presents two rulings: (1) qassam must not share fees, and (2) partition by consent is valid except when one co-owner is a minor.
2
Understand the rationale for prohibiting qassam from sharing fees.
If qassam collude, they will charge higher fees; competition keeps fees low. The judge prevents their partnership.
This is a public interest (maslaha) ruling to avoid hardship.
3
Distinguish between consensual partition and compulsory partition.
Consensual partition (qisma tarādiyya) is valid with all co-owners’ consent, but not binding until adjudicated if a minor or absentee is present. Compulsory partition (qisma jabbariyya) is ordered by the judge.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on ratification after death.
If a minor or absentee dies and their heirs ratify the partition, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold it effective (istihsan), while Muhammad says it is ineffective (qiyas). The relied-upon view is istihsan.
The dispute hinges on whether a non-binding act can be made binding by subsequent ratification.
5
Apply the mufta bihi ruling to a practical scenario.
A judge should follow the istihsan view: heirs’ ratification makes the partition binding, providing finality.

[1] His saying: 'And the qassam should not share' etc.; al-qassam with damma on qaf and shadda on sin, plural of qasim, meaning the judge should not leave them to combine (their efforts) so that the fee does not become high. Explanation: the judge does not leave the qassam to themselves on their own agreement to share in such a way that the matter of partition does not go beyond them to others. Rather, the judge says to each of them: 'You are appointed for partition without the participation of the other,' so each of them is authorized and permitted for partition from the side of the judge. Then each qassam vies for whatever fee is easier, so the shared properties are divided at the cheapest fees so that that fee is not lost. The upshot: the imam does not leave the qassam to share in the fee, but commands each to do it alone, otherwise they might agree on an excessive fee. So in Al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyya. Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the regulation of qassam (professional property surveyors/divider) and the validity of consensual partition. The author begins by stating two rulings: (1) qassam must not form a partnership or share fees among themselves, and (2) partition by consent of the co-owners is valid except when one of them is a minor. The commentary explains that the purpose is to prevent excessive fees; if qassam collude, they will charge higher fees, but if each competes independently, fees remain low. The judge is instructed to appoint each qassam individually. The second ruling specifies that partition with consent is valid (sahih) but not binding (lazim) unless the judge ratifies it, especially if one party is a minor. The text then delves into an ikhtilaf: if a minor or absentee dies and their heirs ratify the partition, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold it effective (istihsan), while Muhammad considers it ineffective (qiyas). The commentary also notes that ratification can be explicit by statement or implicit by action (e.g., selling one's portion). The relied-upon position is istihsan, favoring effectiveness.

Key Terms
قسام (qassam): The plural of qasim; professional surveyors or dividers appointed by the judge to partition shared property.
قسمة (qisma): Partition: the division of jointly owned property among co-owners.
إجازة (ijāza): Ratification: subsequent approval that validates a previously non-binding transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the judge prefers to appoint only one qassam
D) Because it causes delays in partition

Key Takeaway
The qassam must not collude on fees to keep costs low, and consensual partition is valid but not binding until ratified by the judge if a minor or absentee is among the co-owners.

Division of Inherited Immovable Property: Proof Required?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditions for partition of inherited immovable property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on dividing inherited immovable property and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the requirement of proof.

Core Idea
Inherited land requires proof of death and heir count; without it, Abu Hanifa forbids division.

When co-owners seek partition of a property, the type of property and the basis of their claim affect the evidentiary requirements. For movable goods, division is straightforward regardless of the claim. But for immovable property claimed as inheritance, a critical difference arises within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa insists on proof of the deceased's death and the number of heirs, while his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, take a more lenient view. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property types and claim categories
The passage distinguishes between movable (manqul) and immovable (aqar) property, and between claims of purchase/absolute ownership and claims of inheritance from a named person.
For movable property, division is always permitted. For immovable, only inheritance claims trigger extra requirements.
2
State the rule for immovable inheritance according to Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa requires proof of the death of the deceased and proof of the number of heirs before dividing the property.
The text explicitly says: 'لا يقسم حتى يبرهنوا على الموت وعدد الورثة' – 'it is not divided until they prove death and number of heirs.'
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) appear to allow division without such proof, as implied by the phrase 'عندهما' in the discussion on ratification and by the overall context of the dispute.
Their reasoning: partition does not adjudicate against others; it merely separates existing joint ownership.
4
Analyze the rationale (ta'lil) for the difference
Abu Hanifa's caution stems from the possibility of unknown heirs who might be prejudiced by the division. The Sahibayn consider partition a non-adversarial proceeding that does not bind absent persons.
This is a classic ikhtilaf rooted in the nature of qisma (partition) – whether it involves qada' (adjudication) against third parties.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
Despite the Sahibayn's more lenient view, the relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's stricter requirement, due to the greater caution it affords unknown rights.
This is consistent with later Hanafi practice: in matters of inheritance, the safer approach is preferred.

Source Text

لأنهم ما أقروا بالملك لغيرهم ، قال في «الهداية»: هذه رواية «كتاب القسمة». وفي «الجامع الصغير»: أرض ادّعاها ،رجلان وأقاما البينة أنها في أيديهما، وأراد
القسمة لم يقسمها حتى يقيما البينة أنها لهما ؛ لاحتمال أن تكون لغيرهما.
(And a division of) movable (property) which they claim as inheritance among them, and immovable (property) which they claim by purchase or ownership absolutely: if they claim it as inheritance from Zayd, it is not (divided) until they prove his death and the number of his heirs according to Abu Hanifa. (And a division of) movable which they claim as inheritance among them, and immovable which they claim by purchase or ownership absolutely: if they claim it as inheritance from Zayd, it is not divided until they prove his death and number of his heirs according to Abu Hanifa. [1] A group appeared before the judge and requested the division of what is in their hands. If it is movable, and they claim purchase or absolute ownership, it is divided – but this is not mentioned in the matn. If they claim inheritance from Zayd, it is also divided. If it is immovable, and they claim purchase or absolute ownership, it is also divided. However, if they claim inheritance from Zayd, it is not divided according to Abu Hanifa until they prove death and number of heirs. And he intended by validity bindingness; no deputy or absent without agent, because it is not binding then except by the judge's ratification. Apparently, it reverts to the three exceptions, or the absent or the minor when he reaches majority. If the absent or minor dies and his heirs ratify, it is effective according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), contrary to Muhammad. The first is istihsan, the second is qiyas. And just as ratification is established explicitly by speech, it is established implicitly by action, like sale. As in al-Tatarkhaniyya. In al-Minhaj from al-Jawhara: A child and an adult divided something, then the child reached majority and disposed of his share, and sold some – that constitutes ratification, or his guardian. This is for his heirs: i.e., its bindingness by the judge's ratification and the like if they were partners in inheritance; if partners in something else, it is invalid, and its implication is that it is not executed by ratification, so it requires contemplation. [1] His saying: 'And a division of movable which they claim as inheritance among them' etc.; meaning if the partners appear before the judge and they have a shared property, then there are several images: one of them is if it is movable and they claim purchase or absolute ownership – this image is not mentioned in the matn, and the complete speech is mentioned in the commentaries and glosses. [2] His saying: 'It is divided also' etc.; i.e., among them, because in partition there is no adjudication against others, since they did not acknowledge ownership for others. He said in al-Hidayah: This is the narration of Kitab al-Qisma. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: A land claimed by two men, and they established evidence that it was in their possession, and they wanted division, he would not divide it until they established evidence that it belongs to them, because it might belong to others.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the conditions for dividing property among co-claimants depending on the nature of the property and the basis of their claim. The author distinguishes between movable (manqul) and immovable (aqar) property. For movable property, regardless of whether they claim purchase, absolute ownership, or inheritance, division is allowed because there is no possibility of affecting a third party's rights. For immovable property, if they claim purchase or absolute ownership, division is also allowed. However, if they claim inheritance from a specific person (e.g., Zayd), Abu Hanifa requires proof of the death of the deceased and the number of heirs before division. This is because inheritance claims may involve unknown heirs, and division could wrongfully exclude them. The commentary elaborates that this is from Kitab al-Qisma (not the well-known narration in al-Jami' al-Saghir, where stricter proof is required). It also links back to the earlier discussion on ratification: if a minor or absentee was involved, and they die, their heirs' ratification is effective according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (istihsan) but not according to Muhammad (qiyas). The passage also notes that ratification can be by action (e.g., selling a share) as well as by speech. The relied-upon position is the more lenient one for movable property and the stricter one for immovable inheritance claims.

Key Terms
نقلي (manqul): Movable property; goods that can be transported.
عقار (aqar): Immovable property; land and real estate.
إرث (irth): Inheritance; the property inherited from a deceased person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only proof of the claimant's ownership
B) Proof of the death of Zayd and the number of his heirs
C) Proof that the property was not purchased
D) Approval from the judge only

Key Takeaway
For inherited immovable property, Abu Hanifa's precaution requires proof of death and heirs; this is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Division of Multiple Houses: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on division of multiple houses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on dividing multiple houses in one city and evaluate the practical difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions.

Core Idea
Houses differ by location; Abu Hanifa says separate; Sahibayn allow cross-division if beneficial.

When co-owners of multiple houses cannot agree on division, a judge must enforce a fair method. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement: Abu Hanifa treats each house as a separate genre due to location-based value differences, while his two disciples consider them the same genre in one city, permitting cross-division to avoid small shares. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
The scenario involves two or more houses owned jointly, all located in the same city (misr wahid). The co-owners cannot agree on how to divide them, so a judge must order compulsory division (qisma jabriyya).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules that each house must be divided separately. He sees houses as different 'genres' (ajnas) because location, neighbors, and proximity to the mosque cause gross disparity (fahish) in value, making fair cross-equation impossible without mutual consent.
His proof is based on the substantial difference in purposes (agrad).
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani allow dividing some houses in exchange for others within the same city. They consider houses one genre due to the same name and form, and argue that cross-division may be more beneficial (aslah), preventing a partner from receiving a very small share.
They agree with Abu Hanifa if houses are in different cities.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, a judge must physically partition each house among co-owners. Under the Sahibayn, the judge could assign entire houses to some partners and compensate others with cash or other houses, provided it is more beneficial.
This difference affects the ease and fairness of division.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider two partners jointly owning three houses of very different values. Apply each view to determine the judge's order.
See practice tasks for a concrete exercise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو دار وحانوت قُسِمَ كُلِّ وحدَها) : أي إذا كانت الدور قريبة بأن كانت كلها في مصر واحد، قسم كل وحدَها عند أبي حنيفة ، وقالا : يقسم بعضُها في بعض، وإن كانت الدور بعيدة : أي في مصرين، فقولهما كقول أبي حنيفة له. [١] قوله : عند أبي حنيفة الله ؛ له : إنّ الدور أجناس مختلفة ؛ لاختلاف المقصودِ باعتبار المحال والجيران والقرب من المسجد، فكان اختلافاً فاحشاً، فلا يمكن التعديل في القسمة، فلا يجوز جمع نصيب كلِّ واحدٍ في دار إلا بالتراضي، والساحة كالدور؛ لأنه بين البيت والدار، فأخذ حظه من كلِّ واحدٍ منهما كذا. في تكملة البحر). [٢] قوله : وقالا : يقسم بعضها في بعض ؛ إذا كانت في مصر واحد، وكانت القسمة أصلح لهم ؛ لأنهم جنس واحد ، نظراً إلى اتحاد الاسم والصورة، وأصلُ السكني جنسان؛ نظراً إلى اختلافِ الأغراض، وتفاوت السكني، وإذا قسم كلّ دارٍ على حدة ربّما يتضرّر لقلة نصيبه . كذا في تكملة البحر
Or a house and a shop, each is divided separately: i.e., if the houses are close, all being in one city, each is divided separately according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: some of them can be divided in exchange for others. If the houses are far, i.e., in two cities, then their opinion is like the opinion of Abu Hanifa. [1] His saying: according to Abu Hanifa; his proof: houses are different genres due to difference in purpose considering locations, neighbors, proximity to the mosque, so there is a gross difference, thus equalization in division is not possible, so combining everyone's share in one house is not allowed except by mutual consent. The courtyard is like houses because it is between the house and the dwelling, so each takes his share from each of them. This is from Takmilat al-Bahr. [2] His saying: and they said: some can be divided in exchange for others when they are in one city, and the division is more beneficial for them because they are one genre, looking at the unity of name and form. The origin of dwelling is two types considering difference of purposes and disparity of dwelling. If each house is divided separately, he might be harmed due to the smallness of his share. This is from Takmilat al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the compulsory division of multiple houses (or a house and a shop) owned jointly. The base text (matn) states: 'Or a house and a shop, each is divided separately.' The sharh explains that if the houses are all in one city (misr wahid), Abu Hanifa rules that each house must be divided independently. His reasoning is that houses are different 'genres' (ajnas) because their value varies significantly with location, neighbors, and proximity to the mosque. Such disparity is gross (fahish), making it impossible to achieve a fair division by assigning a partner's entire share into one house. Therefore, each house must be partitioned separately, and cross-division (e.g., giving one partner a larger share in one house and another partner a share in another house) is only permissible with mutual consent. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, known as al-Sahibayn) disagree: they rule that if the houses are in the same city, some houses can be exchanged for others in the compulsory division, because houses are of one genre considering the unity of name and form. They argue that such cross-division may be more beneficial (aslah) for the partners, as separately dividing each house could leave a partner with a very small share. However, both parties agree that if the houses are in different cities (misrayn), the Sahibayn's opinion aligns with Abu Hanifa—each house must be divided separately. The hashya from Takmilat al-Bahr elaborates the proofs, noting that Abu Hanifa focuses on the substantial difference in purposes (ikhtilaf al-maqasid) and dwellings, while the Sahibayn emphasize the unified name and appearance.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qisma): Division of jointly owned property among co-owners, here specifically compulsory division (qisma jabriyya) by a judge.
الدور (al-dūr): Houses or residential buildings; considered here as items subject to division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which condition do the Sahibayn agree with Abu Hanifa that each house must be divided separately?
A) When the houses are in the same city but far apart
B) When the houses are in different cities
C) When the houses are of different sizes
D) When the partners request a cross-division

Key Takeaway
In dividing multiple houses, Abu Hanifa insists on separate division due to value disparities; the Sahibayn allow cross-division when beneficial.

Procedure of Partition: Labeling and Casting Lots
Ruling statement with commentary on partition procedure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe and apply the Hanafi procedure for partitioning jointly owned property by labeling sections and casting lots, explaining the role and steps of the surveyor.

Core Idea
Partition of joint property is finalized by casting lots to assign labeled shares, ensuring fairness without requiring mutual agreement on assignment.

When partners in a joint property cannot agree on who gets which portion, Islamic law provides a practical remedy: partition by a court-appointed surveyor who measures, evaluates, labels, and then allocates shares by lottery. This lesson unpacks the step‑by‑step procedure as outlined in the classical Hanafi texts, showing how equity is achieved through a combination of precise measurement and divinely guided chance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the labeling system
Recognize that the surveyor designates portions as 'first,' 'second,' 'third' (e.g., starting from the western side and moving eastward). This creates a sequential order that will later correspond to the lottery outcome.
2
Understand the surveyor's preparation
The surveyor draws the property on paper, measures every cubit (represented by a grid square like a brick), evaluates buildings and courtyards, and adjusts the plan to ensure shares are equal in value.
3
Explain the lottery mechanism
Names of the partners are written on slips of paper (or otherwise) and lots are cast. The order in which names are drawn determines the order of choosing shares.
This mechanism resolves disagreement without bias.
4
Apply the assignment rule
The partner whose name is drawn first receives his entire share from the first labeled section (e.g., the western side). The second name drawn receives his share adjacent to the first, and so on, until all partners are served.
This ensures continuity of the allocated portions.
5
Recognize the flexibility
The partition may start from any side chosen by the surveyor, and shares may be equal or unequal (as per ownership percentages). The same sequential method applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القسمة تعريفها وأحكامها
ويلقب الأقسام بالأول والثاني والثالث ، ويكتب أسماءهم ويقرع ، والأول لمن خرج اسمه أولاً ، والثاني لمن خرج ثانياً
[1]
ويلقب الأقسام بالأول والثاني والثالث ، ويكتب أسماءهم ويقرع ، والأول لمن خرج اسمه أولاً ، والثاني لمن خرج ثانياً : أي يُصَوِّرُ الدَّارَ المقسومة على قرطاس ؛ ليرفع إلى القاضي، ويعدلها : أي يسويها على سهام القسمة، ويذرعُها ويُصَوِّرُ الدرعان على ذلك القرطاس بقلم الجدول، فيكون كل ذراع في ذراع بشكل لبنة ، ويُقَدِّرُ البيوت والصَّفْةَ ، وغيرهما بتلك الدرعان، ويقوم البناءً ويبتدئ القسمة من أي طرف شاء، فإن جَعَلَ الجانب الغربي أولاً يجعل ما يليه ثانياً، ثمّ ما يليه ثالثاً وهكذا، ويكتب أسماء أصحاب السهام إما على القرعة أو غيرها، فمن خرج اسمُهُ أولاً يعطى نصيبه من الجانب الغربي جملة من العرصة والبناء إلى أن يتم نصيبُهُ ، ثُمَّ مَن خَرَجَ اسمه ثانيا يعطى نصيبه متصلاً بالأول، وهكذا إلى أن يتم سواء كانت الأنصباء متساوية أو متفاوتة.
رواه
Book of Partition: Its Definition and Rulings. And he labels the sections as first, second, and third, and writes their names and casts lots. The first (share) is for the one whose name comes out first, and the second for the one whose name comes out second. [Commentary]: i.e., he draws the divided house on a paper to submit to the judge, and adjusts it: i.e., makes it equal according to the shares of partition, and measures it, and draws the measurements on that paper with a grid pen, so that each cubit by a cubit is in the shape of a brick. And he estimates the rooms and the portico, etc., with those measurements, and evaluates the building, and begins partition from any side he wishes. If he makes the western side first, he makes what is adjacent to it second, then what is adjacent to it third, and so on. And he writes the names of the shareholders either by lottery or otherwise. So the one whose name comes out first is given his share from the western side entirely, from the courtyard and building until his share is complete. Then the one whose name comes out second is given his share adjacent to the first, and so on until completion, whether the shares are equal or unequal. Narrated/transmitted?
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on partition (qisma). The matn states that shares are labeled first, second, third, etc., and names are written and lots are cast. The first lot determines the first share, etc. The commentary expands on the procedure: The surveyor draws the property on paper, marks dimensions using a grid where each square represents a cubit by a cubit like a brick, evaluates the buildings, and starts partition from any side. For example, if the western side is designated first, the adjacent section becomes second, etc. The names of the shareholders are written on lots, and the first drawn gets his entire share from the western side, the second gets his share adjacent to the first, until all shares are assigned, whether equal or unequal. This ensures a precise and equitable division. The note 'رواه' at the end may indicate a transmission variant, perhaps from a different source.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qisma): Partition of jointly owned property into separate shares
القرعة (al-qur'a): Casting lots to determine the order of choosing shares
العرصة (al-arsa): Courtyard or open space of a house

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the partition of joint property is executed by labeling equal‑value sections and then assigning them through a binding lottery, ensuring fairness and finality.

Inclusion of Dirhams in Partition Requires Consent
States the ruling and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on cash in partition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on including cash in partition, explain the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, and identify when cash compensation is permitted under Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Cash cannot be introduced into a partition without consent; in-kind compensation is preferred, cash only as a last resort.

This passage from Al-Hidayah and its commentary addresses a practical problem in inheritance and partnership: how to handle an imbalance when dividing real estate, especially when one portion has more building than another. The default rule is that no outside cash may be brought into the partition, but the three leading Hanafi imams differ on the proper method of equalization. Understanding their reasoning helps us apply the maxim that partition is a right of partnership, and cash should only be used when necessary or with mutual consent.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the key statement in the matn: '... ولا يُدْخِلُ الدّراهمَ في القسمة إلا برضاهم' (dirhams are not included in partition except with their consent). Note that this applies to real estate and dirhams from outside the estate.
2
Identify the three imams' positions
Extract the three rulings from the first commentary: (1) Abu Yusuf: divide by value (land and building together). (2) Abu Hanifa: divide land by measurement; the one who gets extra building compensates in dirhams out of necessity. (3) Muhammad: compensate first with land (courtyard); only if impossible then use dirhams.
3
Understand the reasoning
Analyze the basis for each view. Abu Yusuf aims for equal total value. Abu Hanifa starts with measurement but allows cash to avoid inequality. Muhammad prioritizes in-kind compensation to avoid cash, citing the principle that there is no partnership in dirhams.
4
Apply to a concrete scenario
Example: A house with courtyard and building, partners want partition. One partner's share includes more building. How would each imam resolve it? Trace the steps for each.
5
Determine when cash is allowed
According to the second commentary, cash from outside the estate is only permissible (1) with mutual consent, or (2) out of necessity (darura) when in-kind compensation is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُدْخِلُ الدّراهمَ في القسمة إلا برضاهم
: أي لا يدخل في قسمة العقار الدراهم إلا بالتراضي، حتّى إذا كان أرض وبناء يُقْسَمُ بطريق القيمة عند أبي يوسف ، وعن أبي حنيفة له أنه يَقْسِمُ الأرض بالمساحة ، فالذي وقع البناء في نصيبه يردُّ على الآخر دراهم حتى يساويه، فيُدْخِلُ الدراهم ضرورة، وعن محمد له أنَّه يَرُدُّ على شريكه من العرصة في مقابلة البناء، فإذا بقي فضل ولا يمكن التسوية فحينئذ يردُّ الفضل دراهم ؛ لأنَّ الضرورة في هذا القدر. لم يكن جاز. كذا في «الهداية»(1).
[٢]قوله : ولا يدخل الدراهم في القسمة...الخ ؛ المراد بالدراهم هي التي ليست من التركة، يعني لا يدخل من خارج التركةِ الدراهم في قسمة التركة، فلو كان في القسمة فضل لا يستوي بالدراهم، بل بما كان من جنس المقسوم كفضل بناء فإنّه عوض بالأرض دون القيمة. صورة المسألة : دار بين جماعة فأرادوا قسمتها وفي أحد الجانبين فضل بناء
،
فأراد الشركاء أن يكون عوض البناء الدراهم، والآخر أن يكون عوضه من الأرض، فإنه يجعل عوض البناء من الأرض، ولا يتكلّف الذي وقع البناء في نصيبه أن يردّها بإزاء البناء من الدراهم إلا إذا تعذر صح ذلك.
وإنما قلنا : الدراهم التي ليست من التركة، حتى لو كان كذلك فيجز بها نقصان بعض الأنصباء، هل يجبر النقصان بشيء من التركة، والدليل على عدم الدخول أنه لا شركة في الدراهم، والقسمة من حقوق الاشتراك. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية».
And he does not include dirhams in partition except with their consent. i.e., he does not include dirhams in partition of real estate except by mutual consent. So if there is land and building, it is divided according to value according to Abu Yusuf. From Abu Hanifa: he divides the land by measurement, so the one in whose share the building falls gives dirhams to the other to equalize, thus he includes dirhams out of necessity. From Muhammad: he gives back to his partner from the courtyard in exchange for the building; if there remains excess and equalization is not possible, then he returns the excess in dirhams, because necessity applies to this extent. It is not permissible otherwise. This is in Al-Hidayah. [Commentary]: The meaning of dirhams is those that are not from the estate, i.e., he does not include dirhams from outside the estate in the partition of the estate. If there is an imbalance in partition, it is not equalized with dirhams, but with something of the same kind as the divided property, like excess building, it is compensated with land, not value. Example: A house among a group, they want to partition it, and on one side there is excess building. The partners want to compensate the building with dirhams, and the other wants compensation in land; then compensation for the building is taken from land, and the one in whose share the building falls is not required to give dirhams for the building unless it becomes impossible. And we said: the dirhams that are not from the estate, even if it were so, it would compensate for the shortfall of some shares; does the shortfall have to be compensated from the estate? The evidence for not including is that there is no partnership in dirhams, and partition is a right of partnership.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about including cash (dirhams) in the partition of real estate. The matn states that dirhams cannot be introduced without consent. The first commentary explains the positions of the three leading Hanafi imams: Abu Yusuf says land and building are valued together; Abu Hanifa says land is measured and the one who gets more building pays compensation in dirhams by necessity; Muhammad says compensation should be in land (courtyard) first, and only if impossible then in dirhams. The second commentary clarifies that 'dirhams' here means money from outside the estate, not from the jointly owned property. If there is an imbalance, it should be rectified with property of the same kind (e.g., land for extra building), not with cash, unless impossible. The reasoning is that there is no joint ownership in dirhams, and partition is a right of partnership. Thus, cash is only a last resort or with agreement.

Key Terms
التركة (al-tarika): The estate of the deceased being divided
الضرورة (al-darura): Necessity that permits deviation from the general rule (excluding dirhams)
الدراهم (al-darahim): Cash (dirhams) from outside the jointly owned estate
القسمة (al-qisma): Partition of jointly owned property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the preferred method to compensate for an excess building in a partition?
A) Cash dirhams from outside the estate.
B) Land from the courtyard of the same property.
C) A payment from the partner's personal wealth.
D) Ignoring the excess and keeping the partition as is.

Key Takeaway
Cash may only be introduced into a partition by mutual consent or genuine necessity; in-kind compensation is the default to preserve partnership rights.

Drain or Path in Another's Share: Partition Validity
Ruling on partition defect due to easement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a drain or pathway falls into another's share after partition, and apply the remedy (divert or rescind).

Core Idea
A partition must completely sever interference; any remaining easement requires removal or the partition is void.

When two co-owned properties are partitioned, each must receive independent access and drainage. If a drain or path of one share ends up on the other's land without a prior condition, the partition is defective. The law requires immediate rectification: the burden must be shifted if possible, or the entire partition is rescinded to restore the status quo.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage speaks of a drain (masil) or path (tariq) of one share lying in another share after partition, without any stipulation (bila shart). Recognize that this creates a continuing servitude that defeats the purpose of partition: severing all joint rights.
2
Determine the remedy: diversion
First, it is obligatory (wajib) to divert the drain/path to the rightful owner's own land if physically possible. This restores full ownership and eliminates the interference.
The text says 'surifa in amkan' – it is turned away if possible.
3
If diversion impossible: rescission
If diversion is not feasible, the entire partition is rescinded (fuskh). The partition is fasid because it leaves a mixture (ikhtilat) in one share, defeating the essential goal of qism.
The partition is annulled and must be redone properly.
4
Understand the rationale
The purpose of partition (qism) is to eliminate all forms of joint ownership and interference. If a drain or pathway remains, the benefit of one share is dependent on the other's land, contradicting the meaning of partition.
The commentary cites 'tahqeeq ma'na al-qism' – actualizing the meaning of partition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وَقَعَ مسيلُ قِسْم أو طريقهُ في قِسْم آخر بلا شرط فيها صُرِفَ إن أمكن وإلا فسخت (١).
[1] قوله : فإن وقع مسيل...الخ ؛ صورته : دار بين رجلين فيها صفة وبيت كان بابه ومسيل ماء البيت على ظهر الصفة، فاقتسماها ، فأصاب الصفة مع قطعة من الساحة أحدهما، ولم يشترط في القسمة أن يترك الطريق والمسيل على ما كان قبل.
وصاحب البيت يقدر أن يفتح بابه فيما أصابه من المساحة، ويسيل ماؤه في ذلك ، فليس له كذلك، بل حرف باب البيت ومسيل الماء إلى ما أصابه من الساحة تحقيقاً لمعنى القسمة، وهو قطع الشركة، وإن لم يقدر فسخت القسمة ؛ لاختلالها ببقاء الاختلاط من وجه. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية».
(1) أي صرف لإمكان تحقق معنى القسمة ، وهو قطع الشركة وتكميل المنفعة من غير المضرة، وإن لم يمكن صرفه فسخت القسمة، واستؤنفت على وجه يمكن لكل واحد أن يجعل لنفسه مسيلاً وطريقاً ؛ لأنها وقعت مختلة لبقاء الاختلاط وعدم حصول المقصود بها. ينظر: «فتح باب العناية» (٢) : ٤٠٦).
If the drain of one share or its path falls into another share without a stipulation about them, it is turned away/diverted if possible; otherwise the partition is rescinded. (1) His saying: 'If a drain falls...' its example: A house between two men, in it a portico and a room whose door and water drain were on the roof of the portico. They divided it, and the portico along with a piece of the courtyard fell to one of them, and they did not stipulate in the partition that the path and drain remain as before. The owner of the room is able to open his door onto the area that fell to him and drain his water there, but he is not allowed to do that; rather he changes the door of the room and the water drain to the courtyard area that fell to him to actualize the meaning of partition, which is cutting the partnership. If he is not able, then the partition is rescinded because it is defective due to remaining mixture in some respect. This is in Al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyya. (1) i.e., it is diverted to make possible the realization of the meaning of partition, which is cutting the partnership and perfecting the benefit without harm. If it cannot be diverted, the partition is rescinded, and it is recommenced in a manner that each can make for himself a drain and path; because it occurred defective due to remaining mixture and not achieving the intended goal.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage gives a specific ruling about a drain (masil) or path (tariq) of one share falling within another's share without any condition in the partition. The rule: the drain or path must be redirected (sarf) to a place that is part of the rightful owner's share, if possible. This is to realize the purpose of partition: severing the partnership and ensuring each can use his share independently. If redirection is not possible, then the entire partition is rescinded (faskh) because the mixture remains in some respect, defeating the purpose. The example clarifies: two men share a house with a portico and a room; the room's door and water drain open onto the portico roof. After partition, one gets the portico and part of the courtyard. He cannot keep the door and drain as before because that would mean his property is burdened by the other's right. He must move the door and drain to his own courtyard area. If he cannot, the partition is invalid and must be redone properly.

Key Terms
مسيل (masil): A drain or watercourse for water to flow
فسخت (fuskh): The partition is rescinded/invalidated due to defect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling if a drain from one share falls into another share after partition and cannot be redirected?
A) The partition remains valid with compensation
B) The partition is rescinded
C) The shares are exchanged
D) The drain is allowed as an easement

Key Takeaway
A partition that leaves a drain or path in another share without a condition must be corrected by diversion or, if impossible, by rescission, to achieve complete independence.

Dividing Upper and Lower Floors: Value vs. Measure in Hanafi Partition
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partition method for multi-story properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on dividing shared upper/lower floors and apply the relied-upon valuation method to a given scenario.

Core Idea
When floors differ in utility, equal partition by area is unjust — valuation ensures equity.

When co-owners share a multi-story property, simple area-based division may not reflect the true economic value of each floor. The three leading Hanafi imams held different views on the correct method. The later preferred view (mufta bihi) is that of Imam Muhammad, who required each floor to be independently valued and divided accordingly, because the lower floor has unique utilities that the upper floor lacks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asset and the dispute
The asset is a property with an upper floor (ulu) and a lower floor (sufal) that are jointly owned. The dispute is about the method of partition when co-owners request division from a judge.
2
Learn the three Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa: divide by cubit, with 1 cubit of lower equal to 2 cubits of upper. Abu Yusuf: divide by cubit, with equal exchange (1:1). Muhammad: divide by market value, each floor valued separately.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; all agree division is valid, but differ on the method.
3
Understand the rationale (talil) for valuation
The lower floor is suitable for utilities like wells, cellars, and stables, which the upper floor cannot accommodate. Thus they are effectively different types of property, and equitable division requires valuation.
This reasoning supports Muhammad's view.
4
Identify the mufta bihi and its authority
Muhammad's valuation method is recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position for fatwa. Later Hanafi masters like al-Marghinani adopted it.
Even though Abu Hanifa's view has strong precedent, the later preference is for Muhammad's because of its practical equity.
5
Apply the mufta bihi to a scenario
If the lower floor is valued at 100,000 and the upper at 60,000, and each co-owner's share is half, the lower floor owner takes the lower and compensates the other, or they divide proceeds after sale proportionate to value.
The key is that valuation captures the different utilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سفل ذو علو، وسفل وعلو مجرّدان قُوْمَ كُلِّ وحدَه ، وقسم بها عند محمد وبه يُفْتَى.
(۲) سفل ذو علو ، وسفل ) وعلو مجرّدان (۱) قُومَ كُل وحده، وقُسِمَ بها عند وبه يُفْتَى (٢) ) : أي قسم بالقيمة عنده، وعند أبي حنيفة له يقسم بالذراع كل ذراع من السفل في مقابلة ذراعين من العلو، وعند أبي يوسف له يقسم بالذراع أيضاً ، لكن العلو والسفل متساويان.
[١]قوله : علو وسفل ... الخ ؛ صورة المسألة : أن يكون العلو مشتركاً بين رجلين وسفله لآخر، أو سفله مشتركاً بينهما وعلوه لآخر، طلباً القسمة من القاضي أو طلب أحدهما. فعند أبي حنيفة له يحسب في القسمة ذراع من السفل والعلو، فإن كانت قيمتها على السواء يحسب ذراع بذراع، وإن كانت قيمة أحدهما ضعف قيمة الآخر يحسب من الذي قيمته على الضعف ذراع بدرهمين من الآخر، حتى يستويا في القسمة. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية». وأيضا: لأن السفل يصلح لما لا يصلح له العلو كالبئر والسرداب والاصطبل وغير ذلك فصارا كالجنسين فلا يمكن التعديل إلا بالقيمة. ينظر: (الدرر (٢): ٤٢٥)، (الدر المختار) (٥: ١٦٧)، (الشرنبلالية) (٢: ٤٢٥)، وغيرها.
Upper floor having a lower floor, and lower floor and upper floor being abstract: each is valued separately, and division is made according to that (value) according to Muhammad, and with it fatwa is given. (2) Upper floor having a lower floor, and lower floor and upper floor being abstract: each is valued separately, and division is made according to that (value) with fatwa given: meaning division by value according to him. According to Abu Hanifa, he divides by the cubit: each cubit of the lower floor is in exchange for two cubits of the upper floor. According to Abu Yusuf, he also divides by the cubit, but the upper and lower are equal. [1] His statement: 'Upper and lower... etc.'; the scenario of the issue: the upper floor is shared between two men and its lower floor is for another, or its lower floor is shared between them and its upper floor for another, they request division from the judge or one of them requests. According to Abu Hanifa, he calculates in the division a cubit of the lower and upper; if their values are equal, he calculates cubit for cubit; if the value of one is double the value of the other, he calculates from the one whose value is double a cubit for two dirhams from the other, until they become equal in the division. Thus in al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyya. And also because the lower floor is suitable for what the upper floor is not suitable for, like wells, cellars, stables, etc., so they become like two different types; thus equalization can only be achieved by value. See: al-Durar (2:425), al-Durr al-Mukhtar (5:167), al-Shurunbulaliyya (2:425), etc.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning how to partition a property with upper and lower floors when the co-owners request division. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Imam Muhammad: each floor is valued separately and division is made according to value. Imam Abu Hanifa held that division should be by linear measure, with one cubit of the lower floor corresponding to two cubits of the upper floor. Imam Abu Yusuf also held division by measure, but with equal exchange. The rationale for valuation is that the lower floor (sufal) can be used for purposes the upper floor cannot, such as wells, cellars, and stables, making them different types; equitable partition requires valuation.

Key Terms
سفل (sufal): The lower floor or lower part of a property.
علو (ulu): The upper floor or upper part of a property.
قسمة (qisma): Division or partition of jointly owned property.
تعديل (ta'dil): Equalization; making the shares equitable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), how should a property with upper and lower floors be divided among co-owners?
A) By equal share of area regardless of value
B) By valuation: each floor valued separately and divided accordingly
C) By cubit measure with lower floor double the upper
D) By cubit measure with equal exchange

Key Takeaway
When dividing jointly owned property with floors of different utility, the Hanafi mufta bihi requires each floor to be valued separately and divided according to market value.
