Rulings on Post-Division Claims: Oaths and Annulment
Two rulings on post-division claims with commentary on oaths and annulment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for resolving claims after division, distinguishing between claims of usurpation and mistake.

Core Idea
A claim before division acknowledgment leads to mutual oaths and annulment; a claim after receipt leads to a single oath without annulment.

In Hanafi fiqh, after property is divided jointly, disputes may arise. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies how to resolve such disputes: when one party alleges taking after receipt, the other swears an oath; when a party claims that the division itself was inequitable before it was finalized, mutual oaths are taken and the division is rescinded. Understanding this distinction is crucial for anyone dealing with inheritance or partnership divisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the first ruling: 'If he said: I received it, then took some of it' (claim after receipt). Read the second: 'If he said before my acknowledgment: I was entitled to such-and-such and it was not given to me' (claim during division).
The key is the timing: after receipt vs. before acknowledgment.
2
Determine the legal response
For the first scenario: the opponent is made to swear a single oath. For the second: both parties swear mutual oaths and the division is annulled.
The commentary explains the second case is like a dispute over sale price.
3
Understand the distinction
The first claim is one of usurpation (ghasb) after delivery; the second is a claim that the division itself was flawed before it was finalized. The nature of the claim dictates the procedure.
4
Apply the example from the commentary
In the sheep division example: if one claims a mistake in the count after division is complete, the other's word is taken with oath. If a specific taking by mistake is alleged, the defendant is sworn.
The difference between a general mistake and a specific act of taking.
5
Synthesize the rule
Formulate the rule: Claims that challenge the division itself lead to mutual oaths and annulment. Claims about acts after division lead to a single oath without annulment.
This preserves finality of completed divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القسمة/ تعريفها وأحكامها
وإن قال : قبضته ثم أخذ بعضه، حُلّفَ خصمه، وإن قال : قبل إقراره أصابني كذا ولم يُسَلِّمْ إليَّ، تحالفا وفُسِخَتْ
قلنا : لا بل شهادة على فعل غيرهما، وهو الاستيفاء.
(وإن قال : قبضتُه ثُمَّ أَخَذَ بعضه، حُلّفَ خصمه ) : أي قال : قبضتُ حقي ولكن أخذ بعضه بعدما قبضتُهُ حُلّفَ خصمه.
(وإن قال قبلَ إقرارِهِ ) : أصابَني كذا ولم يُسَلِّمْ إلي ، تحالفا" وفُسِخَت) ؛ لأنه اختلاف في مقدار ما حصل له بالقسمة ، فصار كالاختلاف في مقدار المبيع.
المال واحد لا تقبل شهادته ؛ لأنَّ شهادة الفردِ غير مقبولة ، ولو أمر القاضي أمينه بدفع ا فيقبل قول الأمين في دفع الضمان عن نفسه، ولا يقبل في إلزام الآخر إذا كان منكراً(٢). [١] قوله : على فعل غيرهما ؛ وهو الاستيفاء ؛ لأنّ فعل القاسمين الإفراز والتعمير، وهو مستغن عن الشهادة ؛ لكونه محسوساً بخلاف الاستيفاء، فإنّه فعل صاحب الحصة، وهو في غاية الظهور. قاله الجلبي
[٢]قوله : حلف خصمه ؛ لأنه يدعي الغصب وهو منكر، والقولُ قولُ المنكر مع الحلف، في تكملة البحر (٤) قال : ولو اقتسما مئة شاة وقبضا ثمّ ادّعى أحدهما على صاحبه أنك أخذت خمسة من نصيبي غلطاً، وأنكر الآخر، وقال: اقتسمنا على أن يكون لي خمسة وخمسون، ولك خمسة وأربعون، فالقول قوله مع يمينه ؛ لأن القسمة قد تمت.
ثم ادعى أحدهما على الآخر أنه أخذ خمسة غلطاً، وأنكر الآخر، فإن قامت بينة عمل بها، وإلا استحلف المدعى عليه. كذا في «المحيط»، ففي المسألة الأولى هـو يدعي الأخذ بطريق الغصب، وفي هذه الأخذ بطريق الغلط فافترقا.
Book of Division / Its Definition and Rulings. And if he said: 'I received it, then took some of it,' his opponent is made to swear. And if he said: 'Before my acknowledgment, I was entitled to such-and-such and it was not given to me,' they both swear and the division is annulled. We said: No, rather it is testimony about the action of others, which is taking delivery. (And if he said: 'I received it, then took some of it,' his opponent is made to swear): i.e., he said: 'I received my right, but then took some of it after I had received it,' his opponent is made to swear. (And if he said before his acknowledgment): 'I was entitled to such-and-such and it was not given to me,' they both swear and the division is annulled; because it is a dispute over the amount that came to him through division, so it becomes like a dispute over the price in a sale. Property is one, his testimony is not accepted because the testimony of an individual is not accepted. And if the judge orders his trustee to pay, the trustee's word is accepted in discharging himself from liability, but not in imposing liability on the other if he denies it. [1] His statement: 'about the action of others; which is taking delivery'; because the action of the dividers is separation and determination, and it is independent of testimony because it is perceptible, unlike taking delivery, which is the action of the owner of the share, and it is extremely apparent. Al-Jalabi said it. [2] His statement: 'his opponent is made to swear'; because he claims usurpation and the other denies, and the word is that of the denier with his oath. In Takmilat al-Bahr: He said: And if they divided one hundred sheep and took possession, then one of them claimed against his companion: 'You took five from my share by mistake,' and the other denied, saying: 'We divided on the basis that I would have fifty-five and you forty-five,' then his word is accepted with his oath because the division had been completed. Then one of them claimed against the other that he took five by mistake, and the other denied; if evidence is produced, it is acted upon; otherwise the defendant is made to swear. Thus in al-Muhit. So in the first case he claims taking by way of usurpation, and in this case by way of mistake, so they differ.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from the Book of Division (Kitab al-Qismah) regarding disputes that arise after property has been divided. The first ruling: if one party says 'I received my share but then took some of the other's share,' the opponent (the alleged taker) is made to swear an oath denying the claim. The second ruling: if a party says 'Before my acknowledgment (i.e., before the division was finalized), I was entitled to so much but it was not given to me,' then both parties swear oaths (mutual oaths) and the division is annulled. The commentary explains that the second case is analogous to a dispute over the quantity in a sale, leading to mutual oaths and rescission. The passage also discusses the admissibility of testimony: testimony about the act of dividing (which is perceptible) does not require witnesses, but testimony about taking delivery is on the action of the owner and thus should be proven. The commentary cites Takmilat al-Bahr and al-Muhit with examples: if two people divide sheep and one later claims a mistake in the count, the other's word is taken with oath if the division is complete; but if a specific taking by mistake is alleged, the defendant is sworn. The difference hinges on whether the claim is of usurpation (ghasb) or error (ghalat). The authority weight is from the later Hanafi commentaries (Sharh al-Wiqayah and its supercommentary).

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qismah): The legal division of jointly-owned property into shares.
التحالف (al-tahāluf): Mutual swearing of oaths by both parties when they dispute the quantity or terms of a transaction.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Annulment or rescission of the division, returning property to its pre-division state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first ruling, if one party says 'I received my share, then took some of the other's share,' what happens?
A) The division is annulled immediately.
B) The opponent is made to swear an oath.
C) Both parties swear mutual oaths.
D) A panel of witnesses is appointed.

Key Takeaway
Claims made before the division is finalized (before acknowledgment) can lead to annulment through mutual oaths, while claims made after receipt are resolved by a single oath without annulment.

Ruling on Istihqāq After Division: Imam vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on istihqāq after qismah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf when a third party claims a portion of one co-owner's share after division, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Imam: division stands with proportional compensation; Abu Yusuf: option to annul to avoid tashqis.

In this passage, we encounter an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the effect of a third party's successful claim (istihqāq) on a portion of divided property. The key question: does the claim annul the entire division, or does the division stand with compensation? Understanding this ruling is essential for anyone dealing with property division disputes in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A third party successfully claims ownership of a specific portion of one co-owner's share after a property division has taken place.
The claim is on part of the share, not the whole.
2
State the positions
Imam Abu Hanifa: the division is not annulled; the claimant recovers proportionally from the other co-owner's share. Abu Yusuf: the affected co-owner has the option to annul the entire division.
3
Understand the reasoning
Imam prioritizes the finality of completed divisions to avoid destabilization. Abu Yusuf prioritizes preventing harm (tashqis) from fragmenting a co-owner's share.
tashqis = splitting of a share into small fragments.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Under Imam's view, the division remains and compensation is paid. Under Abu Yusuf's view, the co-owner can undo the division entirely and potentially seek a new division.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن استحق بعض حصة أحدهما شاعَ أو لا لم تُفْسَخ ، ورَجَعَ بقسطه في
كذا في «تكملة البحر» [١] قوله : فإن استحق...الخ ؛ يعني إذا استحق بعض نصيبه أحدهما بعينه لم يفسخ القسمة عند الإمام، ورجّح المستحق بحصة نصف ما استحق عليه في نصيب شريكه، وقال أبو يوسف له بالفسخ.
صورة المسألة : إذا أخذ أحدهما الثلث المقدّم من الدار، والآخر الثلثين من المؤخَّر، وقيمتهما سواء، ثمّ استحق نصف المقدم : يعني استحق النصف من الثلث المقدم الذي وقع في نصيب أحدهما.
فعند الطرفين : إن شاءَ نقض القسمة دفعاً لضرر التشقيص، وإن شاء رجع على صاحبه يربع ما في يده من المؤخّر ؛ لأنه لو استحق كل المقدم رجع بنصف ما في يده ، فإذا استحق النصف رجع بنصف النصف، وهو الربع اعتباراً للجزء بالكل.
توضيح ذلك : أن جميع قيمة الدار ألف ومئتا درهم، وباستحقاق النصف من الثلث المقدَّم تبين أن المشترك بينهما تسعمئة فحق كل واحد منهما في أربعمئة وخمسين
And if some part of the share of one of them is claimed by a third party, whether the property is common or not, the division is not annulled, and he recovers his proportionate share in (the other's share). Thus in Takmilat al-Bahr. [1] His statement: 'And if some part...' etc.; meaning if some specific part of one's share is claimed, the division is not annulled according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), and the claimant is compensated with half of what was claimed against him from the share of his partner. Abu Yusuf said he has the option to annul. The form of the case: if one of them took the front third of the house, and the other took the back two-thirds, and their values are equal, then half of the front third is claimed: i.e., half of the front third that fell into the share of one of them is claimed. Then according to the two parties (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?): if he wishes, he may rescind the division to avoid the harm of splitting (tashqis), and if he wishes, he may seek recourse from his partner for a quarter of what is in his hand from the back; because if the entire front third had been claimed, he would seek half of what is in his hand; so if half is claimed, he seeks half of that half, which is a quarter, considering the part as similar to the whole. Clarification: The total value of the house is one thousand two hundred dirhams, and by the claim of half of the front third, it becomes clear that the common property between them is nine hundred, so each of them is entitled to four hundred and fifty (dirhams).
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where, after division of jointly-owned property, a third party establishes a right (istihqāq) on a specific portion of one co-owner's share. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the division is not annulled; instead, the successful claimant recovers proportionally from the other co-owner's share. Abu Yusuf, however, gives the affected co-owner the option to annul the division entirely. The commentary provides an example: a house divided such that one party gets the front third, the other the back two-thirds (equal value). If half of the front third is claimed, Abu Hanifa's position allows the affected party to either rescind the division (to avoid having a fragmented share) or demand compensation: a quarter (half of half) from the other's share, because if the whole front third had been claimed, half of the other's share would be due. The numerical clarification shows the calculation: total value 1200 dirhams; after claim, the remaining common value is 900, so each is entitled to 450. The authority weight is from later commentaries (Takmilat al-Bahr), and the dispute is intra-Hanafi between Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
الاستحقاق (al-istihqāq): A third party's legal claim to ownership of a portion of divided property.
التشقيص (al-tashqīṣ): The splitting of a share into multiple fragments due to a partial claim, causing harm to the owner.
الربع (al-rub'): In this context, the fractional compensation of quarter (half of half) for a partial claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens when a third party successfully claims a portion of one co-owner's share after division?
A) The entire division is annulled.
B) The division remains, and the claimant recovers compensation from the other co-owner's share.
C) The division remains, and the affected co-owner is compensated by the claimant.
D) The case is referred to a judge for a new division.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a third party claims a portion of divided property, Imam Abu Hanifa's position is that the division stands and the claimant recovers proportionally; Abu Yusuf allows annulment to avoid tashqis, but the mufta bihi is Imam's view.

Claim of Right After Division: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Dissolution of Qisma
Explains the intra-Hanafī dispute on dissolution of division upon a third party's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding dissolution of divided property upon a third party's claim of a shared part, and apply the most correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) to determine the claimant's legal options.

Core Idea
A claim on a shared part of one partner's share does not dissolve the division; the claimant may annul or seek compensation.

When jointly owned property is divided, a third party may later prove ownership of a portion. Does this void the original division? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement, with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differing on whether the division remains valid. This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah explains the ruling and the underlying reasoning, and it clarifies that the most correct view follows Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios of claim after division
The passage lists three possibilities: claim on a specific part (mu'ayyan) of one share, claim on a shared part (shāi') of both shares, and claim on a shared part of one share. Identify each scenario as referenced in the text.
The distinction between mu'ayyan and shāi' is crucial.
2
State the consensus rulings
For a specific part in one share, the division is not dissolved by consensus. For a shared part in both shares, the division is dissolved by consensus. Confirm these from the passage.
These two cases are agreed upon by the imams.
3
Focus on the disputed case: shared part in one share
This is where Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ. Abu Hanifa rules the division is not dissolved (the claimant has a choice), while Abu Yusuf rules it is dissolved. Identify the evidence each uses.
The text also mentions the most correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) that Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa.
4
Compare the proofs of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa argues that the meaning of ifrāz (separation) is not destroyed because the claim is limited to one share. Abu Yusuf argues that a third partner emerges and division without his consent is invalid. Trace these arguments from the passage.
The Hanafi response is that the claim is only on one share, so the division remains effective with compensation.
5
Apply the most correct view and the claimant's options
According to the most correct view (Abu Hanifa's), the division stands. The claimant has a choice: either annul the division to avoid splitting his share, or take compensation from the other partner. Calculate the compensation as shown in the example.
The example shows the claimant can take a quarter of the other partner's share to even values.

حصة شريكه، وتفسخُ في بعض مشاع في الكلّ ، اعلم أن الاستحقاق إما في بعض نصيب أحدهما : فإن كان بعضاً شائعاً لا تفسخ عند أبي حنيفة له، وتفسخ عند أبي يوسف ، والأصح ح (۱) أن محمداً مع أبي حنيفة . وصورته : أنهما اقتسما دارا فوقع النصفُ الغربي لأحدهما ، فاستحق النصف الشائع من هذا النصف الغربي ، فإذا لم تفسخ ، فالمستحق منهم بالخيار : إن شاءَ نقض القسمة دفعاً لضرر التبعيض، وإن شاء رجع على الآخر بالربع. والذي بقي في يد صاحب المقدّم يساوي ثلاثمئة وما في يد صاحب المؤخر يساوي تسعمئة، فيرجعُ بربع ما في يده، وقيمته مئة وخمسون، حتى يسلّم لكل واحد منهما ما يساوي أربعمئة وخمسين. ذكر في «العناية»: هاهنا ثلاثة أوجه باستحقاق بعض معين في أحد النصيبين أو فيهما جميعاً، واستحقاق بعض شائع في النصيبين ، ففي الأول لا يفسخ القسمة بالاتفاق، وفي الباقي يفسخ بالاتفاق، وفي الثالث يفسخ عند أبي حنيفة ، ولكن يخير إن شاءَ رجع بحصة ذلك في حصة صاحبه، وإن شاء ردّ ما بقي وأقسم ثانياً. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية». (۱) ذكره أبو حفص، وهو الأصح ، لما ذكر أبو سليمان قول محمد مع أبي يوسف . ينظر : «فتح باب العناية (٢) : ٤٠٦). وإن كان بعضاً معيناً من نصيب أحدهما ، فقد قيل : إنه على الاختلاف، والصحيح أنها لا تفسخ بالإجماع، بل يرجعُ بقسطه في حصة شريكه، كما إذا كانت الدار بينهما نصفين فقسمت ، فاستحق من يد أحدهما بيت هو خمسة أذرع رجع بنصف ما استحق في نصيب صاحبه. وإن كانت أثلاثاً ثلث لأحدهما ، والثلثان للآخر، فاستحق من يدِ صاحب الثُّلُثِ رَجَعَ بِثْلُثي ما استحق من صاحب الثلثين ، وإن استحق من يدِ صاحب الثلثين رَجَعَ بثلث ما استحق. وإن استحق البعض من نصيب كل واحد ، فإن كان شائعاً، فسختِ القسمة، وإن كان معيناً لم يذكر هذه المسألة. فأقول : لا تفسخ القسمة، بل يُجْعَلُ هذا المستحق كأن لم يكن ، فإن كان الباقي في يد كلّ واحدٍ منهما بقدر نصيبه ، فلا رجوع لأحدهما على صاحبه، وإن نقص من نصيب أحدهما يرجع بالحصة، كما إذا كانت الدار نصفين، والمستحق عشرة أذرع خمسة من نصيب هذا، وخمسة من نصيب ذلك، فلا رجوع لأحدهما على صاحبه، وإن كانت أربعة من هذا وستة من ذلك يرجع الثاني على الأول بذراع. والثالث : إذا استحق جزءاً شائعاً من نصيب أحدهم. لأبي يوسف له : إن باستحقاق بعض شائع ظهر شريك ثالث لهما، والقسمة بدون رضاه باطلة، كما إذا استحق بعض شائع في النصيبين ، وهذا يعنـي بـطـلان القسمة ؛ لأنه باستحقاق جزء شائع ينعدم معنى القسمة، وهو الإفراز؛ لأنه يوجبُ الرجوع بحصته في نصيب الآخرِ شائعاً بخلاف المعين. ولهما : إن معنى الإفراز لا ينعدمُ باستحقاق جزء شائع في نصيب أحدهما ؛ ولهذا جازت القسمة على هذا الوجه في الابتداء. هذا ملخص ما في «الفوائد العارفية».
The share of his partner, and it dissolves in some shared part in the whole. Know that the claim (istihqāq) is either in a part of one of their shares: if it is a shared part (shāi‘), it does not dissolve according to Abū Ḥanīfa; it dissolves according to Abū Yūsuf; and the most correct (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that Muḥammad is with Abū Ḥanīfa. Its example: two persons divided a house, and the western half fell to one of them, then a shared half of that western half was claimed. If it does not dissolve, the claimant has the choice: if he wishes, he may annul the division to avoid the harm of partitioning, and if he wishes, he may claim a quarter from the other. What remains in the hand of the one whose portion is taken is worth three hundred, and what is in the hand of the other is worth nine hundred; he claims a quarter of what is in his hand, valued at one hundred and fifty, so that each ends up with four hundred and fifty. It is mentioned in al-‘Ināya: here there are three aspects: claim of a specific part in one of the two shares or in both, and claim of a shared part in the two shares. In the first, the division does not dissolve by consensus; in the remaining, it dissolves by consensus; in the third, it dissolves according to Abū Ḥanīfa, but he gives a choice: if he wishes, he claims the share of that in the share of his partner, and if he wishes, he returns what remains and divides again. Thus in al-Fawā’id al-‘Ārifiyya. [1] His saying: Know that the claim... etc.; mentioned in al-Hidāya: If a specific part of one of their shares is claimed, the division does not dissolve according to Abū Ḥanīfa, and the claimant recovers half of what was claimed from the share of his partner, and Abū Yūsuf said: the division dissolves. What is meant by 'shared part' is a quarter, a third, a fifth, etc. As for the claim of a specific part, the division does not dissolve by consensus. And if a shared part of the whole is claimed, then by consensus there are three aspects: first, that a shared part of the entire house is claimed. Second, that a specific part is claimed. (1) Abū Ḥafṣ mentioned it, and it is the most correct, because Abū Sulaymān mentioned that Muḥammad is with Abū Yūsuf. See: Fatḥ Bāb al-‘Ināya (2):406. And if it is a specific part from one of their shares, it has been said that it is a matter of disagreement, but the correct view is that it does not dissolve by consensus, rather he recovers his portion in the share of his partner. For example, if the house was half each and was divided, then from the hand of one a room of five cubits was claimed, he recovers half of what was claimed from the share of his partner. And if it was thirds, a third for one and two-thirds for the other, and what was claimed from the hand of the owner of the third, he recovers two-thirds of what was claimed from the owner of the two-thirds; and if claimed from the hand of the owner of the two-thirds, he recovers one-third of what was claimed. And if a part is claimed from the share of each, if it is shared, the division is dissolved, and if it is specific, this case is not mentioned. I say: the division is not dissolved, rather this claimant is treated as if he were not; if what remains in the hand of each is equal to his share, then neither has recourse against the other; if there is a shortfall in one's share, he recovers the share. For example, if the house was half each, and the claimant claimed ten cubits, five from this one and five from that one, then neither has recourse against the other; and if it was four from this and six from that, the second recovers one cubit from the first. The third: if a shared part of one of their shares is claimed. Abū Yūsuf's proof: because with a claim of a shared part, a third partner appears to them, and division without his consent is invalid, just as if a shared part of both shares is claimed. This means the division is invalid; because with a claim of a shared part, the meaning of division (ifrāz) is lost; because it necessitates that he recovers his share in the share of the other as shared, unlike the specific. And for them (Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad): the meaning of ifrāz is not lost by a claim of a shared part in one of their shares; therefore division in this manner is valid initially. This is the summary of what is in al-Fawā’id al-‘Ārifiyya.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidāya discusses the rulings on istihqāq (a third party's claim of ownership) after the division (qisma) of jointly owned property. It is an expansion of the original matn, presenting an intra-Hanafī dispute. The author first explains the ruling: if the claim is on a part of one partner's share, there is disagreement. Abū Ḥanīfa holds that the division is not dissolved (lā tufsakh), while Abū Yūsuf holds that it is dissolved. The most correct position according to the text is that Muḥammad ibn al-Ḥasan agrees with Abū Ḥanīfa. An example is given: two partners divide a house, one takes the western half, then a third party claims a half of that western half. Since the division is not dissolved (per Abū Ḥanīfa), the claimant has a choice: either to annul the division to avoid having his share split, or to claim compensation from the other partner. The text calculates that the claimant can take a quarter of the other's share to even out values. The text then cites al-‘Ināya and al-Fawā’id al-‘Ārifiyya summarizing three scenarios: (1) claim of a specific part (mu‘ayyan) in one or both shares – division not dissolved by consensus; (2) claim of a shared part (shāi‘) in both shares – division dissolved by consensus; (3) claim of a shared part in one share – Abū Ḥanīfa, despite the dissolution, gives the claimant a choice. The commentary from al-Hidāya is then quoted, adding further detail: if a specific part of one's share is claimed, Abū Ḥanīfa rules the division stands and the claimant recovers from the partner's share, while Abū Yūsuf dissolves. The phrase 'shared part' means fractions like quarter, third. If a specific part is claimed, there is consensus that the division remains, and the claimant recovers proportionally. The text then provides examples with halves and thirds to illustrate the proportional recovery. If a part from each partner's share is claimed, the ruling depends on the nature of the claim: if shared, the division dissolves; if specific, the author opines that the division remains and the claimant is ignored, with adjustments if shares become unequal. The passage also records the third case (shared part in one share) and presents Abū Yūsuf's proof: such a claim introduces a third partner, so division is invalid without his consent, because the objective of division (ifrāz) is destroyed. The response from Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad is that ifrāz is not destroyed because the claim is limited to one share, and initial division on such a basis is valid; thus the division remains but with compensation. The summary concludes with the ruling from al-Fawā’id al-‘Ārifiyya.

Key Terms
استحقاق (al-istihqāq) (al-istihqāq): A third party's establishment of ownership or right over a part of the property after a division has taken place.
بعض شائع (ba‘ḍ shāi‘) (ba‘ḍ shāi‘): A fractional, undivided part of the property, such as a quarter, third, or fifth, as opposed to a specific physical portion.
تفسخ القسمة (tufsakh al-qisma) (tufsakh al-qisma): The legal dissolution or invalidation of a pre-existing division of jointly owned property.
إفراز (ifrāz) (ifrāz): The legal effect of separation or segregation of shares in a division, i.e., that each partner's portion is exclusively his.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) in this passage, when a third party claims a shared part (shāi‘) from one partner's share after division, what is the ruling?
A) The division is dissolved by consensus.
B) The division is not dissolved, but the claimant may choose to annul or seek compensation.
C) The division is dissolved only if the claimant demands it.
D) The division remains valid and the claimant has no recourse.

Key Takeaway
When a third party claims a shared part of one partner's share after division, the division is not dissolved; the claimant may either annul the division or take compensation from the other partner.

Definition and Ruling of Muhāyā’a (Division of Usufruct)
Defines muhāyā’a and states its validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define muhāyā’a and state its ruling (sahih) and enforceability in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Muhāyā’a divides usufruct, not ownership, and is a valid, judicially enforceable arrangement among co-owners.

This lesson introduces muhāyā’a, a key concept in Hanafi property law. The passage from the commentary on al-Wiqāya defines the term and affirms its legal validity, distinguishing it from physical division (qisma). Understanding muhāyā’a is essential for handling co-ownership of indivisible assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term and its linguistic origin
Locate the word 'muhāyāʼa' in the text and note the spelling variant. The commentator explains it is derived from the root h-y-', meaning division of benefits.
The spelling with alif instead of hamza is also noted.
2
Understand the definition
The text defines muhāyā’a as 'division of benefits in common properties that cannot be enjoyed while preserving their essence'. This means it applies to assets whose use consumes or alters them (e.g., a house, a well).
It is qismat al-manāfi‘, not qismat al-‘ayn.
3
Recognize the ruling
The commentary states 'Wa şaḥḥa al-muhāyāʼa' (And muhāyāʼa is correct/valid). This indicates the Hanafi position that muhāyā’a is a valid contract.
It is also enforceable if one co-owner requests it.
4
Differentiate from qisma
Qisma divides ownership itself, while muhāyā’a divides only the usufruct. This distinction is crucial for applying the correct rules.
Muhāyā’a is temporary and does not alter ownership.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider two siblings inheriting a single house. They cannot physically divide it. They can agree to use it in alternate years. That is a valid muhāyā’a.
A judge can enforce this if one sibling refuses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وصحت المهاياي ; بالياء المثناة التحتية، وهي عبارة عن قسمة المنافع في الأعيان المشتركة، لا يمكن الانتفاع بها مع بقاء عينها، ولا ينافيها أنها تجبر عليه إن طلب أحدهما ، وقيل : المهايأة بإبدال الهمزة ألفاً في بعض اللغات، يعني: نوبت بنوبت قرار
His saying: And muhāyāʼa is correct; with a dotted yā', and it is an expression for division of benefits in common properties that cannot be enjoyed while preserving their essence, and it is not contradicted by the fact that it is enforced if one of them requests it. And it is said: muhāyāʼa by replacing the hamza with an alif in some dialects, meaning: turn by turn arrangement.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a brief commentary on the matn phrase 'Wa şaḥḥa al-muhāyāʼa'. The commentator defines muhāyāʼa as a term derived from the root h-y-', meaning 'division of benefits (manāfi‘) in shared property that cannot be enjoyed without using up the property itself'. In other words, when co-owners have an indivisible asset (like a building) and they want to divide the usage over time, they can agree to a muhāyā’a (time-sharing). The commentary clarifies that muhāyā’a is a valid arrangement even if it is enforced by a judge when one party requests, i.e., it is not merely voluntary but can be compelled. It also notes a linguistic variant: muhāyā’a can be spelled with an alif instead of hamza in some dialects. The meaning is essentially a 'turn-by-turn arrangement' (nobat ba nobat qarār). This is a standard Hanafī definition: muhāyā’a is the division of usufruct, as opposed to qisma which divides the property itself.

Key Terms
المهايأة (al-muhāyāʼa) (al-muhāyāʼa): A contract between co-owners dividing the usufruct (benefit) of a shared property, e.g., usage by turns, while the ownership remains undivided.
قسمة المنافع (qismat al-manāfi‘) (qismat al-manāfi‘): Division of the benefits or usufruct of a property, as opposed to division of the property's physical ownership ('ayn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the term 'muhāyā’a' mean according to the commentary?
A) The physical division of jointly owned property.
B) The division of the usufruct of shared property that cannot be enjoyed while preserving the asset itself.
C) A sale of a share in property to a third party.
D) A gift of a share in property.

Key Takeaway
Muhāyā’a is a valid Hanafi arrangement for dividing the use of jointly owned property without dividing ownership.

Muzara'ah: Validity, Proof, and Conditions
Defines muzara'ah, rules on its validity, and lists eight conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on muzara'ah, explain the evidence for its validity, and list the eight conditions required for a valid muzara'ah contract.

Core Idea
Muzara'ah is valid by prophetic practice and need, overriding qiyas to the prohibited qafiz al-tahhan.

This lesson explores the sharecropping contract (muzara'ah) — a partnership between landowner and cultivator. While Imam Abu Hanifa initially ruled it invalid due to its resemblance to a prohibited hiring contract (qafiz al-tahhan), his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, established its validity based on the Prophet's practice at Khaybar, widespread custom, and public need. Their view is the relied-upon position for fatwa. We will examine the evidence and the eight conditions that ensure the contract is valid and free from ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Define muzara'ah and its legal nature
Understand that muzara'ah is a contract where the landowner provides land and the cultivator provides labor in exchange for a share of the produce. It is legally considered a form of hire (ijarah) of the land against a share of the output.
The definition sets the stage for the legal analysis.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Recognize that Imam Abu Hanifa held muzara'ah invalid by qiyas on the prohibited qafiz al-tahhan (hiring a miller for a measure of flour), which involves gharar. The two disciples (Sahibayn) argued for validity based on stronger evidence.
The dispute is a classic example of qiyas vs. istihsan and custom.
3
Analyze the evidence of Sahibayn
Study the four proofs: (1) the Prophet's treaty with Khaybar, (2) analogy to mudarabah (partnership in capital and labor), (3) public need, and (4) widespread custom that overrides qiyas, similar to istisna'.
These proofs override the initial qiyas.
4
List the eight conditions for validity
Memorize and understand each condition from the detailed list in Al-Hidayah: suitability of land, capacity of parties, specification of duration, seed provider, share of non-seed provider, vacating land, partnership in produce, and type of seed.
These conditions ensure the contract is free from gharar (uncertainty).
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a hypothetical muzara'ah contract, check each condition and determine whether the contract is valid or fasid. If invalid, identify which condition is missing and state the consequence.
This step consolidates knowledge of conditions in practice.

Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary expanding the base text of al-Wiqayah on the chapter of muzara'ah (sharecropping). The passage first defines muzara'ah and clarifies its legal nature: it is essentially a hiring of land in exchange for a share of the produce. This definition places it in the category of hire (ijarah), and therefore it must include a specified duration, which is a key element of valid hire contracts. The author then explains a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: Imam Abu Hanifa considered muzara'ah invalid by analogy to the prohibited transaction known as 'qafiz al-tahhan', where a miller is hired to grind wheat for a measure of its flour. The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited this because it involves uncertainty (gharar) in the wage. Following this analogy, Abu Hanifa held that the share of crop in muzara'ah is unknown at the time of contract, making it fasid. However, his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (al-Sahibayn), deemed muzara'ah valid. Their reasoning is stronger and fatwa is given on their view. Their evidence includes: (1) The prophetic practice (sunnah) — the Prophet contracted with the people of Khaybar on half the produce. (2) The contract is a partnership between capital (land) and labor, similar to the valid contract of mudarabah (silent partnership). (3) Social need — landowners need laborers and laborers need land, so necessity permits it. (4) Widespread custom (ta'amul) — people have continuously practiced muzara'ah, and custom can override qiyas, as in the case of istisna' (manufacturing contract). Thus, muzara'ah is valid provided eight conditions are met, which the passage details from Al-Hidayah. The eight conditions are: (1) The land must be suitable for cultivation. (2) Both parties (landowner and cultivator) must have legal capacity (are eligible to contract). (3) The duration of the contract must be specified so that the usufruct is known. (4) It must be clear who provides the seed (bather). (5) The share of the party not providing seed must be specified, as it serves as compensation. (6) The landowner must vacate the land for the worker; if he stipulates to work himself, the contract is invalid. (7) There must be true partnership in the produce after it is harvested — any term that cuts the partnership invalidates the contract. (8) The type (jins) of seed must be specified so that the wage (the share) is known. The passage also notes a secondary opinion from Muhammad ibn Salama that specifying the duration is not required, and the contract defaults to one year. However, the relied-upon view requires duration specification.

Key Terms
المزارعة (al-muzāra‘ah): A contract whereby a landowner gives land to a cultivator to work it in exchange for a share of the produce (e.g., half or one-third).
قفيز الطحان (qafīz al-ṭaḥḥān): A prohibited transaction where a person hires a miller to grind grain for a fixed measure of the resulting flour, involving uncertainty.
الخبرة (al-khubrah): In this context, it means 'the share' (al-nasīb) of the produce that the cultivator receives.
المضاربة (al-muḍārabah): A partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, sharing profits; used as analogy to validate muzara'ah.
الاستصناع (al-istiṣnā‘): A contract of manufacturing an item to order; cited as an example where custom overrides qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling on muzara'ah in the Hanafi school?
A) It is invalid (fasid) by qiyas on qafiz al-tahhan.
B) It is valid according to Abu Hanifa but invalid according to the two disciples.
C) It is valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and fatwa is given on their view.
D) It is valid only if the duration is not specified.

Key Takeaway
Muzara'ah is a valid contract in the Hanafi school provided eight conditions are met, based on prophetic practice and public need, overriding the qiyas to the prohibited qafiz al-tahhan.

The Eight Conditions of a Valid Muzara'ah (Sharecropping) Contract
Explaining the eight conditions for valid sharecropping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the eight conditions required for a valid Muzara'ah contract according to the Hanafi school, and identify the consequence of omitting each condition.

Core Idea
A valid sharecropping contract requires eight specific conditions; omission of any renders the contract voidable (fasid).

Muzara'ah is a partnership in agriculture, but it is not a simple rental or a general partnership. The Hanafi school has laid down eight precise conditions to ensure fairness and avoid disputes. These conditions govern the land, the parties, the duration, the seed, the shares, the vacating of land, the partnership in produce, and the type of seed. Understanding these conditions is crucial for anyone engaging in agricultural contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Muzara'ah is a valid contract; its conditions are wajib for validity. The text lists eight conditions that must be present; otherwise, the contract is fasid (voidable).
Fasid means the contract is not effective by default but can be rectified if performed.
2
List the eight conditions
From the passage: (1) land suitable for cultivation, (2) legal capacity of both parties, (3) duration specified, (4) who provides seed, (5) share of non-seed provider specified, (6) landowner vacates land, (7) partnership in produce (not fixed), (8) type of seed specified.
Memorize the list; each has a rationale.
3
Understand the rationale for each condition
The text provides reasons: e.g., duration is needed because it's a contract on usufruct; seed provider determines subject matter; partnership in produce ensures mutuality.
The rationale helps apply the conditions to new scenarios.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on duration
Muhammad ibn Salamah holds that duration is not required and defaults to one year. The majority requires explicit duration to avoid ambiguity.
The mufta bihi is the majority view requiring duration.
5
Apply the conditions to a real scenario
Given a specific contract, check each condition to determine validity. For example, if the landowner stipulates his own work, condition 6 is violated, making the contract fasid.
Practice with examples to internalize the rules.

Condition: Suitability of the land for cultivation, legal capacity of the contracting parties, and mention of the duration. Suitability of the land up to his statement: and partnership in the produce, and it is clarified by what is in Al-Hidayah in detail where he said: First: The land being suitable for cultivation because the objective is not attained without it. Second: That the landowner and the cultivator be qualified for the contract, and it is not exclusive to them because no contract is valid except from a qualified person. Third: Specification of the duration because it is a contract on the usufruct of the land or the worker, and the duration is the measure for it to be known. Fourth: Specification of who provides the seed to cut off dispute and to inform of the subject matter, which is the usufruct of the land or the worker. Fifth: Specification of the share of the one from whom the seed does not come because he deserves it as compensation by condition, so it must be known, and what is not known cannot be made a condition of the contract. Sixth: That the landowner vacate the land for the worker, such that if he stipulates work of the landowner, the contract becomes invalid due to loss of vacating. Seventh: Partnership in the produce after it appears because it is concluded as a partnership in the end, so whatever cuts off this partnership invalidates the contract. Eighth: Specification of the type of seed so that the compensation becomes known. He said: And mention of the duration... etc. because it is a contract on the usufruct of the land or the worker, and it is only known by the duration. And according to Muhammad ibn Salamah: Specification of the duration is not required, and it falls on one year. He said: And mention of the duration... etc. by saying: for a year or two years or similar. And if he specifies a time in which cultivation is not possible, the cultivation fails, so mentioning it and not mentioning it are the same. Likewise if he specifies a duration during which one of them generally does not live, because it becomes like stipulating the contract until after death. It is in Al-Kifayah. He said: And the owner of the seed... etc. because the subject matter differs with its difference. If the seed is from the worker, the subject matter is the usufruct of the land; if from the landowner, the subject matter is the usufruct of the worker. So the subject matter must be specified, because ignorance leads to dispute between them. It is in Al-Kafi. He said: And the share of the other... etc. meaning the portion of the one from whom the seed does not come because it is the hire for his work or his land, so it must be known. It is in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. He said: And partnership in the produce... meaning when it appears, because that is the objective of it. So it is concluded as a hire in the beginning and becomes a partnership in the end. And whatever cuts off this partnership invalidates the contract, and it is supported by the ramification in his statement: so it is invalid if it is stipulated for one of them... etc. It is in Al-Jalabi. He said: And partnership in the produce... meaning it is befitting that a specific amount not be stipulated for one of them, because it is possible that nothing comes out of the land except that amount, so no partnership remains on that estimate, and the condition requires the continuation of the partnership. It is in Al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the conditions of a valid Muzara'ah (sharecropping) contract according to the Hanafi school. The text, derived from Al-Hidayah and elaborated by later commentators, enumerates eight conditions. First: the land must be suitable for cultivation; otherwise the purpose is defeated. Second: both parties must be legally competent (e.g., sane, adult) because no contract holds without capacity. Third: the duration must be specified, as the contract involves usufruct which is measured by time. A minority view (Muhammad ibn Salamah) holds that duration is not required and defaults to one year. Fourth: it must be clear who provides the seed (the landowner or the worker), as this determines the subject matter (usufruct of land or labor). Fifth: the share of the party not providing seed must be specified, as it is compensation and must be known to avoid dispute. Sixth: the landowner must vacate the land for the worker; any stipulation that prevents this (e.g., the landowner also working) invalidates the contract. Seventh: there must be partnership in the produce after it appears; the contract begins as a hire (ijarah) and ends as a partnership. Any condition that eliminates this partnership (e.g., guaranteeing a fixed amount) invalidates the contract. Eighth: the type of seed must be specified to determine the compensation ('ujrah). The passage also clarifies that if the specified duration is so short that cultivation is impossible, or if neither party is expected to live that long, the contract is invalid. The commentary consistently emphasizes that these conditions aim to prevent ignorance and dispute.

Key Terms
مزارعة (muzāra‘ah): A partnership in agriculture where one party provides land and the other provides labor (and possibly seed) to share the produce.
بذر (badhr): The seeds used for cultivation; the party providing seed determines the subject matter of the contract.
تخلية (takhliyah): Vacating the land by the owner for the worker to use without interference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the consequence if the landowner stipulates that he will work alongside the worker in a Muzara'ah contract?
A) The contract is valid but the land owner's share is reduced
B) The contract becomes invalid because it prevents vacating (takhliyah)
C) The contract becomes a regular partnership (Musharakah)
D) The contract is valid if the worker agrees

Key Takeaway
The eight conditions of Muzara'ah ensure that the contract is fair and unambiguous; any omission invalidates the contract.

Post-Harvest Expenses in Muzara'a and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on expenses after ripening and dispute over stipulation on worker

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling on post-harvest expenses in muzara'a, distinguish between the Zahir al-Riwaya position and Abu Yusuf's view, and apply the mufta bihi ruling based on custom.

Core Idea
In muzara'a, post-harvest expenses are shared; but a stipulation shifting them to the worker voids the contract, unless custom makes it valid.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses a critical question in sharecropping contracts: who bears the cost of harvesting and subsequent work? The default rule in the Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwaya) is that such expenses are shared proportionally. However, if the landowner stipulates that the worker bears all these costs, the contract becomes void—unless, as Abu Yusuf argues, local custom (ta'amul) supports such a condition. The dispute illustrates how the later Hanafi masters reconciled textual principles with practical custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the work periods in muzara'a
Work is categorized before ripening, after ripening but before division, and after division. The ruling depends on the period.
2
State the default ruling for each period
Before ripening: on worker. After ripening before division: shared proportionally. After division: on each owner individually.
3
Analyze the effect of stipulating post-harvest work on the worker
According to Zahir al-Riwaya, such a condition makes the contract fasid (void) because it contradicts the contract's requirements.
The condition is seen as benefiting one party without being required by the contract.
4
Examine Abu Yusuf's counter-view
Abu Yusuf holds the contract valid if customary (ta'amul), citing analogy to istisna' where custom is considered.
This view is preferred by later Hanafi authorities in regions where the custom prevails.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its basis
Later authorities (al-Sarakhsi, al-Zayla'i) prefer Abu Yusuf's view where the custom is widespread; the mufta bihi depends on local practice.
The ruling is not absolute but contingent on 'urf.

(If he stipulates it upon the worker, the contract becomes void) because it is a condition contrary to the requirement of the contract. Then the land is sold for debt until the crop is harvested and the judge removes it from detention if it was detained for debt; because when the sale of the land was prevented he was not unjust, and detention is the penalty for injustice. [1] His saying: 'it falls upon both of them' etc.; meaning according to their ownership after the expiration of the term of muzara'a because it is a shared work, since the contract ends with respect to the term, and the entitlement of work upon the worker was only during the term by the contract, and it does not remain contrary to the case when the owner of the land dies and the crop remains such that the work on it is upon the worker because the contract there remains during its term.
And this is the meaning of the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: 'Because there the contract itself is in its term, and the contract requires the work upon the worker.' And by this it becomes clear that the statement of Sadr al-Shari'ah is far from this. So the conclusion: Indeed every work before ripening is upon the worker, carried upon the case before the passage of the term of muzara'a so that the continuation of the contract and entitlement of work upon the worker can be imagined, for if the term passes, there is no contract and no entitlement, so there is no contradiction between his saying 'it falls upon both of them' and his saying 'it is upon the worker', so it should be pondered. Said by al-Jalabi.
[2] His saying: 'Like the wage of harvesting' etc.; harvesting is cutting the crop after it ripens, al-rafa' (with fat-ha on the ra' and kasra) is carrying the crop to the threshing floor, al-daws is trampling the crop to extract grains from their husks, and al-tadhriya is distinguishing grain from chaff by wind. This is found in reliable dictionaries. Said by al-Jalabi.
[3] His saying: 'If he stipulates' means the work that is after the completion of the crop, like harvesting and its like.
And from Abu Yusuf: he holds that it is valid and binding due to customary practice. Imam al-Sarakhsi said: This is the correct view in our lands because of prevalent custom. The contract of muzara'a becomes void because it is a condition not required by the contract, and it contains benefit for one of them, so it voids the contract like the condition of carrying or grinding upon the worker. Said by al-Jalabi.
[1] His saying: 'And from Abu Yusuf he holds that it is valid' means the muzara'a contract is valid when he stipulates that on the worker due to customary practice, by analogy to istisna', and this is the choice of the scholars of Balkh, said al-Zayla'i. And they add to this narration and say: It is permissible to stipulate the following, and carrying to his home on the worker due to custom. The commentator said: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: This is the correct view in our lands.
And in al-Hidayah: So the conclusion is that what is work before ripening, like watering and guarding, is upon the worker; and what is after ripening before division is upon both of them jointly according to the proportion of shares in the apparent narration, like harvesting, threshing, and their like as we have explained; and what is after division is upon both of them.
And al-mu'amala (i.e., musaqat) is analogous to this: what is before ripening of the fruit, like watering, pollination, and guarding, is upon the worker; and what is after ripening, like picking and guarding, is upon both of them.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary (sharh) on a matn discussing the division of post-harvest expenses in a muzara'a (sharecropping) contract. The author first states the ruling in the Zahir al-Riwaya: expenses incurred after the crop ripens, such as harvesting, carrying to the threshing floor, threshing, and winnowing, are shared between the landowner and the worker according to their respective shares in the crop. This is because the work is shared and the contract has ended by the passage of its term. The passage then addresses a stipulation placing these expenses entirely on the worker: such a condition renders the contract void because it contradicts the nature of the contract, which implies shared expenses after ripening. The text then transitions to an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that such a condition is valid and binding if it is customary (ta'amul), and this view is considered the soundest in some regions like Balkh and the author's lands. The commentary explains that the original ruling applies only while the contract term lasts; after the term, the contract ends and the entitlement of the worker to provide labor lapses, so post-termination work is shared. The summary from al-Hidayah clarifies the division: work before ripening (e.g., watering, guarding) is solely on the worker; work after ripening but before division (e.g., harvesting, threshing) is shared proportionally; work after division is on each owner individually. The same principle applies to musaqat (sharecropping of trees): pre-ripening work on the worker, post-ripening work shared. The passage thus provides both the standard ruling and a practical exception based on custom.

Key Terms
الحصاد (al-hasad): Cutting the crop after it ripens.
الرفاع (al-rafa' (or al-rufa')): Carrying the crop to the threshing floor.
الدوس (al-daws): Threshing the crop by trampling to extract grains.
التذرية (al-tadhriya): Winnowing: separating grain from chaff using wind.
المزارعة (al-muzara'a): A sharecropping contract where the landowner provides land and the worker provides labor, with the crop shared according to agreement.
العامل (al-'amil): The worker/cultivator in a muzara'a or musaqat contract.
التعامل (al-ta'amul): Customary practice; prevalent usage in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The contract is void (fasid) in the Zahir al-Riwaya.
B) The contract is valid (sahih) according to all Hanafi scholars.
C) The stipulation is binding only if the worker agrees voluntarily.
D) The expenses are always shared regardless of stipulation.

Key Takeaway
Post-harvest expenses in muzara'a are shared by default, but if custom dictates otherwise, a stipulation on the worker may be valid per Abu Yusuf's view, which is the mufta bihi in many regions.

Sharing and Meat Division in Udhiyah, and the Istihsan of Partnership on Credit
Defines udhiyah, sharing limits, meat division, and istihsan ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for sharing a cow/camel in udhiyah, explain the meat division rules, and apply the istihsan reasoning to permit partnership in a cow bought on credit.

Core Idea
A cow or camel serves up to seven, each at least one-seventh; meat must be weighed unless mixed; partnership on credit is valid by istihsan.

This passage from the Book of Sacrifice (Kitab al-Udhiyah) lays out three key rulings: the limits of sharing a large animal, the method of dividing meat, and a special license (istihsan) permitting selling shares of a cow bought on credit. These rules balance devotion with practical need and reflect the Hanafi preference for facilitating worship within legal parameters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sharing rule for udhiyah
Locate the statement that a sheep is for one person, while a cow or camel can be shared by up to seven, provided no one has less than one-seventh. Note the condition that if any share is less, everyone's sacrifice is invalid.
The reasoning given: 'because devotion is indivisible'.
2
Examine the meat distribution rule
Find the requirement that meat must be divided by weight, not estimation. Then note the exception: if the meat is accompanied by parts like legs or skin, estimation (without weighing) becomes permissible because mixing different categories removes the harm of inequality.
The text says this is a deviation (saraf) from the normal rule.
3
Analyze the istihsan ruling
Read the case of a cow bought on credit for udhiyah. Identify the qiyas (strict analogy) that would forbid selling shares because the animal is devoted to sacrifice. Then see the istihsan that permits it due to the difficulty of finding partners at purchase time.
Imam Zufar opposed this istihsan, holding to the qiyas.
4
Understand the preference among animals
Review the comparisons: sheep vs. one-seventh of cow, ram vs. ewe, female goat vs. male goat, female camel/cow vs. male. Note the criteria: equal value/meat or greater value determines which is better.
The preference is not absolute but depends on quantitative comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأضحية
هي شاة من فرد، وبقرة أو بعير منه إلى سبعة إن لم يكن لفرد أقل من سبع، حتى لو كان لأحد السبعة أقل من السبع لا يجوز عن أحد؛ لأن القربة لا تتجزأ. وعند مالك عن أهل بيت واحد وإن كانوا أكثر من سبعة، ولا يجوز عن أهل بيتين وإن كانوا أقل من سبعة.
ويُقسم اللحم وزناً لا جزافاً إلا إذا ضم معه من أكارعه أو جلده: أي يكون مع اللحم أكارع أو جلد، ففي كل جانب شيء من اللحم وشيء من الأكارع، أو يكون في كل جانب شيء من اللحم وبعض من الجلد، أو يكون في جانب لحم وأركاع، وفي آخر لحم وجلد، وإنما يجوز صرفاً للجنس إلى خلاف الجنس.
وصح اشتراك سنة في بقرة مشرية لأضحية استحساناً، وفي القياس لا يجوز، وهو قول زفر؛ لأنه أعدها للقربة فلا يجوز بيعها. وجه الاستحسان: قد يجد بقرة سمينة، ولا يجد الشركاء وقت البيع، فالحاجة ماسة إلى هذا. وذا قبل الشراء أحب، ذا إشارة إلى الاشتراك، وعن أبي حنيفة: لا يُكره الاشتراك بعد الشراء.
والشاة أفضل من سبع البقرة إذا استويا في القيمة واللحم، وإن كان سبع البقرة أكثر قيمة كان أفضل. والكبش أفضل من النعجة، وإن كانت النعجة أكثر قيمة أو لحماً فهي أفضل. والأنثى من المعز أفضل من التيس، إذا استويا قيمة. والأنثى من الإبل والبقر أفضل من الذكور إذا استويا في القيمة.
Book of Sacrifice: It is a sheep from one person, and a cow or camel from it up to seven, provided that no one has less than one-seventh. So if one of the seven has less than one-seventh, it is not valid for anyone, because devotion is indivisible. According to Malik: for one household even if more than seven, and not valid for two households even if less than seven. And meat is divided by weight, not by guess, unless accompanied by its legs or skin: meaning that with the meat there are legs or skin, so in each portion there is some meat and some legs, or in each portion some meat and some skin, or in one portion meat and legs and in another meat and skin; it is permissible as a deviation from the genus to something of a different genus. And a partnership of a year in a cow bought on credit for sacrifice is valid by istihsan, but by qiyas it is not valid, which is the saying of Zufar, because he prepared it for devotion so its sale is not permissible. The basis of istihsan: one may find a fat cow and not find partners at the time of sale, so need is pressing for this. And before purchase is preferable; 'this' refers to partnership, and from Abu Hanifa: partnership after purchase is not disliked. And a sheep is better than one-seventh of a cow if the value and meat are equal, but if one-seventh of the cow is greater in value, it is better. And a ram is better than a ewe, but if the ewe is greater in value or meat, it is better. And a female goat is better than a male goat if they are equal in value. And a female camel or cow is better than the male if they are equal in value.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Sacrifice (Kitab al-Udhiyah) presents a ruling with proof and istihsan reasoning. The author first states the basic rule: a sheep (shah) is for a single person, while a cow or camel can serve up to seven people, provided that each participant's share is not less than one-seventh. If any partner has less than one-seventh, the sacrifice is not valid for anyone, because the act of devotion (qurbah) is indivisible. This is the Hanafi position; Imam Malik allows more than seven but only from one household. The text then addresses meat distribution: it must be weighed, not estimated, unless accompanied by parts like legs or skin, which allows mixing of different types to avoid injustice. The next ruling treats a special case: buying a cow on credit (مُشَرَّيَة) for udhiyah and then selling shares to partners. By qiyas it is invalid because the cow was intended for devotion, so selling it is prohibited. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), it is permitted if no partners can be found at the time of purchase. Imam Zufar opposed this. Additionally, partnership initiated before the purchase is preferred (أحب) and not disliked after purchase according to Abu Hanifa. Finally, the passage compares the virtue of different animals: a sheep is better than one-seventh of a cow if value and meat are equal; otherwise the more valuable portion is better. Among sheep, a ram is better than a ewe unless the ewe has more value or meat. Among goats, the female is better than the male if equal in value. Similarly for camels and cows, the female is preferred over the male when value equal. These rulings guide the selection and sharing of sacrificial animals according to Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الأضحية (al-udhiyah): An animal sacrificed specifically on the days of Eid al-Adha with the intention of drawing near to Allah.
السَّبْع (al-sab'): One-seventh share; the minimum portion when sharing a cow or camel for sacrifice.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference departing from strict analogy (qiyas) due to necessity or custom.
مُشَرَّيَة (musharriyah): An animal bought on credit (with deferred payment), used here for udhiyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum share per person when sharing a cow for udhiyah?
A) One-eighth
B) One-seventh
C) One-sixth
D) Any share is allowed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a cow or camel can be shared by up to seven people provided each has at least one-seventh; meat must be weighed unless mixed with other parts; and selling shares of a cow bought on credit is valid by istihsan when partners are unavailable at purchase.

Exchange of Leftover Udhiyah Meat for Durable Goods
Ruling on disposing of leftover udhiyah meat for a minor, with qiyas proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that leftover udhiyah meat for a minor must be exchanged for durable items, and distinguish between allowable and prohibited exchanges using qiyas.

Core Idea
Leftover udhiyah meat for a minor must become a durable asset, not cash or food.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains the rule for disposing of the meat from a child's sacrificial animal. While the child may eat part of it, any remaining meat cannot be wasted or sold. Instead, the guardian must exchange it for durable goods—like clothing—that provide lasting benefit. The proof is based on qiyas with the hide, which must also be preserved as a durable object.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is disposing of leftover udhiyah meat for a minor. The ruling: it must be exchanged for something whose benefit is in its essence (durable), not for consumables like bread or for dirhams.
The matn explicitly states: 'وما بقي يُبَدِّلُ بما ينتفع بعينه'.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is qiyas (analogy) with the hide of the udhiyah animal. The hide may be made into a bag or exchanged for durable items; similarly, the meat must be exchanged for durable items.
The text says: 'قياساً على الجلد'.
3
Trace the illa
The effective cause (illa) is the necessity of preserving lasting benefit and avoiding waste. Consumable items and cash do not provide this; durable goods do.
The illa is jaliyyah (manifest) because the hide is obviously durable, and meat leftover cannot be kept fresh.
4
Note the objection and response
Some might argue the meat could be sold or given as sadaqah. The Hanafi response: a minor cannot sell, and giving sadaqah without need is not allowed; instead, the guardian must preserve the value in a durable asset.
The commentary mentions that exchanging for dirhams is tamawwul (wealth acquisition) and consumables are similar to dirhams.

And the child may eat from it, and what remains is exchanged for something whose benefit is in its essence (like a garment or socks), not for something whose benefit is through consumption (like bread and the like). It is only permissible to exchange for that (durable items) not for consumables, by analogy with the hide: it is permissible to benefit from the hide by making it into a bag. When it is exchanged for a durable item, the substitute takes the ruling of the substituted, so it is like benefiting from its essence. However, exchange for dirhams is wealth acquisition, and consumable items are analogous to dirhams. So when the ruling for the hide is this, they analogize the meat when necessary for the child.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on how to dispose of the meat from a minor's udhiyah sacrifice. According to the matn, the minor can eat from the meat, but the leftover must be exchanged for durable goods (like clothing or leather items) that provide lasting benefit, not for consumable items (like bread) that are used up quickly. The commentator explains the reasoning using qiyas (analogy) with the hide of the sacrificial animal. The hide can be used to make durable items like bags; if it is exchanged for something durable, the substitute takes the same ruling as the original (i.e., it is permissible to benefit from it durably). Exchanging for dirhams is considered acquiring wealth, and consumable items fall under the same category because they are like money in being expendable. Therefore, by analogy, the meat leftovers are treated like the hide: they must be exchanged for durable items, not for cash or consumables, especially when the minor has a genuine need for the benefit.

Key Terms
يُبَدِّلُ بما ينتفع بعينه (yubaddilu bi-mā yuntafa‘u bi ‘aynihi): Exchanges for an item that provides benefit through its physical substance over time (durable goods like clothing).
الاستهلاك (al-istihlāk): Consumption that destroys the item in use, like food.
تموّل (tamawwul): Acquisition of property or wealth; here it refers to exchanging for dirhams or consumables which effectively converts the meat into cash.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The guardian may consume it all.

Key Takeaway
Leftover udhiyah meat for a minor must be exchanged for durable goods, not consumables or cash, by qiyas with the hide.

The Dislike of Cutting the Spinal Cord and Skinning Before Cooling
Ruling with qiyas evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting the spinal cord and skinning before cooling, and explain the qiyas-based evidence for its dislike.

Core Idea
Cutting the spinal cord or skinning before cooling is makruh because it inflicts unnecessary pain.

This lesson examines the ruling on two actions during slaughter: cutting the spinal cord (nakh') and skinning before the animal has cooled. While these actions do not invalidate the slaughter, they are disliked (makruh tanzihi) because they cause extra pain without any necessity. The evidence is based on qiyas (analogy) to wounding the animal before cutting the jugular veins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their ruling
The passage rules on two specific acts: cutting the spinal cord (nakh') and skinning before cooling (salkh). Both are considered makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked), meaning the meat remains halal but the action is discouraged.
The ruling does not affect the validity of the slaughter.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to a known case: wounding an animal and then slitting its jugular veins. That act is also makruh because it increases pain without need.
The text explicitly says: 'it is like if he wounded it and then cut the jugular veins.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is 'increasing pain without necessity in slaughter'. This illa is shared between the original case (wounding then cutting) and the new cases (nakh' and salkh), so the ruling (makruh) transfers.
The illa is clear (jaliyyah) and extracted by tanqih al-manat (refining the cause).
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Although not detailed here, the ruling is mukhtalaf fih: some schools may not consider these acts makruh or may have different thresholds. The Hanafi school relies on the principle of minimizing suffering.
The principle: 'كل ما فيه زيادة ألم لا يحتاج إليه في الذكاة مكروه'.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi: makruh tanzihi. This is supported by the comprehensive principle from al-Hidayah and the analogy drawn in al-Majma'.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

The book of slaughter / its conditions and what is lawful and unlawful. And the nakh' (cutting the spinal cord) and skinning before it cools. Nakh' means intense cutting until it reaches the spinal cord, which in Persian is 'haram maghz'. And skinning before it cools means before it stops moving. Until the veins are cut, and if it dies before cutting the veins, it is not eaten due to death without proper slaughter, thus in al-Majma'. And the reason for dislike is that it causes extra pain without need, so it is like if he wounded it and then cut the jugular veins. [Commentary] The saying 'until it reaches the spinal cord': with fatha on the nun, and kasra and damma are dialectal. The author of al-Hidayah interpreted it as a white vein in the neck bone, but the author of al-Nihayah considered that a mistake, saying it is a white thread in the cavity of the neck bone extending to the spine. It was responded that the animal is composed of bones, nerves, and veins, and there is nothing called a thread originally. Then he mentioned in al-Hidayah: The comprehensive principle for understanding the meaning of dislike is that anything that increases pain unnecessarily in slaughter is disliked. (Jalabi). The saying 'before it cools' etc. with damma on the ra' from the chapter of hasuna from baruda, so its interpretation as stopping is an interpretation of the necessary consequence, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the chapter of slaughter. The matn states that it is disliked (makruh) to cut the spinal cord (nakh') and to skin the animal before it has cooled (i.e., before its movements have ceased). The commentator explains that nakh' means cutting deeply until reaching the spinal cord (nukha'), which is described as a white thread within the bones of the neck. Skinning before cooling means removing the skin while the animal is still moving. The reason for the dislike is that this causes additional pain to the animal without any need, and the goal of slaughter is to minimize suffering. The commentator analogizes this to wounding the animal and then slitting its throat, which is also disliked. He cites the principle from al-Hidayah: 'The comprehensive principle for understanding the meaning of dislike is that anything that increases pain unnecessarily in slaughter is disliked.' This is a clear application of the Hanafi legal maxim that harm should be avoided.

Key Terms
النخع (an-nakh'): Cutting the spinal cord deeply during slaughter
النخاع (an-nukha'): The spinal cord, a white cord in the neck bone
السلخ (as-salkh): Skinning the animal before it has cooled (ceased movement)
الذكاة (adh-dhaka): The Islamic method of slaughter

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Cutting the spinal cord or skinning before cooling is makruh tanzihi due to unnecessary pain, but the slaughter remains valid and the meat halal.

The Condition of the Slaughterer: Muslim or Kitabi
Ruling on slaughterer's faith with modern application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for a valid slaughterer and apply it to contemporary meat sources.

Core Idea
The slaughterer's faith is a condition for halal meat; today, Kitabi meat requires verification.

The Hanafi school holds that the slaughterer must be a Muslim or a Kitabi (Jew or Christian). This ruling originates from Quranic permission and prophetic practice. However, due to changes in religious observance among modern Christians, later scholars have imposed additional conditions. This lesson examines the classical condition and its contemporary application, especially regarding imported meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The passage states that the slaughterer must be Muslim or Kitabi (dhimmi or harbi). This is the core condition for the meat to be halal.
Note: Kitabi refers to Jews and Christians who believe in their scriptures.
2
Examine the evidence
The author cites the hadith of Aisha about Bedouin sacrifices, which establishes that meat from Muslims is presumed halal. For Kitabi, the Quranic permission in Surah al-Ma'idah (5:5) is the basis.
The hadith is a proof for the presumption of proper slaughter in Muslim lands.
3
Understand the scope
The ruling covers both dhimmi (citizen) and harbi (enemy) Kitabi. It does not cover atheists or materialists.
4
Apply to modern context
Today, due to widespread neglect of proper slaughter methods among Christians, the Hanafi scholars require confirmation that a specific meat was slaughtered by a Kitabi using Islamic method. Meat from non-Muslim countries is generally not permissible even with halal labels.
This is a change from the earlier absolute permission.
5
Recognize the operative fatwa
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that meat imported from non-Muslim countries is not halal, and meat from Christians is only halal if the proper method is confirmed.
This is based on contemporary research by scholars like Muhammad Taqi Uthmani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the condition is that the slaughterer be Muslim or Kitabi: dhimmi or harbi. Allah said: ... The doubt about cutting the veins in some cases. And it is possible to choose the mechanical method for Islamic slaughter by the following methods: 1. To dispense with the use of electric current for stunning, or to ensure its mildness so that it does not cause death before slaughter. 2. To replace the rotating knife with persons who slaughter and say the tasmiya at the time of slaughter. That the water through which the chicken passes after slaughter not reach boiling point. As for cattle and sheep, there are two objections: First, the methods used for stunning (shooting, carbon dioxide gas, electric shock) are not safe from causing the animal's death before slaughter, so these methods must be modified to ensure they are not painful and do not cause death. Second, the slaughter may not involve cutting the veins. If reassurance is achieved by removing these two possibilities, then mechanical slaughter is permissible. The intended meaning of Ahl al-Kitab is Jews and Christians who believe in their basic doctrines, even if they believe in false doctrines like trinity and atonement. As for one who does not believe in Allah, the Messenger, or the heavenly books, he is a materialist and does not have the ruling of Ahl al-Kitab, even if his name is registered as Christian or Jew. Accordingly, meat whose slaughterer is unknown in Muslim lands is presumed to be properly slaughtered and is permissible to eat, unless it is proven that the slaughterer did not slaughter it in the prescribed manner. The evidence for this is the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) regarding the sacrifices of the Bedouins. Whatever is found in the markets of Ahl al-Kitab is considered from their slaughtered meat, unless it is proven that the slaughterer was other than them. Today, Christians have thrown off the yoke of obligation regarding slaughter and abandoned the rulings of their religion, so they do not adhere to the prescribed methods. Therefore, their slaughtered meat is not permissible today unless it is established regarding a specific meat that a Christian slaughtered it in the prescribed manner. So meat sold in their markets without knowledge of the slaughterer is not permissible. Meat imported from non-Muslim countries is not permissible to eat, even if it has a declaration that it was slaughtered according to Islamic method, because it has been established that these certifications are not reliable. The original ruling regarding meat is prohibition. Reference: 'Buhuth fi Qadaya Fiqhiyya Mu'asira' (pp. 443-444).
