Commentary
This passage states the fundamental Hanafi ruling that the slaughterer must be a Muslim or a Kitabi (Jew or Christian), whether a dhimmi (protected) or harbi (enemy). The text then includes a contemporary discussion about mechanical slaughter methods and the status of meat from non-Muslim countries. The commentator explains who qualifies as Ahl al-Kitab today: those who believe in God and His prophets and scriptures, even if they hold false beliefs. It clarifies that meat found in Muslim lands is presumed halal unless proven otherwise, based on the hadith of Aisha regarding Bedouin sacrifices. However, due to the current negligence of religious slaughter practices by many Christians, their meat is not considered halal unless it is certain that a Christian slaughtered it according to Islamic method. Imported meat from non-Muslim countries is prohibited even if labeled halal, because such certifications are unreliable. The original ruling of the Hanafi school is that the slaughterer's faith is a condition, and this traditional ruling is reinforced by contemporary fatwas that strictly limit what qualifies as halal.

Key Terms
الذابح (adh-dhabih): The person performing the slaughter
أهل الكتاب (ahl al-kitab): Jews and Christians who believe in their scriptures and fundamental beliefs
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Muslim protection
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim from enemy territory

Forgetting the Tasmiya at Slaughter: The Hanafi Excuse of Forgetfulness
Ruling with proof: forgetfulness excuses omission of tasmiya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on meat from an animal slaughtered with forgotten tasmiya, cite the Quranic proof from al-Baqarah 2:286, and respond to the Maliki counter-position.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness is an excuse: meat from an animal slaughtered without tasmiya due to forgetfulness is halal.

What happens if a Muslim slaughterer forgets to say 'Bismillah'? The Hanafi school, relying on the Quranic principle that forgetfulness is not held against us, rules the meat permissible. This lesson unpacks the proof and addresses the objection from Imam Malik who disagrees even in the case of forgetfulness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is slaughtering an animal. The default ruling is that tasmiya (mentioning Allah's name) is wajib (obligatory) at the time of slaughter. Deliberate omission makes the meat haram.
This is the baseline before considering exception.
2
State the exception for forgetfulness
The passage states that if the slaughterer forgets (nasiyan) to recite the tasmiya, the meat is halal. Forgetfulness exempts from the prohibition.
The key term is nisyān (unintentional omission).
3
Locate the Quranic proof
The proof is the supplication in Quran 2:286: 'Our Lord, do not impose blame upon us if we forget or make a mistake.' This indicates that Allah does not hold us accountable for forgetfulness.
The verse is a general principle applicable to all acts, including slaughter.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof proves the ruling)
The verse teaches that forgetfulness is not punishable. Therefore, the prohibition on eating meat without tasmiya (based on Quran 6:121) is lifted in cases of forgetfulness because the person is not blameworthy.
The Hanafi school interprets the prohibition in 6:121 as applying to deliberate omission or dedication to idols, not forgetfulness.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute and Hanafi response
Imam Malik holds that even forgetfulness does not make the meat permissible. The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) The verse about eating that over which Allah's name is not mentioned is restricted to meat dedicated to idols; (2) Forgetfulness is not counted among the prohibited categories.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن تركها ناسياً حَل لعذر النسيان، قال الله الله : رَبَّنَا لَا تُؤَاخِذْنَا إِن نَسِينَا أَوْ أَخْطَأنَا ) ، فقوله : «تسمية الله تعالى في قلب كل مسلم يحمل على حالة النسيان حجة ، وبين قوله : وأيضاً. قلنا : الأول : احتجاج بحمل قوله الله : وَلَا تَأْكُلُوا (۳) الآية على قوله جل : أو فِسْما أُهِلَّ لِغَيْرِ اللَّهِ بِهِ ) ، بناءً على قرينة قوله : وَإِنَّهُ لَفِسْقٌ ) (٤) والثاني : احتجاج بحمله عليه، بناءً على عدم عده من المحرمات (1). [١] قوله : نازلاً قبل قوله : ولا تأكلوا... الخ، فيه مناقشة وهي أنّ ظاهر هذا الكلام مخالف للحديث الذي نقله الزَّمَخْشَرِيُّ والبَيْضَاوي في تفسيرهما في آخر سورة الأنعام ، حيث قال : عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : نزلت على سورة الأنعام جملة واحدة) الحديث ؛ لأن نزولها جملة واحدة ينا في ظاهر كون نزول أحدهما قبل الأخرى، فليتأمل. كذا في الجلبي). وعند مالك (۱۱) : لا يحل في النسيان أيضاً.
If he abandons it forgetfully, it is permissible due to the excuse of forgetfulness. Allah said: 'Our Lord, do not impose blame upon us if we forget or make a mistake.' So his statement: 'The naming of Allah in the heart of every Muslim is a proof that it is carried upon the state of forgetfulness.' And his statement: 'And also.' We said: The first is an argument by carrying the verse 'Do not eat...' upon the statement of the Exalted: 'or what has been dedicated to other than Allah', based on the indication of His statement: 'and indeed it is disobedience.' And the second is an argument by carrying it upon him, based on the fact that it is not counted among the prohibitions. The hadith, because its revelation all at once contradicts the apparent meaning that one of them was revealed before the other, so ponder. Thus in al-Jalabi. Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the permissibility of eating meat from an animal slaughtered by one who forgot to recite the name of Allah. The Hanafi position is that the meat is halal due to the excuse of forgetfulness, based on the Quranic verse 'Our Lord, do not impose blame upon us if we forget or make a mistake' (2:286). The author addresses an objection: some argue that the verse 'Do not eat that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned' (6:121) is absolute. The Hanafi response interprets that verse as referring to meat dedicated to idols, supported by the phrase 'and indeed it is disobedience' (6:121). Another response argues that forgetfulness is not counted among the deliberate prohibitions. A further issue is raised about the order of revelation: the claim that 'Do not eat' was revealed later than the permission for forgetfulness contradicts the hadith that Surat al-An'am was revealed whole. The commentator advises caution on this point. Finally, it notes that Imam Malik holds the opposite view: the meat is not permissible even if the tasmiya was forgotten.

Key Terms
النسيان (al-nisyān): The unintentional omission of reciting the name of Allah at the time of slaughtering the animal.
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): The act of reciting 'Bismillah' (in the name of Allah) at the time of slaughter.
حل (ḥall): Permissible (halal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on an animal slaughtered by a person who forgot to recite the name of Allah?
A) The meat is halal because forgetfulness is an excuse.
B) The meat is haram because the condition of tasmiya is not fulfilled.
C) The meat is makruh tanzihan.
D) The meat is halal only if the person repents.

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness of the tasmiya at slaughter does not render the meat haram; the Hanafi school excuses it based on Quran 2:286.

Ruling on Mentioning Other Names During Slaughter: Wasl, Atf, and Fasl
Three rulings based on linguistic connection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three linguistic modes (wasl, atf, fasl) of mentioning another name with Allah's name during slaughter and state the corresponding Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
The Hanafi ruling hinges on grammatical connection: wasl = makruh, atf = haram, fasl = halal.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary unpacks the legal implications of invoking another entity alongside Allah during the slaughter of an animal. While the tasmiyah is required, adding other names is not automatically prohibited. The school parses the utterance into three categories based on linguistic structure, each carrying a different hukm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Locate the terms waslan, atfan, and fasl in the text. These represent the three ways an extra name can be mentioned.
2
Understand the linguistic distinction
Wasl: extra name directly appended without a conjunction (e.g., 'Bismillah Allahumma'). Atf: joined with a coordinating conjunction like 'wa' (e.g., 'Bismillah wa ismi Zaid'). Fasl: separated in form and meaning (e.g., saying 'Bismillah' first, then a separate invocation).
3
Apply the ruling to each
Match each mode to its ruling: wasl = makruh (disliked but animal halal), atf = haram (animal forbidden), fasl = permissible (no issue).
4
Note the grammatical nuance
Al-Zayla'i adds that if the extra name is in the nominative case (raf'), it is still only makruh; if in the genitive (khafd), it is not permissible. However, the preferred view is that grammatical inflection is not considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره أن يذكر مع اسم الله تعالى غيره وصلاً لا عطفاً، كقوله: بسم الله اللهم تقبل من فلان. وحرم الذبيحة إن عطف نحو: بسم الله واسم فلان، أو: بسم الله واسم فلان. فإن فصل صورة ومعنى فلا بأس به. قاله الجلبي: وهو على ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: مكروه. الثاني: حرام. الثالث: لا بأس به. أشار المصنف إلى الأول بقوله: وصلاً لا عطفاً، وإلى الثاني بقوله: وحرم الذبيحة إن عطف، وإلى الثالث بقوله: فإن فصل صورة ومعنى. قوله: بسم الله اللهم تقبل من فلان... قال الزيلعي: أو يقول: بسم الله محمد رسول الله بالرفع، فيكره ولا يحرم الذبيحة، وإن قال بالخفض لا يحل. وقال بعضهم: هذا إذا كان يعرف النحو، والأوجه أن لا يعتبر الإعراب.
And it is disliked to mention with the name of Allah something else, in a connected manner (waslan) not with a conjunction (atfan), such as his saying: 'In the name of Allah, O Allah, accept from so-and-so.' And the slaughtered animal is forbidden if he uses a conjunction, such as: 'In the name of Allah and the name of so-and-so', or 'In the name of Allah and the name of so-and-so'. And if he separates in form and meaning, then there is no harm. Al-Jalabi said: It is of three types: the first: disliked; the second: forbidden; the third: no harm. The author indicated the first by his saying: 'connected not with a conjunction', and the second by his saying: 'and the slaughtered animal is forbidden if he uses a conjunction', and the third by his saying: 'for if he separates in form and meaning'. His saying: 'In the name of Allah, O Allah, accept from so-and-so'... al-Zayla'i said: Or if he says: 'In the name of Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' in the nominative case (raf'), then it is disliked and the slaughtered animal is not forbidden, but if he says it in the genitive case (khafd), it is not permissible. And some said: This is if he knows grammar, but the preferred view is that inflection is not considered.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on mentioning another name together with the name of Allah during slaughter. It distinguishes three scenarios based on how the other name is connected linguistically. (1) If the other name is connected directly in pronunciation without a coordinating conjunction (waslan), such as saying 'Bismillah Allahumma taqabbal min fulaan', the act is makruh (disliked) but the animal remains halal. (2) If the other name is joined with a coordinating conjunction like 'wa' (atfan), e.g., 'Bismillah wa ismi fulaan', the animal becomes haram (forbidden) to eat. (3) If the two names are separated both in form and meaning, such as making two separate utterances, there is no problem and it is permissible. The commentator al-Jalabi summarizes these three as: makruh, haram, and la ba'sa. The author signalled the first case by 'waslan la atfan', the second by 'harrama al-dhabihah in ataaf', and the third by 'fa-in fasala suratan wa ma'nan'. A further grammatical point is added by al-Zayla'i: if one says 'Bismillah Muhammadun Rasulullah' with the noun 'Muhammadun' in the nominative (raf'), it is makruh but not haram, whereas if it is in the genitive (khafd) as 'Muhammadin Rasulillah', it is not permissible. Some scholars conditioned this ruling on the speaker's knowledge of grammar, but the preferred view is not to consider grammatical inflection.

Key Terms
وصلاً (waslan): Connecting the extra name in pronunciation without a coordinating conjunction, e.g., 'Bismillah Allahumma'; this results in karahah (dislike).
عطفاً (atfan): Using a coordinating conjunction like 'waw' between Allah's name and another name, e.g., 'Bismillah wa ismi Zaid'; this renders the animal haram.
فصل (fasl): Separating the two names in both form and meaning, e.g., saying 'Bismillah' and then separately invoking another name; this is permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person slaughtering an animal says 'Bismillah wa ismi Muhammad', what is the ruling on the slaughtered animal according to the Hanafi school?
A) The animal is halal because the name of Allah was mentioned.
B) The animal is haram (forbidden) because of the conjunctive 'wa' joining another name.
C) The animal is makruh tanzihan.
D) The animal is permissible only if the person intended the name of Allah alone.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of meat from slaughter depends on how the additional name is linguistically connected: wasl (direct) = makruh, atf (conjunction) = haram, fasl (separate) = halal.

Preferred Method of Slaughter: Nahr for Camels, Dhabh for Others
Hanafi ruling on sunnah slaughter method with Maliki opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the recommended slaughter methods for camels, cattle, and sheep, explain the anatomical and Sunnah-based reasoning, and distinguish between the Hanafi and Maliki positions regarding the permissibility of meat.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, slaughtering a camel by dhabh is only disliked (makruh tanzih), not forbidden; the meat remains halal.

The act of slaughtering an animal for food must follow the Sunnah and be efficient. This lesson examines the Hanafi school's ruling that nahr (cutting at the base of the neck) is recommended for camels, while dhabh (throat cut) is recommended for cattle and sheep, and that the opposite method is merely makruh tanzih (slightly disliked) rather than prohibitive. We will also contrast the Hanafi position with the stricter Maliki view, which holds that using the wrong method renders the meat unlawful.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling for each animal
Identify the recommended (mustahab) method for camels (nahr) and for cattle/sheep (dhabh). Note that the opposite method is makruh tanzih.
The ruling is based on both following the Sunnah and anatomical ease.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof
The passage cites two reasons: following the Sunnah, and the gathering of major veins at the appropriate cut location (lower neck for camels, upper neck for others). This makes the recommended method more efficient.
3
State the Maliki position
According to Malik, slaughtering a camel by dhabh or cattle/sheep by nahr invalidates the slaughter and the meat is not halal.
The Maliki view is stricter, treating the method as a condition of validity.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the karahah is only tanzih (slight dislike) because the essential requirements of slaughter (cutting the required veins, tasmiyah, etc.) are still fulfilled. There is no element of shirk or partnership, so the meat remains halal.
The commentator al-Jalabi emphasizes that dislike does not prevent permissibility.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi, meat from the opposite method is halal (though makruh), while in Maliki it is not halal. This impacts food choices and fatwa in mixed-madhhab contexts.
Know which ruling applies in your community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحُببَ نَحْرُ الإبل وكُرِهَ ذَبْحُها ، وفي البقر والغنم عكسه، هذا عندنا، وعند مالك له إن ذبح الإبل أو نحر البقر والغنم لا يحلّ. الكراهة : وجود الوصل صورة، والقران ظاهراً، ووجه الحل ؛ فقدان معنى الشركة ، قاله الجلبي (٢) [1] قوله : وحبب نحر الإبل...الخ، وهو قطع العروق عند الصدر، والذبح قطع العروق في أعلى العنق تحت اللحيين وجه الاستحباب في الكل موافقه السنة واجتماع العروق في الإبل في أسفل العنق عند الصدر، وفيهما في المذبح، ووجه الكراهة في الكل مخالفة السنة، وهي بمعنى في غيره ، فلا يمنع الجواز والحل(۳). وفي «الكافي»: إنّ موضع النحر من الإبل باللحم عليه وما سوى ذلك . عليه لحم غليظ وعروقها مجتمعة في المنحر، فأما في البقر والغنم فالذبح أيسر لاجتماع العروق في المذبح.
And it is recommended to slaughter camels by nahr, and it is disliked to slaughter them by dhabh. For cattle and sheep, the opposite. This is our position. According to Malik, if one slaughters camels by dhabh or slaughters cattle and sheep by nahr, it is not halal. Dislike: the presence of joining in form and conjunction apparently, and the aspect of permissibility is the absence of the meaning of partnership, said by al-Jalabi. [1] His saying: 'and it is recommended to slaughter camels by nahr...' etc., and it is cutting the veins at the chest, and dhabh is cutting the veins at the top of the neck under the jaw. The aspect of recommendation in all is following the Sunnah and the gathering of the veins in camels at the lower part of the neck at the chest, and in both at the slaughter locus. The aspect of dislike in all is opposing the Sunnah, and it is in the sense that it does not prevent permissibility and halalness. And in al-Kafi: The place of nahr of camels has flesh on it, and besides that... it has thick flesh and its veins are gathered at the manhar (slaughter place). As for cattle and sheep, dhabh is easier because the veins are gathered at the madhbah (slaughter place).
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the preferred method of slaughtering different animals. For camels, the recommended (mustahab) method is nahr—cutting the large artery at the base of the neck near the chest—while slaughtering by dhabh (cutting the throat at the upper neck) is considered makruh tanzih (slightly disliked). For cattle and sheep, the opposite applies: dhabh is mustahab and nahr is makruh tanzih. The reason is anatomical: in camels, the major blood vessels converge at the lower neck, making nahr more efficient and in accordance with the Sunnah; in cattle and sheep, they converge at the upper neck, so dhabh is easier and sunnah. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the disliked method does not render the meat unlawful (haram); it is merely less preferable. In contrast, the Maliki school holds that slaughtering an animal using the method prescribed for a different species invalidates the slaughter and the meat is not halal. The commentary also clarifies that the term 'karahah' (dislike) here refers to a slight detestability due to deviation from the optimal practice, not to prohibition.

Key Terms
نحر (nahr): Cutting the large artery at the base of the neck near the chest, specifically for camels.
ذبح (dhabh): Cutting the throat at the upper neck below the jaw, the standard method for cattle and sheep.
كراهة (karahah): Dislike that does not reach the level of prohibition; it is tanzih (slight dislike).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on slaughtering a sheep by nahr (cutting at the base of the neck)?
A) Haram
B) Makruh Tahrimi
C) Makruh Tanzih but meat halal
D) Sunnah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, using the non-preferred method of slaughter (e.g., dhabh for a camel) renders the slaughter makruh tanzih, but the meat remains halal and permissible to eat.

Ruling on Slaughtering a Domestic Animal that Becomes Wild or Falls into a Well
Hanafi permissibility of emergency slaughter for wild or trapped animals with Maliki opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emergency slaughter for a domestic animal that becomes wild or falls into a well, and explain the key points of disagreement with the Maliki school.

Core Idea
If a tame animal becomes wild or falls into a well, Hanafi allows emergency slaughter; necessity overrides rarity.

The passage addresses a practical scenario: an owned animal escapes and cannot be caught, or falls into a well and cannot be slaughtered normally. Can its meat be eaten if it dies from a wound? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on inability, while the Maliki school disagrees, arguing such cases are rare and do not justify altering the rule. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that if a domestic animal becomes wild or falls into a well and cannot be slaughtered in the usual way, any fatal wound renders it halal due to necessity (dhakat al-idtirar).
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
The Maliki school holds that only proper optional slaughter (dhakat al-ikhtiyariyya) can render the animal halal; emergency slaughter is not accepted.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Maliki argument is that such cases are rare, and the law does not base rulings on rare exceptions (la 'ibrah lil-nadir).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that inability is the genuine legal consideration; rarity does not negate the rule because the reality of inability is established and outweighs the rarity of the situation.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, under Hanafi, the meat is halal; under Maliki, it is haram unless killed by proper slaughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولَزِمَ ذبح صيد استأنس ، وكفى جرحُ نَعَمِ تَوَحْش ، أو سَقَطَ في بئر ولم يُمْكِنُ ذبحه. ومعنى توحش : صار وحشيّاً بعدما كان أنيساً، وقيل : النعم : كلُّ حيوان أنسي كالدجاجةِ والحمامة والإبل والبقر والغنم وغير ذلك. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية». [1] قوله : هذا عندنا...الخ ؛ لأنا نقول : المعتبر حقيقة العجز، وقد تحقق، فيصار إلى البدل : أي ذكاة الاضطرار، ولا نُسَلّم للندرة، بل هو طالب ، وقال مالك : لا يحل إلا بالذكاة الاختيارية لا بذكاة الاضطرارية في الوجهين ؛ لأنّ ذلك نادر، ولا عبرة للنادر في الأحكام.
And it is necessary to slaughter a game animal that has become tame, and the wounding of livestock that has become wild is sufficient, or it fell into a well and cannot be slaughtered. The meaning of tawahhush: it became wild after it was tame. It is said: an'am (livestock) includes every domestic animal like chicken, pigeon, camels, cattle, sheep, etc. So in al-Fawa'id al-Arifiyya. [1] His saying: 'This is our position...' etc.; because we say: what is considered is the reality of inability, and it has occurred, so one resorts to the alternative, which is emergency slaughter. And we do not concede that it is rare; rather it is sought. Malik said: it is not halal except by optional slaughter, not by emergency slaughter in both cases; because that is rare, and no consideration is given to rare occurrences in rulings.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage discusses the situation of a domesticated animal that becomes wild (متوحش) or falls into a well and cannot be slaughtered in the normal manner. The Hanafi ruling is that if such an animal is wounded (e.g., by a bullet or any fatal injury) and the wound leads to its death, the animal becomes halal due to necessity (ذكاة الاضطرار). The key is genuine inability to perform the standard slaughter. The Hanafi school argues that the reality of inability is established, so one may resort to the alternative method. The Maliki school disagrees, holding that only proper elective slaughter (ذكاة اختيارية) renders the animal halal, regardless of difficulty, because such cases are rare and the law does not consider rare exceptions. The Hanafi response is that inability is a valid excuse, and necessity is not negated by rarity.

Key Terms
توحش (tawahhush): The state of a domestic animal becoming wild after being tame.
نعم (na'am): Livestock, including camels, cattle, sheep, and other domestic animals.
ذكاة الاضطرار (dhakat al-idtirar): Emergency slaughter, where a fatal wound from any source suffices due to inability to perform standard slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because it is an emergency
B) No, because only proper slaughter suffices
C) Yes, but only if it was wild to begin with
D) No, because it is rare

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, genuine inability to slaughter a domestic animal allows its fatal wound to count as emergency slaughter, making the meat halal, despite Maliki objections based on rarity.

Fetus Found Dead in Slaughtered Animal: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fetus halal status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) and the opposing view of the Sahibayn and Shafi'i on the permissibility of a fetus found dead in a slaughtered animal, and identify the dalil and thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A fetus is not made halal by the mother's slaughter — Abu Hanifa requires its own slaughter.

In this passage, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a fetus found dead in the womb of a slaughtered animal. The core question: does the mother's slaughter render the fetus permissible for consumption? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that it does not, while his two main students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with Imam al-Shafi'i, argue that if the fetus is fully formed, it is halal. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the hadith evidence, and the practical consequence in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the legal question: whether a dead fetus found in a slaughtered animal is halal. Note the two opposing positions.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its proponents
Imam Abu Hanifa, along with Zufar and al-Hasan, holds that the fetus is not halal by the mother's slaughter; it requires its own.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Identify the opposing position and its proponents
Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that if the fetus is fully formed, it is halal because the mother's slaughter suffices.
They cite the hadith 'ذكاة الجنين ذكاة أمه'.
4
Analyse the dalil
Examine the hadith and the incident with a Companion. The Sahibayn use it as evidence; understand the Hanafi response that the fetus does not become slaughtered by the mother's slaughter.
The Hanafi interpretation requires a direct act of slaughter for each living being.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Imam Abu Hanifa: the fetus is not halal.
This is the operative ruling despite the conflicting view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يَحِلُّ جنين ميت وُجِدَ في بطن أمه، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما وعند الشَّافِعِي له إذا تَمَّ خَلْقُه أَكل ، وذكاة الأم ذكاة له. [٢] قوله : ولا يحل جنين ميت...الخ ؛ في تكملة البحر») : يعني لا يصير الجنين مذكّى بذكاة أمه حتى لا يحل بذكاتها، وهذا عند الإمام وزفر والحسن . وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد له وجماعة أخر : إذا تم خلقُهُ حلَّ أكله بذكاتِها ؛ لقوله : «ذكاة الجنين ذكاة أمه» (٦) ، ولقوله الله لما قيل له : إننا ذبح الناقة ونذبح الشاة وفي بطنها الجنين، أنلقيه أم نأكله ، قال : «كله إن شئت فإن ذكاته ذكاة أمه ) ؛ ولأنه
And it is not halal a dead fetus found in the belly of its mother. This is the position of Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafi'i, if its creation is complete, it is eaten, and the slaughter of the mother is slaughter for it. [2] His saying: 'And it is not halal a dead fetus...' etc.; in Tukmilat al-Bahr: meaning the fetus does not become slaughtered by the slaughter of its mother, so it is not halal by its slaughter. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa, Zufar, and al-Hasan. Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and a group of others said: if its creation is complete, its eating is halal by its mother's slaughter, due to his saying: 'The slaughter of the fetus is the slaughter of its mother' and due to his saying when it was said to him: 'We slaughter the camel and we slaughter the sheep and in its belly is a fetus, should we throw it or eat it?' He said: 'Eat it if you wish, for its slaughter is the slaughter of its mother' and because...
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage addresses the juridical status of a fetus found dead inside its mother after she has been slaughtered. According to Imam Abu Hanifa (and Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad), the fetus is not made halal by the mother's slaughter; its meat is not permissible. The basis is that the fetus is a separate entity and requires its own independent slaughter. However, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that if the fetus is fully formed (complete creation), it is halal to eat because the mother's slaughter suffices as its slaughter. They support this with the hadith: 'The slaughter of the fetus is the slaughter of its mother' (narrated by Abu Dawud) and the practice of the Companions. The practical consequence: in Hanafi fatwa, the relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa (the fetus is not halal), while the view of the Sahibayn is also considered but not the operative in the school.

Key Terms
جنين (janin): Unborn offspring in the womb of an animal.
ذكاة الأم ذكاة له (dhakat al-um dhakat lahu): The slaughter of the mother is considered as slaughter for the fetus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The fetus is halal because the mother's slaughter suffices
B) The fetus is not halal and must be discarded
C) The fetus is halal only if it was alive before slaughter
D) The fetus is halal for the Sahibayn but not for Abu Hanifa

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position is that the mother's slaughter does not suffice for the fetus; the fetus requires its own slaughter.

The General Prohibition of Predatory Animals and Birds
Commentary on the prohibition and definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on consuming animals with fangs and birds with talons, cite the hadith evidence, and define the key terms dhu nāb, dhu mikhlab, sabu', and hashara.

Core Idea
Predatory animals with fangs and birds with talons are haram based on a clear hadith; the prohibition is based on habitual predation, not merely anatomy.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains the foundational ruling on which animals and birds are prohibited for consumption in the Hanafi school. The commentator begins by citing the base text's prohibition and then provides the prophetic hadith that serves as the primary evidence. He also clarifies the definitions of key terms to avoid misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited categories
The matn states that any animal with a fang (dhu nāb) from predatory beasts (sabu') and any bird with a talon (dhu mikhlab) from predatory birds are prohibited. Note that the prohibition extends to all predatory birds and beasts, not just those with fangs/talons.
The phrase 'wa kadhā sibā' al-tayr wa al-bahā'im' indicates the prohibition covers all predatory birds and beasts.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites the hadith: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade eating any animal with a fang from the beasts of prey and any bird with a talon.'
This hadith is the primary evidence for the ruling.
3
Understand the definition of sabu'
The commentator defines sabu' as any animal that habitually snatches, plunders, wounds, and kills. This is a behavioral definition, not purely anatomical.
The actions of birds (ikhtilaf) and beasts (intihab) are distinguished.
4
Distinguish between birds and beasts
The act of seizing (ikhtilaf) is typical of birds, while plundering (intihab) is typical of beasts. Both are included in the prohibition.
5
Note the ruling on hasharaat
The matn also prohibits hasharaat (small crawling creatures). The commentator lists examples (ants, mice, geckos, etc.) and explains they are prohibited because they are not slaughtered and are considered filthy.
Hasharaat are independently prohibited; they are not considered eligible for slaughter.

ولا ذو ناب أو مخلب من سَبع أو طير، ولا الحشرات (١) ، والحمر الأهلية، والبغل جزء من أمه حقيقة ؛ لكونه متصلاً بها وحكماً حتى يدخل في الأحكام الواردة على الأم من البيع والهبة والعتق.
وللإمام قوله : إن الله حرم الميتة، وهو اسم الحيوان مات من غير ذكاة ، والجنين مات حتف أنفه، فيحرم بالكتاب، ويكره ذبح الشاة إذا تقارب ولادتها ؛ لأنه يضيعُ ما في بطنها. الدجاجة إذا تعلقت فرماها وأصابها ، ينظر إن كان لا يهتدي إلى منزله حل أكله ؛
لأنه عجز عن الذكاة الاختيارية، وإن كان يهتدي ذكر الفقيه أبو الليث : إن أصابَ
المذبح حلّ، وإن أصاب غيره فعند محمد الله لا يحلّ، وعن غيره : يحلّ. [١] قوله : ولا ذو ناب...الخ ؛ هذا بيان فيما يحلّ وما لا يحل، اعلم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن أكل ذي ناب من السباع، وعن أكل ذي مخلب من الطيور، وكذا سباع الطير والبهائم لا كل ما له مخلب أو ناب.

هو حرام عندنا، وكذا مارماهي. والانتهاب : غارت کردن والاختطاف ربودن، فالأول ينسب إلى كلاغ يشبه. اعلم أن الغراب على أربعة أنواع : نوع تأكل الحبوب فقط، فهو حلال اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه ليس من سباع الطير، ولا تأكل

[١) قوله : والخيل ...الخ ؛ أي ولا يحلّ الخيل، والمفهوم من «المجمع»: الحرمة، ومن «الهداية»: كراهة تحريم، قيل في الفرق بين الحرام والمكروه التحريمي : إن فاعل الأوّل
And not (any animal) with a fang or a claw from a beast of prey or a bird, nor insects, nor domestic donkeys, and the mule is part of its mother in reality, due to its connection to her, and legally, so that it enters into the rulings that apply to the mother regarding sale, gift, and manumission. And for the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is his statement: Indeed Allah has forbidden carrion, and it is the animal that died without slaughter, and the fetus died of its own accord, so it is forbidden by the Book. And it is disliked to slaughter a sheep when its birth is near, because it wastes what is in its belly. If a chicken is shot with an arrow and hits it, if it cannot reach its home, it is permissible to eat it, because it is unable to perform optional slaughter; but if it can reach its home, al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned: if the arrow hits the throat, it is permissible; if it hits elsewhere, according to Muhammad (al-Shaybani) it is not permissible, and according to others it is permissible. [1] His statement: 'And not (any animal) with a fang...' etc.; this is an explanation of what is permissible and what is not. Know that the Prophet, peace be upon him, forbade eating any animal with a fang from the beasts of prey, and eating any bird with a talon, and similarly predatory birds and animals, not everything that has a talon or fang. And what is meant by sabu' is every animal that habitually snatches, plunders, wounds, and kills. Then the act of seizing is from the actions of birds, and plundering is from the actions of beasts. The talon for a bird is like the nail for a human, and the fang in Persian is: 'jahār dandān-i shatr' (four teeth like a camel). And the hasharaat are the small crawling creatures of the earth, plural of hashara, like the ant, the mouse, the gecko, the lizard, the snake, the frog. And al-jarith: with a fatha on the jim, and a tashdid on the ra, and ending with a tha: a type of fish, it is haram according to us, and similarly mar-mahi. And al-intihab is plundering, and al-ikhtitaf is snatching; the first is attributed to a crow-like (bird). Know that the crow is of four types: a type that eats only grains, it is halal by consensus because it is not among the predatory birds, and it does not eat... And a type that eats only carrion, it is haram by consensus. And a type that is counted among the predatory birds, it is also haram by consensus. And a type that combines grains and carrion, it is halal according to the greatest (imam), it is called al-'aq'uq, and in Persian: 'akah'; and it is said it is makruh, and it is a bird with a long tail, having black and white, it is said: the crow of the field called al-zaytun, and it is a small bird of Jannah, red-legged, black-bodied, does not eat except grains. [1] His statement: 'And the horse...' etc.; meaning the horse is not permissible. And what is understood from 'al-Majma'' is prohibition, and from 'al-Hidayah' is karahah tahrimi. It is said regarding the difference between haram and makruh tahrimi: that the doer of the former...

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn (base text) of al-Wiqayah, specifically on the section regarding permissible and impermissible animals for consumption. The commentator begins by citing the base text's prohibition of animals with fangs or claws from beasts of prey or birds, as well as insects and domestic donkeys. He then explains the ruling on the mule, which is legally considered part of its mother, thus subject to the same rulings regarding sale, gift, and manumission. Following this, a separate ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa is mentioned: the prohibition of carrion (maytah) based on the Qur'an, and the dislike (karahah) of slaughtering a ewe near parturition because it wastes the fetus. There is also a discussion about a chicken shot with an arrow: if it cannot be reached for regular slaughter, it is halal; if it can be reached, there is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school—Muhammad al-Shaybani says it is not halal if the arrow hits elsewhere than the throat, while others say it is halal. The commentary then returns to the main prohibition, citing the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbidding the consumption of any animal with a fang from predatory beasts or any bird with a talon. The commentator defines 'sabu'' as any animal that habitually snatches, plunders, injures, and kills, and distinguishes between the actions of birds (ikhtilaf) and beasts (intihab). He then explains the terms 'hasharaat' (small crawling creatures) and provides examples that are haram. The passage also discusses the 'jarith' fish and 'mar-mahi' as haram according to the Hanafi school. Finally, the commentary addresses the different types of crows, dividing them into four categories based on diet: those that eat only grains (halal by consensus), those that eat only carrion (haram by consensus), those that are predatory (haram by consensus), and those that eat both grains and carrion (halal according to Abu Hanifa, though some say makruh). The passage concludes with the start of a discussion on horse meat, stating that it is not permissible, with a difference between 'al-Majma'' (which says haram) and 'al-Hidayah' (which says makruh tahrimi), but the text cuts off before completing the distinction.

Key Terms
ذو ناب (dhu nāb): An animal possessing a fang, i.e., a canine tooth used for predation.
ذو مخلب (dhu mikhlab): A bird possessing a talon, used for seizing prey.
السبع (al-sabu'): Any predatory animal that habitually snatches, plunders, wounds, and kills.
الحشرات (al-hasharāt): Small crawling creatures of the earth, such as ants, mice, geckos, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, what is the basis for prohibiting a bird?
A) It has a talon.
B) It is a predator that uses its talon to seize prey.
C) It eats carrion.
D) It has a hooked beak.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits predatory animals and birds based on the Prophet's hadith, defining predation by habitual behavior, not just anatomy.
